• • * «
Ifowrfay.—This new creature with the long hair is a
good deal in the way. It is always hanging around and
follow,ng me about. I don't like this ; I an, not used to
eompany. I wish it would stay with the other animals.
. • . Gloudy to-day, wind in the east; think we shall have
ram. ... We? Where did I get that word?. .. I
remember now—the new creature uses it.
Tuasdmy— Been looking over the estate. The new
creature calls it the * Garden of Eden ’—why, I am sure I
do not know. Says it looks like the Gard n of Eden,
That is not a reason, it is mere waywardness and imbe-
«hty. I get no chance to name anything myself. The
new creature names everything that comes along, before
I can get in a protest.
Wednesday.— Built me a shelter against the rain, but
couia not have it to myself in peace. The new creature
obtruded. When I tried to put it out it shed water out
of the holes in the front of its head that it looks with, and
wiped it away with the back of its paw, and made a
piteous noise such as some of the other animals make
when they are in distress.
Saturday.— The new creature eats too muoh fruit. We
are going to run short, most likely. •We ’ again—that is
its word; mine, too, now, from hearing it so much.
Good deal of fog this morning. Ido not go out in the
fog, myself. The new creature does. It goes out in all
weathers, and stumps right in with its muddy feet. And
talks. It used to be so pleasant and quiet here.
Sunday. -Pulled through. This day is getting to be
more and more trying. It was selected and set apart
last November as a day of rest. I already had six of
them per week before. This is another of those unac-
countable things. There seems to be too much legisla-
tion, too much fussing, and fixing, and tidying-up, and
not enough of the better-let-well-enough-alone policy.
[lfem. —Must keep that sort of opinion to myself.) This
morning found the new creature trying to clod apples
out of that forbidden tree. But it can’t hit anything—it
throws like—that. I think the apples are safe.
* * » »
Monday.— The new creature says its name is Eve. That
is all right, I have no objections. Says it is to call it by
when I want it to come. I said it was superfluous, then.
The word evidently raised me in its respect; and indeed
it is a large, good word and will bear repetition. It says
it is not an It, it is a She. This is probably doubtful; yet
it is all one to me; what she is were nothing to me if she
would but go by herself and keep quiet.
Sunday.—Pulled through.
Monday.— I believe I see what the week is for: it is !•
give time to rest up from the weariness of Sunday. It
Seems a good idea, in a region where good ideas are rather
conspicuously scarce. [Jfem.-Must keep this sort of
remarks private.] . . . She has been climbing that tree
again. Clodded her out of it. She said nobody was
looking. Seems to consider that a sufficient justification
for chancing any dangerous thing. Told her that. The
word justification moved her admiration—and envy, too,
I thought. It is a good word.
Thursday.— She told me she was made out of a rib
taken from my body. This is at least doubtful, if not
more than that. I have not missed any rib. . . . She is
in much trouble about the buzzard ; says grass does not
agree with it; is afraid she can’t raise it; thinks it was
intended to live on decayed flesh. The buzzard must get
along the best it can with what is provided. We cannot
overturn the whole scheme to accommodate the buzzard.
Sunday. —Pulled through.
Tuesday. —She has taken up with a snake, now. The
Other animals are glad, for she was always experimenting
with them and bothering them ; and I am glad, because
the snake talks ; and this enables me to get a rest.
Friday.— She says the snake advises her to try the fruit
of that tree, and says the result will be a great and fine
and noble education. I advised her to keep away from
the tree. She said she wouldn’t. I foresee trouble.
Will emigrate.
* * * *
Wednesday. —l have had a variegated time. I escaped
that night, and rnde a horse all night as fast as he could
go, hoping to get clear out of the Garden and hide in some
other country before the trouble should begin ; but it was
not to be. About an hour after sun-up, as I was riding
through a flowery plain where thousands of animals were
grazing, slumbering, or playing with each other, accord-
ing to their common wont, all of a sudden they broke into
a tempest of frightful noises, and in one moment the
plain was a frantic commotion as far as the eye could
reach, and every beast was destroying its neighbor. I
knew what it meant—Eve had eaten that fruit, and death
was come into the world. . . . The tigers ate my horse,
paying uo attention when I ordered them to desist, and
they would even have eaten me if I had stayed—which I
didn't. ... I found this place, outside the Garden,
and was fairly comfortable for a few days, but she has
found me out. ... I find she is a good deal of a com-
panion. I see I should be lonesome and depressed with-
out her, now that I have lost tny property. Another
thing ;,she says it is ordered that we work for our living
hereafter, bhe will be useful. I will superintend.
Ten days later.—She aoouses me of being partly the
cause of our disaster 1 I like that I
* * • •
Next year.—We have named it Cain. She oaueht it
while I was up country trapping ; caught it in the timber
a couple of miles from our dug-out—or it might have
been four, she isn’t certain which. It resembles us in
some ways, and may' be a relation. That is what she
thinks, but this is an error, in my judgment. The differ-
ence in size warrants the conclusion that it is a different
and new kind of animal—a fist), perhaps, though when I
dumped it in the water to see, it sank, and she plunged
in and snatched it out before there was a chance for the
experiment to determine the ma ter.
uunday. fjhe don’t work, Sundays, but lies around all
tired out with her week’s digging and hoeing, and likes
to have the fish wallow over her; and she makes fool
noises to amuse it, and pretends to chew its paws, and
that makes it laugh. I’ve never seen a fish before that
could laugh. This makes me doubt . . . I have come
to like Sunday myself. Superintending all the week tires
a body so. There ought to be more Sundays. In the old
days they were tough, but now they come handy.
H ednesday. It isn’t a fish. I cannot quite make out
what it is. It makes curious devilish noises when not
satisfied ; and when it is, says "goo-goo.” I said I be-
lieved it was an enigma, but she only admired the word
without understanding it. In my judgment it is either
an enigma or some kind of a bug. If it dies, I will
take it apart and examine its works. I never had a
thing perplex me so.
* • • *
Thtee months later. —ft must- have been a young one
when it came, for it has grown exceedingly since. It
must he five times as big now as it was then, and when
discontented is able to make from twenty-two to thirty-
eight times the noise it made at first. She reconciles it by
persuasion, and hy giving it things which she had told it
she wouldn’t give it before. As observed previously, I was
not at home when it first came, and she told me she found
it in the woods. It seems odd that it should be the only
one, yet it must be so, for I have worn myself out these
many weeks trying to find another one to add to my col-
lection, and for this one to play with ; for surelv then it
would be quieter and we could tame it more easily.
Eight months later. —l examined its mouth. There is
no danger yet; it has only one tooth. It hasn’t any tail
yet. It makes more noise now than it ever did before—
and mainly at night. I have moved out But I shall go
over, mornings, to breakfast, and to see if it has more
teeth. If it gets a mouthful of teeth it will be time for
it to go, tail or no tail; for a bear does not need a tail in
order to be dangerous.
Four months later. —I have been off hunting and fishing
a month. Meantime the bear has learned to paddle
around all by itself on its hind legs, and says ‘poppa’
and ‘momma.’ It is certainly a new species. I will go
off on a far expedition among the forests of the north and
make an exhaustive search. There must certainly be
another one somewhere, and this one will be less danger-
ous when it has company of its own species.
Three months later. —lt has been a weary hunt, yet I
have had no success. In the meantime, without stirring
from the home estate, she has caught another one! I
never saw such luck. I might have hunted these woods a
hundred years, I never would have run across that thing.
Three months later. —l have been comparing the new
one with the old one, and it is perfectly plain that they
are the same breed. She calls the new one Abel. I was
going to stuff one of them for my collection, but she is
prejudiced against it for gome reason or other. The new
one is as ugly now as the old one was at first; has the
same sulphur-and-raw-meat complexion, and the same
singular head without any fur on it.
* * • «
Ten years later.— They are boys ; we found it out long
ago. It was their coming in that small, immature shape
that fooled us ;we were not used to it. There are some
girls now. Abel is a good boy, but if Cain had stayed a
bear it would have improved him. After all these years,
I see that I was mistiken about Eve in the beginning; it
is better to live outside the Garden with her than inside
it without her.
• • • «
Mark has been more spontaneous : the base-
ideas here are trite and conventional : but
there is one fine conception—Adam’s hunt for
another Cain. I’o display that red-earthy person
in the skins, with the club —a hunter familiar with
all that rau or swam or flew—posed by tins new
animal, the first Batiy, a monster unthinkable,
unheard-of; arguing in his slow way that every-
thing else went in batches, and so must this •
and taking his club into the woods to discover
the rest of the tribe this is to associate ideas
which only jump together for one man in a
million.
* * * »
‘ Mark Twain ’ and ‘ Bret Harte ’ are the two
American humorists of greatest note. The Ameri-
cans are living still ; the humorists are dead
Naturally enough. _ You can ‘knock out ’ a brain-
cell thousands of times, and when the sponge of
Sleep is passed over it up it comes fresh°and
smiling. But look closely, and you can see the
mark of the blow. By-and-by the response
is less speedy, and at last there comes a
day when the call of ‘Time!’ finds the
brain motionless, exhausted; and writer, or
artist, or scientist bitterly yields victory to
the Adversary with the scythe. Yet so few
recognise that there is no force like Youth ; that
the head grows grey within as well as without-
that as the joints stiffen, and the muscles shrivel'
and the face wrinkles, and the eye fades, so is the
brain stiffening, and shrivelling, and wrinkling,
and fading. As Age creeps on, it grows harder
to concentrate energy, harder to recover from
fatigue. Old impressions dominate fresh facts.
Accumulation tails to issue in creation. From
one point of view—the broadest view—it is always
too late to learn.
Mixed Paragraphs .
Acurrent question is whether the book of 'The Sign
of the Cross ’ is cruder, duller, and uglier than the play ;
or vice versa.
S.F, Ar onaut stresses one great objection to the lino-
type—the ugly monotony of its type-faces, ‘round, ex-
pressionless, heavy; suited, perhaps, for the slovenly
work of daily journalism, but painful to the eyes of those
used to chaste typography.’
The photographs included in Gordon and Gotch’s
‘Glimpspsof Australia’ are very well varied; as a rule,
very well executed ; and only moderately well repro-
duced. The photo.-engraver in Australia still, for the
most part, relies on his acids only ; in America and
Europe he has called the wood-engraver to his aid to
heighten his whites and deepen his contrasts and get rid
of the flatness so often seen in reproduced photos. Aus-
tralian illustrative art is way ahead of Australian metal-
engravers—and printers.
Flong: * A Gordon story. Many years ago, when on
the Diamantina River, W.Q., I was one day reading
“Gordon” to the two white stockmen on the run, and
Jack Baker, a half caste from the Adelaide side. None of
the three had heard any of Gordon’s poetry before. As
I closed the book I remarked that the author was reckoned
one of the best riders over fences ever seen in Australia.
No fear, said Jack, “the best rider ever I seed was a
bloke that teached me and a few more boys to ride. He
could ride, and no mistake ; you ought to have seen
him taking a horse up to a jump.” Then Jack for half-
an-hour told the good qualities of “the bloke who
teached me to ride.” Till then Gordon’s name had not
been mentioned, but as I was putting the poems on the
shelf I said: “Well, Jack, your boss might have been a
good rider, but Gordon was admitted by everyone to "
What! ’ said Jack, “what did you call him?” “Gor-
don—Lindsay Gordon.” “Why, that’s the very bloke I
was talking about," said Jack. He took the book from
the shelf, I ot ed at it, turned over a few leaves, and said :
“Did Mr. Gordon write all this book?” “Yes, cer-
tainly.” How his eyesdid shine 1 If Gordon ever had a
genuine admirer it is Jack Baker, half-caste, a really
splendid rider and one of the whitest men I ever met.’
Maxillus: ‘The sea appeals to me like a woman. It
smiles and dimples; the little waves, like fingers, run
lightly and caressingly up the shore. The gentle rustle,
the very shimmering of the sun upon its surface, have a
silken suggestion, as of some dainty toilette.’

When the Argonaut is apprised of the facts it will credit

Mrs. Crawford: ‘ Sainte-Beuve was a 9andy-haired man
with a phenomenally thin beard and rather swinish
cheeks. He had a pair of small, blue, shrewd, scrutinising
eyes, in which there was a deal of mental expression, but
no other. Not a gleam of sentiment ever passed over the
face. He liked his food, his wine, and an educated female
(I won t say woman)os a t6te-4-t6te in a restaurant. There
could have been no more selfish man. His pleasures were
so purely cerebral as to make him not companionable
except with men as clever as himself. He was also a book-
worm, and liked a silent, solitary house.*
Bushwoman: ‘ Ethel Turner’s pretty verses about the
sea almost persuade me to be a Christian to her in the
mnter of those bullocks in “Seven Little Australians.”
When our teams do twelve miles a day over average
country the men's oaths are comparatively mild- but
hers seemingly think nothing of a 40-mile trip. A dozen
of them start in the morning with a picnic-party for a
nice place 14 miles away, then go on six miles farther for
a >ig tree, and return to “ bring the children back in the
cool of the evening.” Please leave a little space here for
the remarks of Bill and Jim.’

are the gases of oxygen and hydrogen without the need-
ful ehctric spark to turn them into the water of life.
Morris's flitting lovely spectres have no flesh and blood.’

and w ritings ; he is being 1< aniedly discussed in French,

BLIND.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
His strife for daily bread died with the day:
Where’er his mood then led he’d wend his way.
With night his note to City’s hum would cease;
Far from men’s haunts he'd go in search of pea.
By rippling waves dancing o'er sandy bars,
He’d walk alone and gaze up at the stars.
There came a night when sea-birds heard a wail.
As of a human heart in sore travail.
Borne on swift wings, a soul-revealing mood
Had flashed on him his own drear solitude.
Amid the circling waters could be seen
Those stars above with all their silv’ry sheen.
M.L.

THE INDIFFERENT.
If from the lustre of the sun,
To catch your fleeting shade you raa.
In vain is alt your haste. Sir;
But if your feet reverse the race,
The fugitive will urge the chase,
And follow you as fast, Sir.
Thus, if at any time, as new,
Some scornful Chloe you pursue,
In hopes to overtake her;
Be sure you ne’er t ,o eager be,
But look upon’t as cold as sire,
And seemingly forsake her.
So I and Laura, t’other day,
Weie coursing round a cock of hay,
While I oould ne'er o’er get her;
But, when I found I ran in vain,
Quite tir’d I turn'd me back again,
And, flying from her, met her.
W. Pattison
A WARNING FOR WOOERS.
Some love for wealth and some for huet
And none of both these loves are true;
For when the mill hath lost her sailed
Then must the miller lose his vailes:
Of grass comes hay,
And flowers faire will soon decay:
Of ripe comes rottot^
In age all beautie is forgotten.
Some love too high and some too
And of them both great griefs do growth
And some do love the common sort.
And common folk use common sport.
Look not too high,
Lest that a chip fall in thine eye:
But high or lowe,
Te may be sure she is a shrewe.
But, sirs, I use to tell no tales,
Each fish that swims doth not bear scales;.
In every hedge I find not thornes:
Nor every beast doth carry homes :
1 say not soe,
That every woman causeth woe.
That were too broad :
Who loves not venom must shun tlie i*ad.
Who useth still the truth to tell,
May hlamfed be, though be say well;
Say crow is white, and snow is black.
Lay not the fault on woman’s back:
Thousands were good.
But few scap’d drowning in Noe’s fiaadt
Most are well bent,
I must say so, lest I be spent.

Depend on’t for a rule.
If she’s a fool she’ll wed the knave—

A SONG.
When thy beauty appears
In its graces and airs,
All bright as an angel new dropt from the s)rye{
At distance I gaze, and am awed by my leant
So strangely you dazzle my eye!
But when, without art,
Your kind thought you impart,
When your love runs in blushes through every veto ;
When it darts from your eyes, when it {nuts in you*
heart,
Then I know you’re a woman again.
“ There’s a passion and pride
In our sex,” she replied,
And thus (might I gratify both) I would dot
Still an angel appear to each lover beside,
But still be a woman to you.”
T. PARENSELL.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
GOOD-BYE, LYNETTE!
I have worked for you—toil made sweet, love,
And never I grudged an hour;
Now the dead leaves drift at our feet, love,
That trod by the trees in flower.
The scent of the rose blows over—
Head roses of all regret;
And you were my only lover,
So give me your hand, Lynette 1
O, Love-star loyally leading,
You fade in the gathering gray 1
O, Hope that has long lain bleeding,
I bury you deep to-day !
If Time has left never a token
The easier, love, to forget ;
So over the old spells broken
Give me your hand, Lynette !
I have sounded the deeps of sorrow,
I have drunk to the dregs of tears,
I shall suffer no more to-morrow,
No more than in dead past years ;
And what is our life but greeting
And parting and long regret ?
So here’s to our first mad meeting !
And here’s to our last, Lynette 1
GLENROWAN.
THE QUARRYMAN.
When the fuse is lit and the blast goes off,
The whole bill shakes with the shook ;
For there’s thirty feet of a quarry face,
And most of it solid rock.
The quarryman’s heart beats fast and high
With the clash of hammer and drill,
For he feels as the giants felt of old,
Who warred with the ribs of the hill.
The hammer of Thor that split the rocks
And cleft the hills apart,
He wouldn’t take for his tried old “ spawl,”
The delight of the quarryman’s heart.
Old as the world his kingdom is
And wide as the widest hill;
As a king he rules with an iron rod,
His sceptre a jumper or drill.
What Nature made in a million years
By the light of sun and star,
He overturns in a single day
With hammer and gad and bar ;
He knows the seams where the stone will split
To the sturdy stroke and strong —
Where the waters waved o’er the hard’ning mass
For myriad ages long.
He tells of tons by a shot brought down—
A “ knack ” that it’s hard to acquire !
Of “ narrow squeaks” when the fuse was damp
And the treacherous blast hung fire.
With hairy arms like the old moss’d rocks,
And hands that are hard as stone,
The quarryman boasts of his deeds, and thinks
No trade is as good as his own.
N.S.W.
ULOOLA.
A SCENT OF HAY.
The sun beats down upon the plains,
The grasses smell like hay,
And absent hands let slip the reins—
The rider is away
A thousand miles, a score of years ;
The crickets at his feet
Sound, in their whirring, to his ears
A reaper in the wheat.
He sees the nodding horses come,
The reaper’s circling arms ;
More distantly he hears the hum
From off the neighb’ring farms.
And hark ! his sister’s calling shrill
To Jessie on the farther hill.
Ah ! Jessie, dearest, ours was ne’er
The love that laughter grieves ;
I see you romp with flying hair
Among the drying sheaves.
I watch the flashing petticoat
Follow your movements fleet,
And swear ’tis like the foam about
Queen Aphrodite’s feet;
And, though I kissed you long ago
In the dim vanished years,
I hear your laugh, and feel your blow
Still'tingling in my ears.
A scent of hay—and then at will
I hold you close, I kiss you still.
Q.
R.A.F.
BODY AND SOUL.
When God had made your form
Of heavenly, matchless grace,
With tender touch and warm
He framed your glorious face.
He lit your eyes with his softest light,
He sprayed your hair with his sunbeams bright,
And made your beauty whole—
But Hell kept festival the night
When Satan made your soul.
GLENTAGGART BOGLE.

the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
1 Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted —
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission

paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under

applying to MS.

SATURDAY, MARCH 13, 1897-
The Result.
In three provinces—New South Wales, Vic-
toria, and Tasmania —the Federal election is
definitely settled. Definite returns from
South Australia and Westralia, where the poll
was later than in the eastern provinces, are
not available at time of writing, but the three
results already to hand are sufficiently inter-
esting. The Federal election was the first
occasion when the whole population of each
province voted as one constituency, there-
fore in some respects it probably presents a
much better reflex of popular opinion than a
poll conducted on ordinary principles could
do. In Victoria the contest was on a
straight-cut issue of Federation on Liberal
or Conservative lines ; there was practically
no sectarian or anti-Federal issue at all. In
New South Wales it was a confused, many-
sided contest between the anti-Federa-
tionists, the Catholic Federationists, Orange
Federationists, and Protestant but not
avowedly Orange Federationists, these latter
three groups being subdivided into Labor-
men, and Protectionists and Freetraders
with the fiscal issue partly hung up for the
time being, and sub-divided again into the
entire Federationists, of whom Carruthers,
Wise, and Ashton were fair specimens, and
the Federationists, like Reid and Lyne, who
were provincial so far as concerned the rail-
ways and some other questions, and only
moderately enthusiastic on other points. In
Tasmania about five candidates were enthu-
siastic Federationists, and the rest were
mostly old, well-advertised politicians who
travelled on their names and gave little
information as to the kind of Commonwealth
they proposed to construct. The poll was
largest in New South Wales, where the in-
trusion of the good old sectarian shindy
roused the public to sudden enthusiasm.
It was only a very little smaller in Victoria,
where it turned on a political issue only,
and where even that was a foregone con-
clusion ; and proportionately it was almost
microscopic in Tasmania, where there wasn’t
even a clear-cut political issue to fight about,
and where the Opposition and the Ministry
are so much alike that one might as well he
the other without anyone observing the
difference.
There were many tickets in the field. In
New South Wales the Protestant ticket con-
tained nine out of the ten winners, hut as it
was avowedly made up, not by selecting the
ten best men, but by picking the ten who
were most likely to win in any case, the
achievement didn’t amount to much. The
parsons who gathered together, and foamed,
and prayed that the Deity would be pleased
to cover their fellow-Christian, Patrick
Francis Moran, with defeat and humilia-
tion, T>y their own showing didn’t try to
choose either the most upright, or the most
intelligent, or the most patriotic, or even
the most Protestant candidates ; their one
desire was to throw in their weight with the
people who had the best chance, and by
giving them a few extra votes to make their
return additionally sure. Under these cir-
cumstances it is difficult to decide whether
the nine successful nominees won partly by
reason of the Protestant support, or whether
their majority was decreased by it, and
whether the tenth man, who didn’t win, was
blighted by the Protestants and lost his
chance through their clumsy interference. The
Orange ticket, which was almost the same as
the Protestant ticket, contained eight
winners. The National Ass, or Patriotic
League, or Prudent Federationist, or Mangy
Dog ticket didn’t contain the name of a
single winner, or the name of one individual
who came within miles of winning. The
Labor ticket was equally unfortunate. The
Bulletin ticket nominated five winners. In
Victoria, the ten candidates put forward by
the Age were all returned—a striking evi-
dence of the fact that David Syme’s paper
is not only the power behind the throne in
Victorian politics, but is, to all intents and
purposes, the throne itself. Six of the can-
didates recommended by The Bulletin were
returned. The Champion issued a ticket,
which included seven winners ; and the
Argus implored the public to vote for ten
nominees, and its disastrous support blasted
seven of them right off.
* * * *
In' New South Wales, Cardinal Moran’s
defeat was the principal surprise of the con-
test, The weakness of the support accorded
him makes the scare of the frantic Pro-
testant parsons, who wagged their monkey
beards in conclave about the danger of
Roman aggression, look supremely ridiculous.
The excitement of these twopenny bigots
probably roused up some additional Pro-
testants to vote against Mr. Moran ; but it
is equally probable that it also aroused
many additional Catholics and a good
many Protestants and a whole lot of dis-
gusted unbelievers to vote for him, and
that their interference rather helped
than hindered his candidature. Apparently,
however, Patrick Francis Moran was a
lost quantity in any case. If he had even
got an absolutely solid Catholic vote, with
no support whatever outside his own church,
he should have got ninth or tenth place ; but
apparently he didn’t get the solid Catholic
vote, or anything at all resembling it. Evi-
dently Cardinal Moran’s candidature was a
huge mistake. As the local head of the
most venerable church in Christendom he
was a shadowy power which no one could
really estimate, but which looked vast and
incredible. He voluntarily came down from
his pedestal to jostle with Foran and John
Haynes and O’Sullivan and Varney
Parkes, and he put his importance into
figures, and, as the result, he proved himself
something smaller than Copeland, and just
a fraction bigger than M‘Gowen and Criuk.
Therefore, the mystery is gone, and the
shadowy greatness is exploded, for the fact
that the visible local embodiment of the
Roman church is just a shade bigger than
M‘Go wen and Crick and only about twice
the size of Ferguson brings things down to
hard fact, and very cheap, commonplace,
hard fact at that.
* * * *
The utter failure of the National Ass or
Anti - Federation party, three of whose
party were absolutely at the end of the
long procession, while the others were very
adjacent to the same place, proves that New
South Wales, at all events, wants Federa-
tion. It may not be very sure what kind of
Federation it wants, but the fact that it
does want it is beyond question. Con-
sidering the huge masses of unreliable
literature with which the Prudent crowd
swamped the province, and the money
and energy and eloquence which it ex-
pended, the completeness of its down-
fall is remarkable. There is nothing in the
composition of the successful ten to show
exactly what species of Federation New
South Wales is in favor of. It apparently
selected, in most cases, the Federationists
who were best known, best advertised and
most personally popular, and it also, to all
appearance, went to some extent on the lines
of party politics. The list includes four
leading Protectionist politicians, four Free-
trade ditto, and two men not in politics. It
includes four absolutely solid Federationists ;
four who advocate partial Federation ; and
two who have given very little explanation
as to their views, beyond the bare statement
that they are Federationists of some kind.
But while Carruthers and Wise are among
the winners, the Labor ten, who advocated
very much the same principles, are all
defeated—some of them very badly defeated
indeed. Whether this means that the Labor-
vote is weakening, and that the party in
general isn’t what it was, or whether l
means something else, or whether it means
nothing at all, it is difficult to say.
» * * *
All the evidence tends to show that the
bulk of the electors never troubled to accu-
rately investigate the Fedei'al principles of
the candidates, and troubled very little or
knew very little about their views in detail,
or if it did know them it preferred to be
guided by other considerations. Thus 85,000
votes were cast for Carruthers, while Ash-
ton, on almost exactly the same opinions,
scored about 20,000. Again, H eydon, of
the Prudent Federation crowd, obtained
16,000 votes, while his colleague, Nugent
Robertson, who upheld the same banner,
and who should have got the same votes if
they were given to Heydon for his opinions
only, comes ignominiously at the end with
2000. Of the ten successful candidates eight
are the best-advertised politicians in the pro-
vince—the eight whose names are oftenest in
print and are most familiar to everybody who
reads the daily papers. Apparently the
public decided —first, that it wanted ho sec-
tarian disturbance in the matter; second,
that it wanted Federation; third, that it
didn’t want the Labor element, but desired,
in the main, to stick to the two old, solid,
stock crowds that it had heard most about —
their chronic fiscal shindy having kept them
well to the front. Then it voted principally
for the old familiar hacks of the old familiar
parties, with no regard at all for finer shades
of opinion. That is the moral of the New
South Wales Federal election, so far as the
results show any moral at all.
* * * *
The Victorian result throws very little
light on the Federal sentiment of the pro-
vince in most respects, though, on one at
least, it makes it sufficiently clear. But it
throws a great illumination on local politics.
The candidates were divided mostly into the
Conservative or Upper House party, and the
Liberal party as represented by the Assem
bly. The men who came out on top include
eight Liberals and two Conservatives, and
these two are Conservatives of a very mild
type ; also, even they got in with difficulty,
and probably for no other reason than that
they were on the ticket of the all-powerful
Age. Victoria, unlike New South Wales,
has one Labor-man, Teen with, among the
successful ten. The results show—first, that
Premier Turner is the most popular and the
most trusted among the public men of Vic-
toria ; he comes first on the list by a big
majority, whereas in New South Wales Pre-
mier Reid is only a moderate second. Also,
the fact that Turner’s two colleagues, Pea-
cock and Isaacs, are high up amongst
the chosen representatives, shows that
the Ministry in general has the strong
support of the people. The dismal
failure of every Legislative Council can-
didate, except the two who were sup-
ported by the Ministerial newspaper, de-
molishes in one act the impudent pretence
that the House of 48 Rich Landlords really
represents the popular sentiment, and proves
that the Victorian people want a Federal
Parliament as unlike the House of Landlords,
and as unlike the one which the 48 Rich
Landlords would naturally construct, as is
possible. As a commentary on the alleged
Freetrade revival, not one Freetrade candi-
date came within gunshot of success; and
the fact that not one Oppositionist, not even
M ‘lntyre and Gillies, the leaders and shin-
ing lights of the party, was at the front, makes
the chances of the old boom party for next
election look too small to be worth discus-
sion. The exact details of the Federation
which the Victorians want are about as hard
to arrive at, judging from the ten selected
delegates, as the exact views of the New
South Wales people on the same subject.
But the exact views of Victoria about the
Argus and the Upper House and the Tory
Opposition, and about its newly-returned
Gillies, and Murray Smith, the leader of
the Foreign-trade forlorn hope, and about
the large, dull, hesitating MTntvre, who
fondly imagines the country is waiting
anxiously for a chance to make him Premier
—all these are quite evident.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Few Casual Difficulties.
If the Federal Convention adopts the scheme,
advocated by Carruthers and others, whereby
the railways are to be taken over at a valuation
based on net earnings, instead of all the debts,
railways, telegraphs, post-offices, fortifications!
lighthouses, &c., being taken over in a lump
without inquiry, it will start a fine series of
complications. The following are a few of
them. The telegraphs must, like the railways
be taken over at a valuation based on net earn-
ings. The post-offices must be treated on the
same basis ; none of the other provinces, except
W estialia, can make so good a showing on the
railways as N.S.W., but most of them show
better on the posts and telegraphs, and if the
valuation system is to be applied where N S W
and Westralia will gain by it, then the other
three will inevitably demand that it shall come
in likewise where they will realise a profit. The
fortifications, barracks, guns, lighthouses, &c.
will have to be valued by experts and taken
over at their present worth also. The railway
valuation, too, isn’t so simple as it looks. It is
not merely a question of taking over the lines
according to the net earnings as shown bv each
province, for each province has a method"of its
own by which it arrives at the earnings, and
they must be ca culated all over again on a
uniform scale before anything can be arrived
at. in Victoria, pensions to superannuated em-
ployes are reckoned as part of the expenses ; in
N.S.W. they are not. Some provinces, esp e .
eially N.S.W., swell the apparent earnings bv
making large payments to the railway depart-
ment as the value of free-passea to politicians
distinguished visitors, unemployed, &c. • Vj c !
toria swells its revenue by paying the railways a
subsidy on condition that they carry local coal
at reduced rates. Some provinces pay the
railways a needlessly large amount for carrying
postal matter ; others do not. As the matter
merely consists of a cross-entry in a book, the
railways can be credited with any amount un
to twenty millions a year or more under this
heading ; it costs nothing save the ink used in
making the entry, and it merely depends on
whether the local idea is to make the railways
look profitable while the post-office isn’t or
whether the yearning is to make the post-office
look profitable while the railways are not. In
Victoria the lines are almost all fenced in-
they are mostly fenced in N.S.W., and mostly
unfenced in S.A. Fencing doesn’t pay any
cash return, but it is an expense which must be
faced as population increases, and the liability
which is handed over to the Commonwealth
under this head counts for something. The
next trouble is that no two provinces have the
same policy as regards the expenses which
are paid out of revenue and called main-
tenance, and those which are paid out of
borrowed money and called construction
and this matter has to be carefully adjusted
before a valuation can be arrived at. Also
the amount and condition of the rolling-
stock must be considered ; old, worn - out
vehicles may earn as much just now as new
ones, hut there is a great difference between
having to replace them next year or ten years
hence. When the estimate is made and the
amount of debt to be taken over is decided,
the question which next arises is—which part
of the debt is to be taken over by the Common-
wealth ? Is it to be the 4 and 5 and 6 per cent,
portion of it, or mainly the 3£ per cent, part?
Are the provinces to keep the part bearing the
smallest interest, or the highest, or some of
both ? Supposing the 3J and 4 per cent, debt
is about enough to take over in payment for the
assets surrendered by the States, does N.S.W.
get any special concession as compared with
Victoria, because it has less debt at 4 per cent,
and more at 3£ ? Also, do Victoria and N.S.W.
get any special concessions from S.A. because
the latter has a larger proportion of 4 percent,
debt and a smaller proportion of 3jj per cent,
ditto than either? Again, does Victoria get
any concession as against N.S.W. because the
4 per cent, debt which it will hand over runs
out at an earlier date, and can be sooner coif-
verted into 3 per cents., and a big saving thus
effected ? The idea of handing over the pro-
vincial assets and liabilities on a strict valuation
basis, instead of taking them as they come
without question, looks very beautiful, but it
will bleach the hair of the financier who attempts
to figure things out.

Orthodoxy and “Blanky.”
The panning - out of the Federal election
strongly supports The Bulletin’s contention
that the sectarian spectre isn’t feeling well in
N.S.W. these times. On Thursday night half
Sydney turned out to watch the results as they
arrived, and where the figures were* exhibited
the streets were practically impassable, and
the air was filled with a sound like 2000 people
saying “ blanky ” all at the same instant. And
in all the great city not one chunk of road-
metal, so far as is known, was thrown in the
sectarian cause ; nobody was stoushed for the
love of God ; nobody obtrusively damned the
Pope ; nobody cheered once for the Orange ten ;
nobody vociferated for the Cardinal worth a
cent.; and Derry and William 111. and the
Protestant conference and St. Patrick were as
dead to that great, good-humored mob as if
they had never been alive. The 800,000 Aus-
tralian natives in N.S.W. are ten years older
than they were in the last days of the Kiama
ghost, and are making themselves felt in poli-
tics, and the white native is almost as stolidly
indifferent as the black aboriginal to parsonical
influences. The infuriated clergy of Sydney
wildly spanked the dead horse of orthodoxy in
the ears of this multitude to no visible purpose.
They explained to their hearers that the
universe is infinite, that the worlds in-
cluded in it are infinite and beyond
number, and that all the numerals in
creation, though they commenced with a
nine and filled fifty thousand huge volumes
with noughts, would not even begin to explain
how many they were. Then they explained
that our globe is one small unit among them all
—a thing akin to an odd speck of dust lying
about promiscuously nowhere in particular, and
that Australia is an odd fraction of this speck,
and N.S.W. a mere fraction of Australia; and
they invited the people to believe that the
Almighty architect of the whole affair was
personally watching them and their little creeds
hustle each other on the side of this speck to
keep down an individual whose views about the
Architect and the speck were somewhat dif-
ferent from their own. And the 800,000 irre-
verent natives said something that sounded
like “ blanky ” and took practically no further
notice. The great Australian adjective is, to a
large extent, the symbol of the great and grow-
ing Australian contempt for sectarianism and
sectarian squabbles. If the churches can put
down “ blanky,” then sectarianism may take
permanent root in Australia ; the present signs
are that “ blanky ” will probably put down the
churches instead. The Bulletin doesn’t pre-
tend that “ blanky ” is preferable to bigotry —
on the whole, it is inclined to think it would
rather have bigotry than “ blanky it merely
notes the fact that “ blanky ” is uppermost at
present, and seems likely to continue so.

State Rights and National Wrongs.
The party which wants to make the Federal
Parliament mostly a shadow, and to reserve the
bulk of the power in the hands of the State
Legislatures, has one argument that is dished up
until it becomes a weariness. The United
States, it is said, is run on this principle;
therefore the principle must be correct. The
average auditor hasn’t the shadow of an idea
how the U.S. gets on under the State Rights
system, but when it is quoted as a glorious


example lie assumes that it is right else it
wouldn’t be quoted. He is told to “ Look at
the United States,” and he looks vaguely in an
eastward direction towards Watson’s Bay and
feels a good deal convinced. The U.S. is the
country where each State makes its own mar-
riage and divorce laws, so that the woman who
is a wife in one town may be only an un-
married mother in the next one across the State
border, and the lawful infant in this village
may be a bastard in the one adjacent. It is a
country, too, of many weird political complica-
tions. Not very long ago, a Louisiana mob
murdered some Italian citizens, and the State
courts refused to do justice on the murderers ;
also the State Legislature declined to pay com-
pensation to their widows and orphans. Where-
upon it was discovered, in the course of the
row with Italy which naturally ensued, that
the miserably impotent Federal Government
couldn’t compel the arrest of the criminals ; it
couldn’t compel compensation to be paid ; it
couldn’t interfere, unless requested to do so by
the State authorities, to prevent more butchery ;
in fact, it could do nothing to prevent any little
wild-cat State of the Union running it into a
war with a big European Power if the State
felt so inclined, and when the war came it
would have to do the fighting all the same.
The Federal Government, in this case, got out
of the difficulty by privately grovelling
to Italy and paying compensation without
the authority of Congress, from the secret
service fund—in other words, it crawled out of
a very awkward situation by misappropriating
the public money. The U.S. Government had
even to break the law very badly before it
could interfere to suppress the Chicago riots ;
according to the strict letter of the beautiful
State Rights principle it should have stood by
idly while the second city of the Union was
wrecked and possibly laid in ashes. It also
broke the law badly when it suppressed slavery;
in fact, it is so impotent that it can do very
little in the way of keeping order without
breaking the law. That republic is a solid
proof that our Federal Government should be
made as strong as possible ; on the State Rights
question it isn’t so much an example to be fol-
lowed as a horrible warning as to where this
country may get to if it goes in for being a ser-
vile copyist of things it doesn’t rightly under-
stand.
The Prisoner of ’Frisco.
In spite of x-rays, treaties of arbitration, the
cinematograpiie, the poems of J. C. Neild,
and such matters —human nature doesn’t seem
to go ahead much, after all. It isn’t easy, at
all events, to get swelled-head about the “ pro-
gress of the species ” in view of the fact that
the prisoner of ’Frisco is having greatness thrust
upon him by the women who are “ sending him
every day piles of flowers and quantities of
dainty victuals, and inquiring anxiously what
more they can do for his comfort.” And this,
be it remembered, wholly and solely upon the
strength of their belief in his double-dyed crimi-
nality. As an ordinary, single-barrelled mur-
derer, or as a man reasonably suspected of being
innocent, it’s pretty certain that Mr. Ashe-
Harwood-Bctler w'ould have had to content
himself with the delicate attentions of his
gaolers, but by the delightful possibility that
he is the cold-blooded and treacherous assassin
of an indefinite number of confiding persons
he is at once invested with a putrid kind
of interest, and placed upon a pedes-
tal for the diseased hero - worship of
the neurotic and sensation-chasing woman who
appears to have reached her highest develop-
ment in the States. Sentimental and super-
sympathetic though a woman of this stamp
may appear upon the surface, there are grounds
for believing that this craving to be associated
by hook or crook with some notorious criminal
has its root in a criminal tendency latent in the
woman herself. Hysteria and homicide are
mysteriously, but unmistakeably related, and
hysterico-sentimentalism is a marked charac-
teristic of the congenital and instinctive
criminal. The people, also, who whoop most
valiantly for “battle, murder, and sudden
death,” are mostly mighty careful of their own
skins, because theirs is that sub-hysterical tem-
perament which loves personal notoriety as
much as it dreads personal danger. As long,
therefore, as we have still with us the war-
preaching parson, the murderer-worshipping
woman, and the caterwauling cut-throat, we
had better cease to blow a fantasia in our own
honor upon the Trumpet of the Nineteenth
Century.
Straight-Out!
Dear Bulletin,— The Turko-Grecian amenities
in Crete have brought down upon us a cataract
of the weary old slush about the “ brutal and
bloodthirsty Turk ” and the “ poor, persecuted
Eastern Christian ” —patient, virtuous, and in-
offensive, according to the prating pulpiteers
who have had no personal experience either of
him or of his supposed- oppressor. Now, I
know well both Turk and Levantine, and I
deliberately assert that the average Eastern
Christian isn’t fit to be mentioned in the same
day with the average Moslem. Hassan is
mostly a stolid, goodmatured kind of animal
kind to his family, charitable to the unfortunate,
not often a liar, and very rarely a rogue ;
whereas Spirito— besides being pretty gener-
ally both liar and rogue—is almost invari-
ably a sneak, heartily despised as such by a
people who—at all events in the middle and
lower classes —are just about as manly as
they make ’em,” and who are, paradoxi-
cally, much more in accord with the Chris-
tian ideal than most of those who profess to
be striving thereafter. For which latter reason
—speaking, myself, as an entirely and hopelessly
creed less person —I say, straight-out, that the
religion of Mahomet produces better practical
results locally than the Christian religion
that the Turk, under ordinary conditions, is a
better man in every relation of life than the
“Christian” within his gates —and that, when
it comes to reciprocal throat-cutting, he is not
one whit more ferocious than the Christian
aforesaid. In these slaughterings at Crete and
elsewhere it has been six of one and half-a-
dozen of the other. Hatred begets hatred, and
the Turk is no more to blame than the Greek or
the Armenian for a racial antagonism which he
inherits ready-made and piping-hot. Also,
when an eminent evangelic went a-maundering,
the other day, about the “ accursed religion of
the sword,” and the “brutal murdering of
thousands for no other offence than that of
being Christians,” he didn’t remember —or
made it convenient to forget—that the Thirty
Years’ War alone, out of countless wholesale
Christian buteherings, cost a vastly greater
number of lives than the whole Mahometan
propaganda, and that the most devilish tor-
ments ever devised by mind of man were
invented by Christians— for Christians 1 It is
Christianity—after 19 centuries of mouthing
about “peace and goodwill towards men”—
that is to-day essentially the “ religion of the
sword ” —of the repeating-rifle, the machine-
gun, and the torpedo. The “ Christian nations”
have become little better than vast camps of
elaborately-trained killers of their kind, and
the brain of the Christian inventor is every day
devising yet more and more ingenious con-
trivances for the wholesale immolation of his
fellow-Christians. And all the while, from
pulpit, and altar, and platform are droned-
out the same old mendacities about the
blessings of the Bible, and the growth of
brotherly love, and the regeneration of man-
kind by a creed which has stubbornly obstructed
every single step forward in the real enlighten-
ment of humanity. Also, when, one of these
days, the bayonet-bristling situation shall
become no longer endurable, and two nations
spring at each other’s throats, the same old
snuffle will be wafted upwards from the sham-
bles, and the winning side will blasphemously
bless the Lord for having helped it to be the
more successful murderer ! Does God ever
laugh ? —Heretic.
♦
The coming Australian Convention will start,
so far as certain of the provinces are concerned,
with almost the whole of the political stock-in-
trade in hand which the first Chartist Conven-
tion, 60 years ago, vainly hoped to achieve for
England. Humanity progresses, at all events,
in sections. The first Chartist Convention met
in the Dr. Johnson Hotel, in London, in 1839,
and drew up a charter of demands which
scared Liberals and roused Tories to shout for
the police. Yet the six points of that charter
were as follow :
1. Universal Suffrage.
2. Vote by Ballot.
3. Annual Parliaments.
4. Abolition of Property Qualification.
5. Equal Electoral Districts.
6. Payment of Members.
With the exception of annual Parliaments, most
divisions of Australia now possess all the points
of that charter. This of itself should give the
Convention a base broad enough to enable it to
make the Federation the freest and, humanly,
the most powerful of political organisations.
What could men like the Chartists do, hemmed
in as they were by the prejudices of ages and
denied the use of the political instruments
Australian Conventionists now possess ? Vote
by ballot was called revolution, manhood suff-
rage execrated as a mad folly, abolition of'pro-
perty qualifications denounced as the dream of
sansculottes, and payment of members stigma-
tised as robbery of the exchequer.

Truly the world is full of people who can’t read
ordinary large print. The Bulletin explained
all it knew that it wasn’t giving a list of the men
who, it believed, would get in, but of those who,
in its opinion, ought to get in. It was perfectly
aware that its list had no great chance, because
it selected its men on their merits, whereas the
ordinary Trotter Bethune kind of person
always votes for the best-advertised old hack
politicians; it didn’t aim at tipping winners,
and it said so over and over again. But the
Australian is apparently so accustomed to
horse-race tips that he can’t understand the
distinction. The Bulletin announced the 40
men whom it wanted to see returned according
to its own political conscience, and Trotter
Bethune and his kind can’t get rid of an idea
that this paper had gone out with a bag and a
hooked nose and was betting on t hese ten.
Verily the great horse-disease breaks out in
strange places.

In Victoria they propose to hang very par-
ticularly the survivor of the attempted double
suicide at Middle Park, but in the same pro-
vince, when an assassin was convicted the other
day of systematically scattering incurable
disease by selling milk from a tuberculosed
cow, the penalty was <£lo fine. The horrible
animal and its owner resided in a suburb. It
was a particularly bad cow —in fact, its disease
had almost degenerated into living decomposi-
tion, and its state was visible even to the most
unprofessional eye, and it was set down as
almost the record diseased cow of the province.
The owner, in addition to selling the milk as
long as the cow would hold together, finished up
by trying to sell the animal itself—presumably
for food. The law provides a maximum penalty
of <£loo or two years’ imprisonment, which is
sufficiently cheap for wholesale murder done
for the sake of the price of a few quarts of
milk—in fact, considering the view the law
takes of Butler, it seems amazingly cheap. As
the local Doo berries, however, fixed the
price of about the worst case going at only £lO
it appears that even the moderate penalty
provided by law is mainly a matter of form.
The dirty J.P. and the insanitary magistrate
make reform practically impossible in such
cases ; all over Australia the average price of
wilful murder by diseased milk and diseased
meat is hardly more than £5 at the out-
side. And, under these circumstances, the
absurdity of the fuss that is made about a
comparatively insignificant alleged criminal
like Butler is apparent. Obviously Butler,
on the same basis, should, if convicted, be fined
£2, and ordered to pay the money within a
week.

Dear Bulletin,—Here is a Sydney daily
paper paragraph :
At Newtown Court yesterday, Aug. Conrade was
charged, at the instance of Matilda Coot (agent for
James Ellison), with having on 19th inst., removed a
quantity of furniture from a house in Park-avenue, Ash-
iield, to avoid distraint for rent. Defendant said he was
out of work, and sold r.he furniture to get food. Mr.
Johnson, S.M., inflicted a line of £5 (being double the
estimated value of the furniture), levy and distress, with
the alternative of six months’ hard labor. Conradk was
allowed a month in which to pay the fine.
This is a case where only the poor man goes
to gaol. It is under an old Act of Geo. 11.,
which provides in effect that where a tenant
fraudulently conveys away his goods to evade
distraint, the landlord can recover double their
value by action in the Supreme Court. But if
the value of the goods doesn’t exceed <£so, then
he may proceed in the police-court, and it is
only in these proceedings that the tenant is
liable to imprisonment in default of payment.
The magistrate has no option to give less than
six months with hard labor unless the money be
sooner paid. So, if you remove £6O worth,
you can only be sued in the Supreme Court
for £l2O, and if you haven’t the money,
the landlord can whistle for his debt and costs.
There is an easy way of evading the statute —
but poor devils who want to sell their furniture
to get food don’t know of it. The Act refers to
the fraudulent removal of the tenant’s goods.
There’s nothing to prevent his removing any-
one else’s. So if he sells the goods first, then
he can take them away safely. Of course, the
landlord can still distrain on them, and stop
their going, if he gets there in time. But that’s
another matter. He can’t send the tenant to
gaol, for then the goods are not his —they are
the buyer’s. So if he gives a bill-of-sale—they
then belong in law to the bill-of-sale holder.
This isn’t speculation ; it’s established law*. So
well-established that, though the same Act
gives the landlord the right to follow the goods
removed and distrain on them wherever they
are, the careful lawyer always advises him not
to risk it. There are too many pitfalls. But
it’s a safe game going for a poor beggar who
has only a few sticks, and cheap even if you
lose. By the way, the anomaly has a curious
origin. When the Act was passed, if you got
a judgment against a man in the Supreme
Court, you could ca. sa. him, and keep him in
gaol till he rotted —or paid. So the provision
about six months’ imprisonment was a merciful
one, in favor of the poor man. Now that the
ca. sa. for debt has been to a large extent
abolished, it cuts the other way.— Shark.
“Operator” on the telegraph code question: —
Dear Bulletin,—The howl from “Lightning Squirter”
in your issue of 20th Feb. was caused by sheer funk. I
ought to know, as I felt it badly myself some years ago,
when, after being suckled for eight years on the “per-
fect” Australian code, I joined the Cable Co., and found
I had to learn the miserable Universal. The dear old
sounds “engraved on my brain,” however, wore out in
one week, and the next I took charge of the circuit. The
land circuit in the same office used the Australian alpha-
bet, and for seven years, all day long, I heard the two
codes rattling away, cheek by jowl. Strange to say,
“ malgr6 moi,” I grew to detest the jerky jingle of the
old love. Strange also to say, there was never any con-
fusion, nor were mistakes made, although a man would
work Australian one day and Universal the next. Yet
“Lightning Squirter” foresees terrible havoc played in
Australian business when the change is absolutely made.
He doesn’t give outsiders a high opinion of the ability of
his fellow-operators. The Universal code is faster.
Its alphabet of the 26 letters contains 82 dots and
dashes, while the Australian contains 86-. TO say, there-
fore, that the latter is faster is to utter “tommy
rot.” What is “Lightning Squirter” giving us about
the American code? The Yankees use a fearful and
wonderful system of dots that would puzzle the Austra-
lian operator worse than shorthand. The Universal
alphabet contains signals to meet the requirements of
every language and the whole range of accented letters.
The question is, why should Australia put up with a
mongrel code neither Universal nor American, when the
code proposed is scientifically arranged and actually
faster ? I would like to assure the general body of opera-
tors that there is really no cause for alarm. There are
only 13 letters different, F and R are the only two that
clash, and a little daily practice will prove that the new
code is astonishingly easy to master.— Operator.
“ Crimson Thread ” on the same subject:—
Editor Bulletin. —Let me briefly answer the two main
points of “ Silken Bond’s ” letter, viz., the alleged “ sim-
plicity ” of the American code and his speed figures, the
result of “ recent exhaustive tests.” The American code
contains certain symbols called spaced letters. For in-
stance, c is composed of a combination of the signs repre-
senting i and e ; two e’s form the letter o ; several more are
built on the same plan, whilst l is signalled by a “ pro-
longed dash,” which also stands for the cypher 0!
“ Unless very carefully formed,” says Gulley, in his
“ Practical Telegraphy ” (page 249), “ these letters are
liable to be mistaken for others.” The “spaced letter”
and the “ prolonged dash ” with a dual significance
should find no place in any scientifically - con-
structed alphabet, and the American man in this respect
showed a poverty of resource quite foreign to his
nation. There are no complexities of this kind in the
International code. It was designed on the principle
which should govern all codes and cyphers ; one char-
acter, one signification. Wheatstone’s automatic, in its
present form, is not possible in Australia so long as the
“ prolonged dash ” monstrosity exists. As regards speed,
“A. 5.8.” is sadly in error. His “38 words per minute
for an hour or longer ” is good work, but it is not sensa-
tional, while his modest allowance of 31 words per
minute for the International is sheer nonsense. Some
authenticated Maoriland records herewith. During one
of our cable breaks, a financial statement was cabled
from Wanganui to Wakapuaka—the only alternative
route. Time taken, two hours ; rate of working, 43)4
words per minute. The same operator has frequently ex-
ceeded 40, and Maoriland rejoices in many men of his
calibre. The frequent cable breaks brought out picked
men from whom a minimum of 35 or 36 words per minute
was expected. The men responded so well that 40 words
per minute was often exceeded. Six hours a day, in two
three-hour spells, with an interim of six hours, was the
daily task of these officers. In conclusion, let me remind
“A. 5.8.” that the International is also a Morse code :
moreover, the Australian code is not the American code,
pure and unadulterated. Let him refer to his Culley and
see for himself.— Crimson Thread.
Melb. Age thinks each State of the Common-
wealth should fix its own franchise for Federal
elections because “Victoria is no more en-
titled to dictate to Queensland.and West Aus-
tralia that they shall adopt the ‘ one man one
vote ’ than that Mr. Kingston should insist
on all the Australian world accepting female
suffrage for federal purposes.” This is the
last possible absurdity in logic. Victoria has
an absolute right to help to dictate how N.S. W.
and S.A. and Tasmania shall elect their repre-
sentatives in the Federal Parliament, and they
have an absolute right to dictate how Victoria
shall elect hers, and each province has a right
to dictate all she can to all the other provinces.
In the Federal Parliament, the vote of a member
irom the Northern Territory will govern Vic-
toria just as much as the vote of one from Mel-
bourne ; and the vote of the man from Cool-
gardie will govern N.S.VV. as much as that of
the man from Sydney ; and the Sydney and
Melbourne men will boss Tasmania as much as
the men from Hobart and Launceston, and
rice versa right through the whole list. There-
fore, if eacii man hasn t as much say in fixing
the federal franchise for the other provinces as
for his own he has lost his powers of self-govern-
ment. If the theory that each province is to
tix its own Federal franchise holds good, then
each electorate should fix its own provincial
franchise ; if N.S. W. hasn’t any right to a voice
in deciding how Westralia shall elect its Federal
members, then Sydney has no right to a voice in
deciding on what basis Parramatta shall choose
its local M.L.A. The proposal to have five
different franchises in the Commonwealth is
gratuitous confusion, with no advantage about
it to make up for the confusion.
A Northern correspondent to The Bulletin :
There exists in Sydney an agency which, for 10s. 6d. a
head, will forward to any part of Queensland (or any
other place, for anything I know to the contrary) big,
strapping fellows from 16 years of age, bound down by
agreement as “ generally useful ” station-hands for three
years, at the following rate of “wages”: £l3 for the
first year, £ls for the second year, and £2O for the last
year, with “the usual board and residence.” Out of the
first quarter’s wages the boss deducts the passage-money,
which to Mackay is £3 10s., and which is refunded to the
“ reserves the right to cancel this agreement at any time,
upon giving one month’s notice to the said
should he fail to comply with the terms of this agree-
ment . . . the said ‘ emplovd ’ reserving the same right of
notice, providing the amount of passage-money advanced
to the said ‘ ’is earned.” All the words in in-
verted commas are copied verbatim from the agreement.
There are —or, more correctly speaking, there were —three
of these unfortunates in this district, two of whom have
“ jacked ”up after about six months’ work. The third is
a tall, strapping fellow of over 20 years of age, the son of
apparently well-to-do parents ; he replaced a man who
was driving a butcher’s cart for 15s. a week and tucker—
I suppose driving a meat-cart comes in underthe heading
of the duties of a generally-useful station-lad as per
agreement. The Q Government is going to spend large
sums of money in getting immigrants to come here ; why
not send the necessary commission to the person who
runs that Sydney agency, which, I am sure, will be able
to supply Queensland with all the cheap-labor needed at
half-a-guinea per head, a reduction made on large con-
signments ?—A.S.B.G.
Brisbane (Q.) Courier :
These colonies wish the world to clearly understand
that they thoroughly identify themselves with the mother
country.
Is there any such wish on the part of these
provinces? No. For instance, these provinces
wish China to clearly understand that they
don’t identify themselves with England in the
matter of admitting pigtail-yellowskins free of
poll-tax. These provinces wish Japan to clearly
understand that they don’t identify themselves
with England in the matter of the Anglo-
Japanese treaty. Pious newspapers like Bris-
bane Courier ought not to imagine or invent
Australian wishes —the average Australian is
quite capable of being a good-enough liar
on his own account. The average Australian
doesn’t yearn to identify himself with the
foreign scoopful of dirt that the pious editor
calls “ the mother country.” The average
Australian’s mother-country is Australia. The
average Australian doesn’t care a rap about
identification with England, a place he has
never seen. He’ll hooray for the Queen, if
there is beer about; hut he’d hooray for a man
who rode a bicycle over a bar-counter and
set up the drinks for the township. The
average Australian doesn’t care a curse
about anything except horse-racing, cricket
(at which he believes he can whop Eng-
land) and, just now, biking. The average
Australian’s general attitude is—“Never-
mind !” or “Who’s a-goin’ to do it?” Bull,
on the other hand, brags about his “ kawlinies ”
—his “ beastly kawlinies.” What Bull really
loves about Australia are his mortgages and
liens on it, and the dividends that he gets out
of it. The Australian Fat Man talks England,
and, in his way, loves England as much as he
can love anything—because he loves himself,
and rejoices in “ splashes ” and grovel and titles
and baubles of all sorts. The workers of Aus-
tralia owe England only the love that the pro-
digal owes to his pawnbroker. Poll the
Australias to-morrow and the workers—even
those who hooray for England and Fat Man,
cursing both in their hearts of hearts—would
sign this answer: “These provinces wish the
world to clearly understand that they do not
identify themselves with England, and that
England knows it, and lias to let them have
their own way when they want it. Some day
these provinces will get tired of being too lazy
to run their show under their own shingle.”
The Wesleyan Conference assembled at Mel-
bourne snorted fiercely at “General” Booth
because of his proposed invasion of Fiji and the
other Pacific islands, and suspects the Army
man of meditating a poaching expedition
amongst their spiritual preserves. The Rev.
Bickford pointed out that to all intents and
purposes the Wesleyan missions own Fiji.
“Ninety per cent, of the people are found
every Lord’s Day in our churches.” “We
have 3800 paid workers there,” &c., &c. Booth
will, no doubt, reason that the island which
will support 3800 professional sin-shifters will
support anything. The Army will wade in,
band and all, and Fiji will have to pay the
piper, also the drummer. Evidently the Wes-
leyan Conference believes that when a sect has
got any particular savage by the wool old Eng-
land should grant it a monopoly of that savage
to the hitter end. The Fijian, it seems, has
become very industrious under Wesleyan rule.
The Bulletin is sorry for him ; once he lived
in beautiful idleness in an Eden of his own, and
now he has to work like a fiend to keep a lot
of holy foreigners, who offer in return their
Eden, which, happily, they don’t want them-
selves.
The Age begins to speak coldly of the
doings of Vic. railway-manager Mathieson. Fact
seems to be that D. Syine will not be satisfied
until the railways are run by a private Co. Federa-
tion should put a head on any little game of that
kind, however.

The Australasian Institute of Mining
Engineers will hold its annual meeting at Sydney
in Easter week. Papers on mining, metallurgical
and geological subjects will be discussed, and ex-
cursions to places of mining interest have been
arranged.*
A sceptical doctor recently called at the
Edison Electric Parlor, 162 Pitt-street, Sydney,
to test the effectiveness of the X rays. He placed
his hand upon the fluorescent screen, and straight-
way saw several shot pellets which had been
lodged in his hands in boyhood. The latest
novelties for the Vitascope, sensational and
humorous, provide more entertainment than all
the numerous imitators put together. Other
attractions are the Edison Kinetoscope and
Kinetophone.*

Henry Bracy’s new operatic venture at
Sydney Lyceum commenced well on Saturday
iight. The “Beggar Student” may not be
an absolutely first-class opera, but it is bright and
pleasant and musical, and sufficiently comic for
any reasonable purpose, and it hasn t been ground
out persistently by the fleshless hand of Time on
the barrel-organ of monotony until it has become
a weariness. The “ Student ”is one of the least-
hackneyed of bygone operas, and to most of the
Saturday audience it might as well have been new.
All the evening the proceedings weren’t once
audibly broken in upon by the strident voice of
the person who knows all about it telling his
neighbour what was going to happen next, which
is a sufficient evidence in support of the above
assertion. Henry Bracy has few equals as a stage-
manager, and it naturally follows that the mechani-
cal part of the show goes smoothly, and the mount-
ing is excellent. The co. is also, in most respects,
up to a high standard. Miss Mabelle Darley
made a big success fro ,n the first, and, as her ner
vousness wore off somewhat, her success was still
greater and the enthusiasm of the audience became
tremendous. It is a long time since an almost
unknown actress got such a reception in Sydney.
Miss Darley has a very pretty soprano voice, a
graceful figure, considerable ability as an actress, and
a face and a smile and a very special set of teeth
which made the first-night gallery fall down and
worship her then and there. Miss Julia Spencer,
also more or less unknown, trills her little part
satisfactorily. Mrs. Bracy is still the same sterling
artist, and Bracy himself as Simon the impostor,
John Forde, who plays his very old part as Colonel
Ollendorf, and Miss Nina Osborne, leave little to
be wished for. The chorus, bossed by George
Pack, has been drilled into a remarkable state of
perfection, and altogether the “ Beggar Student ”
goes as well as it ever did —also as well as it is
ever likely to go. The “Student” isn’t in itself
an absolutely overwhelming opera, but Bracy’s
co. brings out all the music, and John Forde in
particular brings out all the comedy there is in it.
Miss Darley’s smile and the aforesaid set of teeth
do the rest.
** # *
“Matsa” has, so far, crowded Her
Majesty’s all it can hold, and only the most grasp-
ing kind of management could ask for more.
Regarded as a pictorial outbreak, the view of
ancient Memphis and the Rising of the Nile could
hardly be improved upon, and, if it can be
guaranteed by any reliable authority that bygone
Khem did look at all like'that, the present writer’s
desire to start back 4000 years or so, and live in
the land where all these graceful brown legs are, is
simply overwhelming. Howard Vernon’s mummy,
which didn't get the space it deserved last week,
is in keeping with the rest of the picture ; Vernon
has, apparently, a natural capacity for being a
generous-souled moral mummy, and his old
Egyptian aspect is very touching. The variety
business which is thrown into the last act is
the weakest part of the show —barring Little
Gulliver, who is always welcome. It has no
relation to the old Egyptian gorgeousness
which went before it, or the new Egyptian
gorgeousness which is around it, and Knowles, not
being got up as a mummy, seems out of keeping,
and his only connection with the Rising of the
Nile lies in the writer’s idea that some of his
jokes must have had a narrow escape from drown-
ing in that aged catastrophe. But, all the same,
“ Matsa ” is a marvellous show, and should have
a long and prosperous future before it, for it em-
bodies the sudden arrival of more paint, and sun-
light effects, and desert, and pale moon, and
massive architecture than the eye can take in all
at once. Moreover, the brown legs that swarm in
Memphis are just the ideal legs that The Bulle-
tin habitually sees in its dreams.
** * *
About six months from now, if present
arrangements hold good, the Brough Comedy
Co. dissolves, and Mr. and Mrs. Brough give up
management in Australia and depart England-
wards. The interval is to be spent playing fare-
well seasons in Brisbane, Sydney, Adelaide, and
the principal towns of Maoriland. Considering
the splendid record the Broughs have put up in
twelve years’ of continuous toil, everyone who has
ever seen them on the stage will regret their de-
parture. The Bulletin moves that an influential
deputation, with Dan O’Connor carefully ex-
cluded, should wait upon them, and ask them to
put a name to any inducement which will lead
them to stay, and that the inducement, if it is
anything within reach, should be put up forth-
with. Even the sacrifice of QuongTart would be
justifiable in such a case.
** * *
Westmacott’s drama, at Sydney Royal, is
getting along as well as could be expected con-
sidering that six theatres are struggling to make a
simultaneous crust in Sydney, and Howden’s
highly deserving bit of scenery in Actl. —which is
a highly deserving piece of scenery even when Hans
Phillips interferes with it by striking impossible
attitudes with a gun on a roof—has a good share
in the success. The management announces, how-
ever, that the “Shamrock and the Rose” will
ensue on Saturday.
The “ Strange Adventures of Miss Brown
is about at the end of its long run, and after to-
morrow Thornton and the muddy policeman will
break up the respectability of a select academy
no more. “ The Bookmaker ” goes up on Satur-
day.
** * *
The Electric Parlor at 162 Pitt-street,
Sydney, is one of the most comprehensive shows
just now going. It includes a whole collection of
kinetoscopes. an x-ray apparatus in which the
visitor can in spect his bones at the shortest notice,
phonographs, vitascope, and the inevitable cine-
matographe. The latter includes a very large
array of pictures, among them a prize-fight, a
bloodthirsty unpleasantness between two roosters,
an express-train, the whirlpool of Niagara, sundry
bits of scenery, leg-shows plain and colored,
camels, a breezy disturbance in a public-house,
and many other things. A very general desire for
bones centres round the x-ray apparatus, but to
those for whom bones are not everything the prize-
fight and the bloodthirsty roosters have much to
recommend them.
** * *
“ A Woman’s Reason,” which deserved to
flourish exceedingly, scored only a moderate suc-
cess at Melbourne Bijou, wherefore the Brough Co.
has already started a series of farewell revivals.
“Sowing the Wind” takes the lead in this interest-
ing procession; on Saturday (13) “Niobe” will
come along for three nights, followed by O. Wilde’s
“Woman of No Importance,” and for further
particulars see next issue. The season finishes on
April 2nd, when the Broughs, according to latest
announcements, will take a final leave of Mel-
bourne, and shake the dust of a fickle-minded
city from their restless feet. Some final ap-
pearances in Brisbane and Sydney are to
happen in due course; then a Maoriland tour,
concluding with a disbandment of the company.
In the meantime Australia would do well to beam
beseechingly upon its Robert Brough, that he may
be persuaded to say Au Revoir and not Good-bye.
** * *
“ The Prisoner of Zenda ” quits the
Princess’s on Friday night with a good business
record. On Saturday the Firm will stage “ Two
Little Vagabonds,” a play that is confidently ex-
pected to carry popular sympathies by storm. A
fortnight after the first production of “Two
Little Vags.,” at London Princess’s, prices were
doubled in obedience to the great law of supply
and demand, which exceptional circumstance
speaks for itself. Miss Florence Terriss and Miss
Marie Neilson make their Australian debut in the
name-parts, also the full strength of the “ Zenda ”
company will be brought to bear upon the new
show.
** * *
At the Royal they are continuing “The
Derby Winner ” regardless of changes at the other
houses, and Bland Holt’s broad comedy-smile
relaxeth not. TJp to now the last nights of that
sensational three-year-old haven’t been hinted at,
so the wicked Majah’s jockey is certain to take off
his jacket and accept a mount on the favorite
for another week or two. The Bulletin, by the
way, would like to remark that the Majah’s
jockey has no occasion for throwing in a statement
that he can “ ride the weight,” seeing that the
Epsom Derby is not a handicap race. But no
matter. Anything seems to be permissible in con-
nection with stage Derbies. Even the Majah’s
neglect to find another jockey, in place of the one
who slips him up at the last moment, makes no
apparent difference in the number of starters.
At Poverty Point.
“ Melba,” writes an Australian in England to The Bul-
letin, “ has suddenly given up her projected South
American trip and returned to Paris ; currently reported
that influenza is the cause, but if ‘ the whole truth and
nothing but the truth ’ were published, her more than
partial failure in German opera (Brunhilde) in the
‘ Fatherland ’ has more to do with this sudden cancelling
of dates than the ever-ready-to-fall-back-on influenza.
Nor is she on the list for Covent Garden this season ; her
demand for a higher ‘ screw ’ than was hers in former
years being promptly refused by the new management.
And yet there is no rising star to seriously challenge her
indisputable supremacy. So far, the only Australian
engaged for the opera is Lempriere Pringle (Hobart) as
prirno basso (he has seceded from the Carl Rosa Co. and
is now on his return journey from a very successful
American tour with Albani and Co.).
The petite daughter of a Melbourne racing magnate,
who was formerly disposed to worship Brownlow, the
baritone, seems now to have transferred some of her
idolatry to Ferris. Little incidents like this are rather
comforting to middle-aged non-professionals, by the
way. Brough’s leading juvenile is a curatish-looking
youth of something more or less than 40.
Some of the “ widest ” men seem to be the most suscep-
tible to theatrical glamor. Directly after the “Zenda”
company arrived in Melbourne a well-known local medico
went into ecstacies over the witching beauty (as he judged
it) of one of the lady principals. And yet a cold world,
viewing the vision off the stage, finds her merely good-
looking. The doctor fell a victim to glamor.
Most people, The Bulletin ventures to say, consider
Lauri’s get-up in “Matsa” (especially the , back view
thereof) as brutally and unpardonably vulgar, and regret
to see so talented a comedian in such a rig.
London, when the mail left, was still awaiting the
arrival of Lalla Miranda and Mary Boucieault (said to be
Melba No. 2) from “ Gay Paree,” but still “ thej’ come
not.” Miss Boucieault, according to a private letter,
entertains not like an embryo prima donna, but like one
who has already won thousands from a generous public.
Arthur Deane, the Sydney baritone (writes a Bulletin
correspondent from London), having finished his tour
with the “Merry Madcap” Co., is looking for “some-
thing good,” and is apparently so satisfied that his time
will come that he has refused to sign on with Carl Rosa
for ’9B. Unfortunately for him, the baritone market at
present is very congested.

Cecchi, who died last week of heart-disease, was the
original music-master of Melba. He was never much of a
tenor, nothing so good as flutey little Coy. It is a sur-
prise to hear he was an architect of skill, and, working in
Reed and Barnes’s office, responsible for some of the
elegance of Melb. Public Library. His standing grievance
was that his “seexyaire” tuition of Melba was ignored.
