Paradis: “Knowles, like every genuine comedian, is
tortured with an idea that the public fancy him a one-
part man, or no part at all, merely a buffoon in his one
droll rig. Sothern got melancholy over the interminable
run of ‘ Dundreary.’ So Jefferson with ‘Rip,’ and ‘Fritz’
made Emmet drunk. Macready feared, at one time, he
would be condemned to act Rob Roy.”
W. F. Hawtrey, of the new “ Zenda ” combination, is a
brother of the Hawtrey, now managing the Comedy
Theatre, who made a fortune out of “ The Private
Secretary.” Miss Elliott Page comes from Hawtrey’s co.
“ Two Little Larrikins,” the melodrama in which Hilda
Spong is now playing the tearful heroine in London, is the
most loose-jointed play the writer ever saw. The way in
which burglars saunter in and out of drawingrooms while
the owners are present, the manner in which ambulances
and other effects appear on the stage without request-or re-
ference of any kind, and the mode in which the characters
meet and disappear without one word of explanation in the
text, is simply astounding.
Billy Walshe sang at the London Lyric Club on Sunday
evening, 10th January (writes a Bulletin correspondent),
when he had for a colleague on the programme the idol
of the halls, Marie Lloyd. Among the audience were
Henri Kowalski, Signor Billgreeno Verdi and Max Rown,
whom Frances Saville is not going to divorce, after all.
Bach’s Passion Music is underlined at the Melb. Angli-
can Cathedral. Half-a-crown will be charged under guise
of subscription. This indicates that full oratorio per-
formances are to be given, as in some English Cathedrals.
Indeed, the place don’t pay.
Signor Schiavi, the eminent bassoonist, has left on a
hurried visit to Europe, but will shortly resume the
conductorship of North Shore Orchestral Society. Signora
Schiavi is an excellent ’cellist, and is a great favorite at
sacred concerts in Sydney.

S.S. : “ The society beauties introduced as spectacular
effects at Melb. Princess’s fail, apparently because of their
niggardliness with the greasepaint—the result of inex-
perience probably, or possibly arising from their fear of
injuring the original complexion. As it is, the much-
photoed Mrs. Morris would make a very poor picture
beside many of the Firm’s chorus-girls—Maie Saqui, for
instance, or Minnie Arkaline. I see a Bulletin corres-
pondent ascribes the appearance of the “ sassiety ” ladies
on the stage to overweening vanity. True, probably.
One I know to be extremely vain, having seen her in all
her store clothes pass a large mirror without even a
glance at the bewitching reflection. Always a sign of
egotistical self-satisfaction in woman, this.”
The passengers on the Orizaba, which brought Ferris
(of the Brough Oo.) from ’Ome, tell amusing stories of the
aesthetic jeune premier. He affected a dressing-gown of
purple velvet, lined with the most becoming heliotrope
quilted satin. A very swagger grey felt hat he wore was
the admiration of all the women, and the envy, hatred,
and malice of the men. This, however, was swept off his
curly head into the blue deep but three days after leaving
London. But, to give the masher his due, Ferris shows
but an indifferent face to the many bright eyes that take
aim at him from below the footlights.
Our acquaintance, Harry Kellar, is conjuring cock-of-
the-walk in America, wizard Herman having just died,
after making and spending fabulous sums, like Bouci-
cault. Curious heredity. Hermann, senior, was a rich
German merchant, with a natural, irrepressible sleight of
hand, which he detested. It ran all through the family.
How the H’s dominate in conjuring : Hermanns, Houdin,
Heller, Haselmayer, Hercat, Hertz—and Handerson, says
’Enery ’Awkins.

Henry Tinney, director of the orchestra at Frascati’a
restaurant, London, dropped dead the other night whilst
conducting a selection from “ Carmen.” The old musical
name calls up memories of H. J. Byron’s burlesque in
which one of the Greek gods returned home from a h’ofi.
day to find his goddess engaged in high saasiety jinks
regardless of expense. She had engaged Coote and
Tinney’s band. Quoth the god—“ And Coote and Tinney
too 1 Why, coute qui coute, I could keeck-oot the lot."
The first-night success of Bracy’s production of the
“Beggar Student,” at Sydney Lyceum, is more pro-
nounced in view of the facts that not six of the cast were
experienced before the footlights, that the co. and Braev
were utter strangers five weeks before, that many of the
chorus and ballet had never previously appeared upon
the stage.
Whelan, of the Bracy co., a Melb. youth, promises to be-
come an excellent mimic. His imitation of R. G. Knowles
quite fetched the first-night audience. He never appeared
professionally before, except in Westralia, but he has been
an amateur for a long time. R. G. Knowles, by the way,
opines that nobody in the world can imitate him.

Julia Spencer, of Bracy’s Opera Co., is from Melbourne.
She first- appeared eight or nine years ago with Williamson
and Musgrove, and played Ariel in “ The Tempest.” She
did a twelve-months tour in the States with Marie
Tempest in comic-opera.
Miss Mabelle Darley, in the trying part of Laura in “The
Beggar Student,” made a great hit on her first night with
a Sydney audience. She is a Melb. girl who has been
singing for a couple of years, mostly as an amateur in
“ Giroflb-Girofla,” “ Marjorie,” “ Pinafore.” Until Satur-
day night she had never appeared in a prominent part.
She was deathly nervous when singing “ Joy and Grief";
her extended hands trembled like leaves. But she charmed
the hearts of the audience, and delighted Mr. and Mrs.
Bracy.
Carl Hertz opened his Maoriland tour with a great bang
at Auckland. The Opera House was crowded half-an-
hour before starting time, and the show caught on un-
mistakeably, according to all accounts. It is very little
more than a one-man show either —Orchestra, Hertz;
more Orchestra, Cinematographe ; more Orchestra, more
Hertz and Gorsavethequeen ! Manager Lohr and the
Wizard have been exchanging smiles by cable.

the theatrical paradise.

church. Ex-pastor Jones is now prohibited from

The Earl of Kilmorey, now visiting Aus-
tralia, was in Sydney, as Lord Newry, when the
Duke of Edinburgh was shot by O’Farrell. Newry
was one of the Duke’s party, and, like many
others at the time, believed an Irish insurrection
at hand. S.M. Herald, by the way, does not say
whether he has been accorded the ‘ ‘ well-deserved
honor of honorary membership of the Union Club.”
What their own constituencies think of
certain members was hinted at in the Vic. Federal
Convention elections. Essendon gave to two other
candidates a greater number of votes than it gave
to Deakin; Bogong preferred one candidate (the
Premier) to Isaacs ; Richmond preferred three to
Trenwith ; East Bourke Boroughs, three to Berry
and ten to Hume Cook; Geelong, three to Hig-
gins ; Hawthorn, four to Murrain Smith ; Maldon,
three to M‘lntyre ; Williamstown, seven to Styles;
Ballarat West, seven to Vale ; and Mornington,
eighteen to L. L. Smith. Several of these facts,
although not necessarily all, point significantly to
weak seats, and will, no doubt, bring forward
aspiring successors at next general election. Pea-
cock and Turner alone topped the poll in their
own preserves. And so it was in N.S. Wales,
where severalcandidatesavowedly only entered for
the purpose of seeing how they stood, as compared
with other men, in the opinion of certain con-
stituencies.

Messrs. Pitt, Son and Badgery, Ltd., have added
to their well-known stock and station agency business
that of wool and produce salesmen. Mr. Edward N. Row,
one of the big leading minds in his calling, will conduct
the new branch. The"firm neither directly nor indirectly
will entertain any interest in produce committed to its
care except that of realising the highest price, and
buyers are to understand that they will compete only
with legitimate competitors. The big convenient stores
on Circular Quay and Macquarie-street are now open for
the reception of consignments, from a few horns or a bag
of cow-hair to 1000 hides or bales of wool. The firm will
prosecute the new branch of business with all its well-
known capacity and energy.*

better than for February, ’96, and for the first eight
months of the financial year £125,371 better than for the
corresponding period last year. Queensland revenue for
February was £12,000 less than for February, ’96 ; Wes-
tralia’s was £153,000 more—in other words, nearly double.

Melbourne Board of Works pot off its .£750,000
3J per cent, loan locally at £lOO 9s. Bd. This is by far the
best that the Board has done up to date, either in Mel-
bourne or London. The question now is—can the Govern-
ment pet off its big conversion loan in the same market
at 3 per cent, and realise par. ? One thing is about cer-
ta'n ; no Australian province could float a 3 per cent,
loan in London now' at par. It must be done in Australia
or not at all. Melbourne is just now the better market of
the two ; Board of Works debentures sell in London at a
Erice which yields the buyer £3 11s. per cent. ; in Mel-
ourne the buyer is content with less than £3 10s.

Vi j. Supreme Court has formally sanctioned the
writi’ig-off, by the Commercial Bank of Australia, of
£1,2( 0,000 ordinary capital. This means that all the
capii U and reserves, as they were before the 1893 recon-
stru> tion—about £2,000,000 in all—have evaporated. The
allef ;d total private wealth of Victoria in the boom days
was about £400,000,000. This one institution alone
ma res a considerable hole in that beautiful calculation.
» * * *
To all appearance, most of the big brewing co’s
of Melbourne will have to go broke and the whole beer
business start again on a new basis. The boom, as usual,
is the cause of the trouble. In the good days the brewery
co’s bought up freehold and leasehold hotels wholesale,
with the idea that the great public thirst w'as immortal.
Now the money paid for goodwill is all gone ; the few
pubs, that still have a genuine goodwill attached to them
are more than counterbalanced by the ones which no
customer in his senses would* take away unless he was
paid removal expenses. In many oases people have to be
paid a salary for remaining in charge of the desolate pub.,
which the brewery isdoath to abandon lest it should some
day become of value again. Taxes have to be paid on the
freehold pubs., and rent and taxes on the leasehold ones ;
and interest on the money borrowed to buy them—which
money sometimes runs into hundreds of thousands-is an-
other' awful item. Also there are repairs; and, against
all this, many of the places only pay wages—some not
even that. If some of the big breweries would abandon
several dozen hotels apiece and pay the landlords to take
several dozen more off their hands, they would be vastly
better off ; but vyhen they took the alleged value of these
hotels out of the balance-sheet they would be visibly in-
solvent—at present they are only invisibly insolvent.
Meanwhile, some small new breweries, which own no
hotels and have no borrowed money to pay interest on,
are making profits, while the old lumbering concerns
lumber on to destruction and hopelessly hope that some-
thing will turn up. , , ,
Still another English fire-insurance co. starts
business in Australia. The newest arrival is the Eastern
Counties Co., a respectable, second-class institution which
hangs out in the grimy provincial town of Hull. This
makes the several-dozenth foreign fire office doing busi-
ness in Australia, and still the absentee-tax cometh not.
New foreign insurance co.’s, English, Scotch, German,
Austrian, Swiss, Italian, Japanese, &c., are now opening
in this country at the rate of about one a month, and the
situation grows interesting.

Mines Minister Smith says N.S.W. Mines De-
oartment was never before in so efficient a state as now.
&ow can any department be efficient while it is controlled
by a man who knows nothing of mining, and is merely a
weak joint in the tail of the Govt. ? That has, except
perhaps in the days of Copeland, always been its un-
happy position. It'should be controlled by three (there
is safety in numbers) highly-paid expert commissioners
who, if they attended to business should profitably spend
every second of their time. The Minister now talks of
consolidating the N.S.W. mining law. The three com-
missioners would probably introduce a new and simple
code preserving the few good principles of Australian
mining law and adopting the chief features of the U.S.
mining law.

Ex-directors of the Overflow ridicule the pub-
lished statement that £lOO,OOO of ore is already in sight.

• • * •
Alick Forrest said at the recent Perth mining-
conference that W.A. Parliament would never listen to
the proposal to give freeholds after a certain amount of
work had been done, thus relieving mine-owners from all
obligations of labour; if such a proposal were carried it
would mean that half their miners would have to leave
the province for want of work. Another delegate, Kerr,
was even more candid. He said the proposal was designed
in the interests of “boomsters;” but it was time the
British investor found out that gold was not too abundant
inWestralia. It was also time they admitted that the
day of reckoning between the British investors and
Westralia was coming. It was not far off now, and the
sooner it came the better. Yet the W.A. papers, in-
judicious enough to print this, continue to talk of another
greater London boom in March. The chairman (Dr.
Simon) was cunning enough to ask Kerr to confine him-
self to the question.
* * * *
The Coolgardie Gold Mining and Prospecting
Syndicate started in Westralia with a capital of £l50 —
Pearce, Brookman and, we think, C. De Rose being the
prospectors. A third of the money went in outfit, another
third had vanished by the time they reached Coolgardie,
and the balance soon followed. The capital of the syndi-
cate was increased two or three times. Now the
market-value of the properties pegged out by these pros-
pectors for the syndicate amounts to over five millions.
Thev include the Great Boulder, Lake View, Associated
Mines, Ivanhoe, Lake View South and Extended, Cool-
gardie Mint, and Boulder No. 1. Several big distributions
of cash and shares have taken place—chiefly shares—and
those held by the co. to-day are valued at £70,884.

* * * *
Clarke and Co., of Melb., issue monthly the
best of all the brokers’ circulars. It gives far more
accurate information of Westralian mines than news-
papers—which, when one thinks of it, is not saying much.
Like most other things, including the writer perhaps, it
has its weaknesses. It says of the Golden Bar : “ This
mine of disappointments and bad management in the
past will yet, under able guidance, astonish those who are
never tired of decrying Coolgardie.” Areally good mine
will stand a lot of bad management. Coolgardie has
decried itself. More British gold has been sunk in its
mines than will probably ever be got out of them. Its
history is briefly—unanimity of liars in the working of
mining jobs. The Lord forbid, for the sake of the mining
industry, that its history will ever be repeated in any
other part of the world. In that history the Golden
Bar has nothing to brag about, and it will take more gold
than is likely to come out of the mine to redeem it from
the degradation into which it has sunk.
Coolgardie : “ A fine slug of pure gold, weighing
530 z., has been brought in from Mt. Margaret; also ; one
from Siberia weighing 210 z.” The world is not taking a
mad interest in Coolgardie slugs at present— a very good
thing for the world. t

* * * *
Frank Nicolas was banqueted the night before
leaving London, and, of course, made another W.A.
boom speech. He said (as reported in a London
paper) the depression in Westralia was due to over-
capitalisation and insufficiency of working capital.
Some people had thought want of gold might have
had something to do with it, but that, is a mistake.
According to Mr. Nicolas, the water difficulty is all a
myth. To prove it, he refers to a time when they
had a flood. He predicts failure for the W.A. Govt,
scheme to pump 5,000,000 gallons a day to Mt. Burgess.
The Govt, of Vic. once had a similar scheme for the mines
of Bendigo, but with the pitiful result that before the
water arrived the mines had plenty. Like other of
Nicolas’s statements, this is incorrect. The Cobban water-
scheme was to supply all the towns between Malmesbury
and Bendigo, and not the mines. Every goldfield in
the world with a yearly rainfall of less than XOin. has a
water difficulty; and all the gas by Nicolas and other
boomsters will not remove it. The writer notes that his
company—the Coolgardie Goldfields (pW.)— ts
50 000 shares for working capital, but what this has to do
with his sanguine speeches the present writer does not
pretend to know. » * *
Somebody has handed writer a copy of the Hit-
or-Miss mine prospectus, an awful document in the light
of the cruel battery truth. Many hundreds of tons of
4dwt stone have been crushed, the battery has shut up,
shares have shut down, exemption has been secured by a
strike and, altogether, poor Bull is in a pitiable condition.
This prospectus foreshadowed quite a different ending.
J O Oxley reported :—“ Upwards of 50 shafts and cos-
teens have been sunk to various depths up to 100 ft and
the reef proved for a distance of two miles, the w ulth in
many places being over 20ft., and even up to 50ft, the ore
being of high grade. I estimate the ore to yield lOoz. to
the ton, the gold being free and evenly distributed.
Other glowing statements were made by other people, but
none came up to Oxley for emphasis. Oxley was also the
vendor rnd promoter, and fixed the price Bull had to pay
— v i z -t 13) 000, £45,000 in cash, the balance in fully-pail
shares ; £40,000 working capital was also put up to work
this great mine. Extracts from newspapers were also pub-
lished, one taken from the Financial News : —“ Last
week the owners dollied IOOOoz., and lOOoz. were cleaned
up from one shot.” The W.A. Review The Hit-or-
Miss will probably prove the finest mine in Western Aus-
tralia.” Oxley has come out from London to see to things.
The manager resigned, disgusted with the whole business.
Unlike Oxlev, he had nothing to do with the float. As
soon as his back was turned, a circular was issued in
London saying the failure was entirely due to mismanage-
ment, a clean sweep had been made, and there was 20,000
tons of rich ore in sight. It also happens that a few days
later the manager’s exposure reached London. Oxley
should now vindicate himself by keeping the 20-head
battery going on the 20 to 50 feet and lOoz. reef of free
gold and thereby produce 10,000 oz. a month. Why have
a strike for the sake of 10s. a week under these circum-
stances?
* • • *
The Bulletin is open to do business with any
man or firm that has preserved intact copies of the pro-
spectuses issued in London of alleged Westralian gold-
mines. This paper has received, as it expected, so much
condemnation when it stood alone in the criticism of
W.A. “ wild-cats ” that it wants to know how it will come
out on the average—whether it has to stick another
feather in its tail, or apologise to the army of Westralian
boomsters who are at this moment in great distress, and
want all the consolation they can get.
* * • *
Westralian newspapers, the writer is sorry to
see, are singing quite a different tune now the boom is
past and the harvest therefrom is over. Thus Geraldton
Express : “ The booming of worthless properties by im-
pecunious newspaper-proprietors and mining-experts is
detrimental to genuine mining investment.” You don’t
say! “The evil, though it exists on the Murchison, is
not so bad as in the southern fields, where over 1000
leases, held in collusion by jumping syndicates, have been
forfeited, and upwards of 70 on the Murchison, to say
nothing of the utterly worthless properties successfully
placed in England, all of which will assist to knock the
bottom out of bogus mining ventures. W.A., as a legiti-
mate-field for investment, is discounted by the number of
swindles that have been perpetrated by unscrupulous ad-
venturers and their allies, corrupt pressmen and mining-
experts.” It gives the name of the paper which recently
exposed an attempted swindle, and the name of the mine,
but also alludes to the other duffers the same paper con-
tinues to write up, and naturally wants to know why one
particular mine is singled out for exposure.

The few great mines of Kalgoorlie are never
allowed to suffer a week for the want of newspaper advt.
The long wire re the Boulder lode at the 300 ft. level is a
fair sample : “ The most important find in the colony for
many months.” “ Lode shows here greater signs of per-
manency than anywhere else.” “It settles for ever all
doubts about the soft formations living in hard country—
the lode here is as hard as cast steel.” “It is 6ft. wide
and is worth 3soz. The ore is sulphide, but its richness
is due to telluride.” “ At the 300 ft. level the lode is much
wider and richer than at the 200 ft. level.’’

rent. As before stated, there will be more leases forfeited

March. Bull this month, at any rate, will not resemble

With over 2000 leases forfeited this year, hun-
dreds of others being thrown up, hundreds of exemp-
tions, the gold-producers’ mines full-handed, unemployed
agitations in Coolgardie, people still coming in every
week, and all the hospitals of the country filled to over-
flowing, the Forrest Govt., which has long lived on jam,
must now take some brimstone and treacle.

Westralian gold-producers : Lake View Consols
(month), 1694 tons, 42990 z. Great Boulder (fortnight),

Bull collectively, presided over by Remember-
Me-No-More Robinson, demands a reduction of W.A.
labor conditions, a permanent title when a substantial
sum has been spent on gold leases, security for costs from
jumpers, and value for improvements in the case of for-
feiture. He will probably get it, too ; but, if he wants
another boom, he must make it. The only boom now on
is in exemptions. , « *
Wilmot Extended, Gympie, 112 tons for 20740 z.
If this happened with any Westralian mine what a song
we should have about it! It is the new, never the old
mine, that creates sensation, and the public knows the
vagaries of the Wilmot and Ellen Harkins mines. They
were opened some 20 years ago by working miners who
for some years were impoverished by calls and ridiculed
for their folly. This month—March—in ’B4, the Extended
struck it mighty rich, putting through 470 tons for
10 9440 z. It paid the record div. of £1 a share, £36,000.
The same year it also crushed 3775 tons for 28,0500 z.
No 1 South Wilmot put through 1100 tons for 13,8280 z. ;
Ellen Harkins, 2177 tons for 14,7280 z. Ellen’s first crush-
ing was 70 tons, yielding exactly 70oz. to the ton, and 70
times 70—49000 z. The Wilmot Extended also did some-
thing in seventies—paying £70,000 in divs. from the pro-
duct of 70ft. of stoping. These and many other equally
rich things of Gympie were got without “ foreign capital,
and their getting awakened no boom in Bull.^
Valuation of Charters Towers registered co’s,
£2 220 000. Divs. over calls show a profit of 7i per cent.,
and if calls are allowed as capital expenditure it is equal
to 11J4 per cent. Let Bull compare this with his mining
investments in Westralia, and weep.
* * • *
The cyanide patent as amended in Queensland
does not expire till 1901.
The general absence of fire-damp in Queensland
coal-mines has led colliery-proprietors there to trust to
natural ventilation, although the Q. Act is framed on the
English Act of ’72, and provides for “ adequate ventila-
tion.” There are no return air-courses, and the inevitable
presence of carbonic oxide is slowly and cumulatively un-
dermining the constitutional and physical powers of Q.
coal-miners, and inflicting noticeable secondary injury on
their progeny. Yet the Mining Laws Commission recently
appointed by the Nelson Government does not include
one member who has the slightest inner or practical know
ledge of the working of Q. coal-mines, although the
leader of the Opposition, Glassey, was a working coal-
miner. The most glaring faults lie in the non-administra-
tion of the existing law, and to this point more especially
the attention of the oommission is directed.

Mt. Morgan, Australasia’s boss gold - mine,
seems to have settled down for a monthly production of
13,f1000z. from about 8000 tons of ore. When the works,
nowin progress, for treating still larger quantities of the
lower grade ore, are finished, the output will probably go
to and remain for some years at 16,0000 z. a month
Truly, a marvellous mine.

Brief, and to the point, report on some Charters
Towers mines, from a mining-man whose opinion the
writer values : “ Kelly’s Queen Block—Straight shaft
1,450 ft. deep, and now sinking on the underlie ; some rich
stone in the sink. In the east drive there is about 18in.
of fair stone ; 250 tons from drives crushed for loz. 12dwt.
yield. I think this mine should do well. Victoria G.M.A.
—Reef runs from a foot to 15in., but goes in and out a
good deal. They nave a large block of ground between
No. 2 and 3 levels west, and a good block along the Band
of Hope boundary, at the bottom of the shaft towards the
Papuan, where some rich stone was got. Also a big block
of good stone on the east of the shaft, between No. 8 and
10 levels, and down to the bottom level, No. 12. This
seems a new make from the Victoria and Caledonia Block.
The latter should be a good mine, but I have not been
down yet. The Vic. G.M.A. is by no means exhausted, as
some people suppose. The Phoebe is one of the pet mines
here, and, though of small acreage, it is good. Clark’s
Worcester and Victory should prove a steady div.-
payer. Clark, who controls half and owns one-third,
in the face of much opposition got the tailings of last
crushing saved at a cost of i 5 and sold them to a cyanide
co. for £llO. Seems curious that the generally level-
headed mining-men of the Towers object to saving their
tailings. They are married to their mills, and don’t want
to admit that they lose gold, but the fact that there are
about 30 small cyanide plants treating tailings from the
creeks below the batteries, and all apparently doing well,
should be proof enough. Three miles below one mill is a
plant treating a deposit of tailings. The circumstances
of the big explosion in the Brilliant Deep Levels, in respect
to which there is a big reward offered, are suspicious.
This mine has a fancy chimney-stack, picked out in blue
and white bricks, with a masonic emblem and other deco-
rations, but so far has not struck much gold. East
Mexican : two levels from the Brilliant are up to the
boundary of this mine, and from the lower one they are
rising up on 3ft. of good stone. In the level above they
have a good reef, which goes in and out a bit, and last
week got some fine specimens in it. The mine is greatly
handicapped by having to work on sufferance of the
Brilliant. They have only two men where the rich speci-
mens came from, and the air is as hot as Sheol. They are
going to haul by-and-by through the Brilliant Block shaft,
over quarter of a mile away, till they are connected with
their own shaft. At the price (£lO,OOO for the mine), the
shares are good enough.”
* • * *
Coal costs £2 per ton landed at the furnaces,
Charters Towers, and that price is prohibitive. The
Transcontinental Railway would take coal from Clermont
to that field for about 255., and this would enable several
mines to join the div.-list. Something will soon have to be
done, as there is not a respectable stick left within 20
miles of the Towers.

After weary years the indomitable John Clark,
of Clark’s Brilliant and Worcester mine, C. Towers, has
been rewarded with a 3oz. crushing for 500 tons, with
alluring prospects ahead. Clark is supposed to hold
45,000 of the shares.

» * * *
Bendigo mine-managers complain in their Asso-
ciation meetings that they are the worst-paid managers
in Australia. The secretary lately said that some of them
were not getting £4 a week. Entirely true. But, per
contra, it is claimed that, as most of the Bendigo mines
are directed by Bendigo boards, the managers don’t re-
quire to know anything of finance or have any business
ability. They are' it is claimed, little more than under-
ground bosses. This, at any rate, has been the view
almost invariably adopted by the Broken Hill directors,
who have, with one or two notable exceptions, fought shy
of Vic. managers as general managers for this reason,
while welcoming them as shift-bosses.

SOCIETY
COOLALTA
Three months hence the 60th anniversary
of Victoria’s accession falls due, and no one has
fairly started to organise any great Australian
grovel for the occasion. Someonte has suggested
that the entrance to Sydney Harbor should be
christened the Quern's Gate, and someone else
has proposed that a huge subscription be started
and a big charity be founded ; also, it has been
proposed that three pictures of Australian scenery
be painted at a cost of £ 1000 and presented to Her
Gracious. These are about all the ideas up to
date, and they suggest that loyalty is suffering
badly from ‘ ‘ that tired feeling. ”
The Bulletin takes this opportunity to pro-
pound its idea of what a real, useful, healthy
grovel would be like. It advises that on the day
of celebration the whole able-bodied country popu-
lation should turn out and remove the stumps off
the roads. Also, that in the cities the police-force
should be permanently doubled, so that the larri-
kin who heaves road-metal will have twice as
SO ENGLISH , YOU KNOW.
Very gilded personage enters fashionable
tea-room.
“I — er—got some rathah nice scones the
othah day : have you any left? ”
“Yes, sir.”
"How much are they?”
"A penny each.”
“ Oh — well, I’ll — er—have two.”
“ Won’t you take three for threepence,
sir? ”
"Eh! — er—vewy well. Will you—er—
send them — er—to the Grand Im-
perial Metropolitan hotel for me? ”
good a chance of being run in as at pre-
sent. Then, that a new standing army
should be started, and every able-bodied
male convicted of larrikinism for the third
time should be run into it for three years,
for his own mental and physical reformation and
the general comfort of the community. It pro-
poses that all the banana-skins should be cleaned
off the streets, and anybody throwing more skins
on them be fined <£2o ; that the police should
begin to have less difficulty in finding unlawful
gambling-shops, seeing that almost everybody in
Sydney and Melbourne can find 20 any day while
the force hardly discovers one in six months ; that
Wexford-street, Sydney, should be burned to the
ground ; and that the tote, and Tattersall’s sweep,
and the drink-traffic should be made State mono-
polies. Further, Max Hirsch should be bound
over in his own recognisances to be in a calm frame
of mind, and to be strictly accurate in his Foreign-
trade statistics for three months ; and Premier
Reid should be compelled to take healthy exercise
by Act of Parliament; and Duncan Gillies be sent
into permanent exile ; and Parson Madgwick be
compelled to present an illuminated address to the
Cardinal.
It is also hereby suggested that every Australian
should be required to wash that day and have his
hair cut; that then and thereafter every tall hat
which appears at the theatre should be confiscated
and jumped upon by a duly-authorised function-
ary ; that the N.S.W. line from Cobar to Broken
Hill, and the Vic. line from Bairnsdale to Eastern
Gippsland should be finished by public subscrip-
tion ; and that the Melbourne Harbor Trust should
spend the whole day on the Yarra, while some one
stirs up the black and geririy stream with long
poles to bring out the true inwardness of its
fragrance. The Bulletin is further of opinion
that Crick, John Norton. Freeman Kitchen,
Attorney-General Want, and Meagher should go
out fishing together on that day of good feeling
and general reconciliation, and that the Queens-
land National Bank should be definitely closed
up, and North Queensland should rise in general
insurrection, and get separation or burst. And if,
in addition to all this, arrangements could be
made to reduce the price of gas, and every
gaunt ’bus-horse got an extra feed of oats, and
every preacher in Australia was called upon
to find security that he would thereafter deliver
his sermon in an ordinary voice and not in a
broken-hearted wail; and it was provided by law
that the sixpenny peaches inside the shop shall be
at least half as large as the threepenny ones in
the window—then this paper submits that it would
be a useful and respectable celebration, and one
that the country might be proud of, and this
great nation could then go to the Zoo and have a
penny ride on the elephant with a light heart.
• • * *
A weird crowd in Adelaide, calling it-
self the Local Government Association of South
Australia, has decided to send the following
address to the Queen, congratulating her on her
Diamond Jubilee: “Your Majesty’s Australian
subjects have ever shown the deepest attachment
to your throne and person, and are now striving,
as a fitting crown to the glories of your august
reign, to inaugurate the federal union of the Aus-
tralian colonies.” Rather !
The four winds blow;
Tides rise and fall;
The sun doth glow
Imperial ;
Potatoes grow
Not quite so small
This year as in past years they used to be
In honor of the Diamond Jubilee.
Night brings the morn,
And men awake,
And babes are born,
And measles take;
And we, forlorn,
Get stomach-ache
Through grovelling—for loyal worms are we—
In honor of Her Gracious’ Jubilee.

Good old Chancellor Brownless, of Melb.
University, has decided that the best way of cele-
brating the “ Diamond Jubilee ” is not to illu-
minate the streets, but to present H.M. with an
illuminated address —which would no doubt inter-
est her deeply if it ever chanced to meet her royal
eye. Dr. Brownless forgets that alighted street
illuminates the addresses of all the local inhabi-
tants.
* * * *
Judge Gibson, a potentate of N.S.W. Dis-
trict Court, appears to have put up a new prece-
dent lately, at Bingara. A prisoner was charged
with the theft of a tin of baking-powder, and the
jury brought him in guilty. Then the Judge
descended on him as follows (local paper report) :
Prisoner was sentenced to three years’ penal servitude ;
and, in passing sentence, his Honor remarked that he was
determined to deal with him as severely as possible, as he
(prisoner) had not allowed the magistrates in the lower
court to deal with his case, but had put the count ry to
the expense of £l9 9s. to bring the trial before him.
The Bulletin refrains from commenting on this
apparently monstrous judgment until it ascertains
whether the statements above quoted are based on
fact, or whether the whole story arises out of a
reporter’s nightmare.

The Adelaide Hospital row has sent 16
Adelaide medical students (including five ladies)
to Melb. Univ., and two to Sydney.
In the “old” days, jury-
men, on retiring to consider their
verdicts, were not allowed any
refreshments. On one occasion,
after a jury had been shut up
for some hours, the Sheriff came
to the presiding Judge, who
was very fond of his glass, and
told him the jury wanted some
water. “ Can I give it to them,
your Honor, since I have sworn
to give them neither meat nor
drink?” His Honor considered
a minute or two: “Well, yes,
you may give them some water ;
it certainly isn’t meat—and I
shouldn’t call it drink.”

* * *
Victorian law has formally decided tt
hang Quinn, the survivor of the attempted double
suicide at Albert Park. In all probability, how-
ever, it won’t hang him, but will let him off with
a long term of imprisonment; but either way his
fate should be a serious warning to all people who
resolve to die together. No man is a good enough
shot to feel quite sure that he can kill both him-
self and his wife even at short range, and when
two people who love each other decide to make a
romantic joint ending neither likes to batter the
other with a chair-leg after the shooting to make
quite sure that life is extincL Even if the man,
who generally supervises the'*suicide, makes quite
sure the woman is dead, he very seldom manages
to kill himself effectually, for he can’t batter him-
self fatally with a chair-leg ; Quinn, for example,
only blew one eye out and got into gaol. The
only sure device that this paper can think of is
for the romantic pair to chain and padlock them-
selves together and step overboard with lewt. of
ballast attached to them. If the water is deep
enough, and the chain doesn’t slip, and the pad-
lock holds, that is sure enough fo r all practical
purposes.

Crowned heads of Europe have always
given their royal patronage to Ed. Pinaud, of
Paris, but never so much as lately ; his perfumes
are now recognised as incomparably the best in
Europe. The biggest hits of Ed. Pinaud have
been “Violette Reine ” (quite different from
“ Rhine Violet ”), and “Preciosa,” the sweetest
and most delicate perfumes ever made ; in fact,
they are known everywhere as the quintessences
of the richest flowers grown in France, and are
sure to be rushed by everybody.*

* * * *
As illustrative of the effects of a godless
education system, a clergyman stated at Master-
ton (M.L.) the other day that he was visiting a
house in the country, when he knelt to have a few
words of prayer. The children thought from his
posture that he was desirous of a game of leap-
frog, and accordingly they sprang over his head in
the most approved style.
* * * »
An interesting duel is being fought out
just now in Brisbane re the leprosy question. The
parents of children attending a city school object
to the sister of a leprous boy now in the lazarette
being allowed to attend above-mentioned school.
The authorities, backed by medical opinion, say
there is no danger of contagion, and refuse, con-
sequently, to exclude the girl. The average parent,
however, who has noticed the leprous plague
spread in the wake of the imported Chow, doesn’t
care a rap for medical dicta, and says he will not
allow his child to attend the same school as the
relatives of lepers —and therefore a boycott on the
school is impending. And, really, it’s difficult to
avoid sympathising with the parent —and still
more with the unlucky girl ostracised for the un-
cleanness of her brother.

A Sydney paper whoops that female
drunks should be taken to the jug in cabs, and
not dragged along the streets by bold, bad police-
men. That paper ignores the financial side of the
question. What a time the taxpayer would have
if every lonely female could go driving in a cab
with a duck of a copper by simply getting drunk !

No Westralian paper publishes the fact
that the other day two of the principal hotels on
the Menzies were rushed by starving unemployed
who cleared each place of its contents, both liquid
and solb 1
Petticoats are heavily
represented in the Collie
(N.S.W.) district. Five
families there, living within
a radius of less than three
miles, are blessed as follows :
two with seven, one with
nine, another with five, and
one with eight girls.
# * *
A Vic. parson, who
spent the last year or so in
the outpost goldfields of
West nil ia, returned to Melb.
recently and was holding
forth on his adventures to
his old congregation. There
was a crowded house, mostly
female, and every little wit-
ticism was sustained with a
trickle of giggles. “My
friends,’’concluded the shep-
herd, “ I shall never forget
the sound advice given me
by our loved Bishop Goe
when I departed to the Wes-
tern land. He told me that
as far as possible I should
always conform to. the cus-
toms of the people among
whom I dwelt. And, friends,
many comforts did 1 derive
by following that advice (ap-
plause). Though, I daresay,
some of my congregation—
the ladies especially—(here
a little giggle) would have
been rather alarmed had
they seen me, for, friends,
I did as others did (laugh),
and took with me every-
where my black gin (cough),
my black gin (cough and
sensation in the congrega-
tion), my black gin -(sup-
pressed titter, which starts
the whole church into a roar
of laughter of five minutes
duration. At last it sub-
sides)—my black gin-bottle
full of water, friends, with
which to allay my thirst.”

“ Love is all very well in the sparsely-
populated rural districts.”—New Woman.
Jack Gulliver, the Pigmy King of ‘ ‘ Matsa, ”
is a native of Bendigo, Vic., and is 21 years old.
Out of a family of six children, Jack and one sister
are the only midgets; Jack stands 3ft. 7in. and
weighs four stone ; his sister, aged 12, is only as
high as his shoulder. The other children, like the
parents, are well set up; Jack’s 16-year-old
brother stands six feet; his father, an engineer,
weighs lljst. ; his mother weighs 14st. Jack
says : “ First let me tell you that J. C. Williamson
did not pick me up in a woful condition ; damn
it! that’s a reflection on my mother, and, although
I’m a stray goose at home, I feel that a boy’s best
friend is his mother. I’m a perfectly developed
man, though small, as you can see; I’m as active
as a cricket, and can carry twice my own weight—
I can carry Gus. Gregory across the stage. I’ve
ridden a bicycle for 14 years, and I can guide her
over a threepenny-bit full butt every time. Sydney
streets are bad, I find; my wheel twizzled right
round with me on a greasy patch yesterday. I’ve
been on the stage, off and on, 12 years, singing
comic songs, chiefly in Melb., Geelong, Ballarat,
Bendigo and Adelaide. I have always taught
myself my business—J.C.W. was the first to give
me instruction. I never spoke lines till I played
in ‘ Matsa ’. I notice the Herald says I’m vulgar;
that’s because on the stage I hug the girls round
the legs, I suppose ; give the Herald my inches
and I’d like to see the Herald more decorous.
On the stage I look very tall, but behind the
scenes the girls play catches with me. There’s a
lot of jealousy in the show business ; but what is
the good of one being jealous of the other ;
it can make the other’s encores no better
nor worse. I’m not frightened of anything
on the stage ; once I learn a thing you
can’t thump it out of me. I’m a big fisherman —
Goody and I intend to clear the harbour out.”
(If Jack gets a large schnapper on, the fish might
pull him overboard and eat him.) “Do you
observe how Goody’s theatre connection worries
him ; look how bald he is on top. I can play a
violin, accordeon, mandolin, concertina, tin-whistle
in more or less degree. I’ve a craze for collecting
curios—people call me a bit ratty. I’ve 1600 Vic.
stamps and 1000 foreign stamps; I’ve a, big box full
of stones, curious shaped tins, wee little sample
tins. Mother has threatened to tip the whole lot
into the dust-box —I say ! you had better not say
she is 14st. —never mind, though, it only shows
that it is not her fault that I’m 4st. I’ve refused
3s. 6d. for my collection of curios.”
* • * *
One of the quarantined passengers per
Nineveh explains how beautifully they manage
things in Melbourne, First he was allowed to
leave the vessel, whereupon he took himself and
his luggage and his alleged germs to a coffee
palace, and shed infection on 90 other lodgers.
Then he was re-captured, but the 90 other lodgers
whom he had germed were left at large. He was
removed in a cab, and presumably infected that
also, and then the contaminated vehicle was turned
loose. Then he was vaccinated and shoved into
another cab, and when he was done with it that
cab was also sent adrift to bestow infection on
other customers. He was next taken on board
the Nineveh, where he was required to sleep in
the ship’s bedding, not yet cleaned and fumigated,
and the nurse who attended the smallpox patients
sat next him at meals. Having gathered all the
germs he could off the nurse he was shifted to the
quarantine station, and when he had been there
two days—so that the germs he brought from the
Nineveh might get well into his bedding—he was
fumigated and disinfected. Then he slept a night
in the same bedding, so as to get a fresh supply of
germs. Next day the bedding was disinfected,
and then he slept in it some more, and shed upon
it the germs he had gathered off it the previous
night after he was disinfected himself. Under
which circumstances his opinions about the Melb.
quarantine arrangements are unfit for full publi-
cation.

Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that _they will
bo honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, March 9,1897.
Mt Dear Mtee—
Some strict olerios were shocked at the Mayoress,
Mrs. Ives, holding a reception on Ash Wednes-
day, but the godless majority present at the Town
Hall didn’t even know that that church-festival or
fast was proceeding, and ate passion-fruit, ices,
and other delicacies unwhipped of conscience.
The ante-room was adorned with the competition
statuary groups for the new markets, and the hall
itself wassplendidly decorated with exotic forestry,
and his Worship, in full and flowing official robes,
the Mayoress, and her married daughter shook
hands unweariedly with a thousand citizenesses,
who sat down hard on the nearest chairs with a
firm grasp of their parasols and a serious stare.
Among the distinguished visitors was a globe-
trotting newspaper-man, whose beard has been
whitened with 55 years of journalism, but the
editorial eye is mild and fatherly, befitting a
record of only two libels in a half-century, which
is his proud boast. Most of the local Nicest being
still out of town, the reception-audience, on the
whole, was suburban, and the gowning to match.
The Cardinal is doing well by the forthcoming
Australian Fair in presenting a 500-guinea
diamond necklace to be competed for by the
covetous faithful at a guinea raffle. The donor
guarantees the jeweller the value of the trinket,
which is raffled at a profit, the money over and
above the market-price going to the good of the
“cause.” If the full sum be not subscribed the
scheme falls through. Most jumbo presentations
to church cake-and-cushion scrambles are worked
in the same way. Prominent firms have re-
sponded generously to appeals from committees
for gifts, which is large-minded of them, as these
monster fairs are bad for local trade.
The crowd at Randwick at the March meeting
on Saturday was like the Spanish fleet in the play
—you couldn’t see it because it was not in sight.
Scattered spectators dotted the empty spaces of
the grand-stand, and the few women with con-
spicuous gowns had really a chance to show them
off, had there been anybody there to see them.
An “ off ” meeting like last week’s is not, as a rule,
illuminating in the way of dress innovations, but
two or three wearers of fashionable attire took a
last opportunity of displaying their summer fur-
belows. A mossy green silk, with a satisfactory
Bkirt hanging from a waist which could have worn
a man’s collar as a belt, was topped, off with
shoulder-elaborations of brocade and a vast and
velvet hat. A bride, formerly one of the belles
among Melb.’s Chosen, now settled here, aired
one of her many wedding-outfit frocks fashionably
corrugated with frills. A well-known dressy
woman successfully mixed red and purply-black.
The Wheelman’s Carnival, which began last
Thursday on the Cricket Ground, was attended
by thousands, who found an absorbing interest in
seeing the blue-clad Lesna circling round and
round, hanging on like a bull-dog to the hind-
wheel of his pacers. Among many women now
the bike is quite supplanting the gee-gee as a
draw, and instead of talking of “starting price”
or “odds on,” they earnestly discuss the merits of
“sprinters” and “stayers,” and “quints” or
“quads,” and “racing behind pace,” and much
other mysterious slang of the jigger and its ad-
herents. Frenchman-like, the hero of the day
showed how to be artistic, though athletic, and
had his pacing teams and machines all in cerulean
hue, to match his own neat racing rig, his sweater
finished off with a black band. In the 20-inile
race he looked aggravatingly cool among his over-
heated competitors and pacers, and his legs
worked with the regular,' monotonous beat of a
pair of piston-rods. The foreign apparel showed
up the local colors as shabby and frowsy, except
the glittering Bill Martin, who shone in yellow.
Among onlookers theatrical folk largely de-
corated the grand-stand, varied with numerous
priests. On the whole, it was by no means a
“ smart ” crowd. A popular music-hall singer
had a pretty blouse-skirt with black cross-bars,
and her newly-wedded husband hung about de-
votedly, as though he, too, were racing behind
pace.
“ Miss Brown ” was visited on election-night
by the Maoriland Premier and party, who, after
a few days in Sydney, left by Saturday’s Welling-
ton boat. The Seddon family on its travels
has been taking things as they come, as every-
day citizens do, with a refreshing absence of the
pompous Premier - on - tour element. The hon.
Richard himself (despite the sneers of the S.M.
Herald) is a good example of the practical
Premier —“ the burly, hard-headed, heavy-fisted
man who fights his way to the front.” Mrs.
Seddon is a comfortable matron with a shrewd,
motherly face and a strong belief in the value of
female suffrage. Miss Seddon is like her father,
and has a local reputation for good looks.
At the latest Nice wedding at Moss Vale, where
Miss Laura Osborne was married to Police-In-
spector-General Fosbery’s nephew, there was a
most elaborate bridal-cake with the orthodox
cupids and orange-blossom in sugar-icing. This
glittering confection was entirely made by the
bride’s family, and besides being glacially beauti-
ful to behold, was cake right through. Much
shop-made wedding show-bread is a hollow fraud,
consisting of six inches of sweetened plaster to
two inches of alleged plum-duff.
“ Little Gulliver,” the dwarf attraction of
“Matsa,” was on the Manly boat one day last
week, dressed in tall silk hat and miniature
“street-sweeper” frock coat, and proved a sensa-
tion to troops of small boys, who whooped three
cheers for him as the ferry left Manly pier. On
Gulliver’s first appearance at rehearsal with the
“Matsa” Co., Millie Young chucked him under
the chin with “ Hullo, little boy.” “ Hullo your-
self,” growled the dwarf in that rumbling growl
which his audience finds so comical, and Millie
gave a surprised start at the deep basso proceeding
from such a tiny body. Before J.C.W. engaged
him he used to appear at Melb. Temperance Hall
sing-song—salary ten shillings a week, “ when he
got it. ”
As the Beggar Student, H. Bracy, though not
looking as young as he did when he was younger,
still gives the same lightsome skin, ami ---.rs
some trim floral-patterned coats, and silver round
his pretty little footwear. The chorus girls also
wear some fur, to give them a Polish air, and they
sing brightly, but I am afraid the public is not too
sweet just now on comio opera in costume —what
audiences like to see are the latest modes on the
soprano and the newest naughtiness in the plot.
In “The Beggar Student ” the blue is in the cos-
tumes, and not in the situations. Nina Osborne
sports azure tights as the cornet, as do her fellow-
officers ; Mabelle Darley’s first frock is blue and
tinselly, but for her wedding the heroine dons a
badly-cut white satin, and has her hair hung in
curls over her face in the old, old stage-terrier
style. A light soprano with tresses au naturel
would fill a long-felt want on the boards.
Australian sassiety federated years ago—an
event in Nice circles thrills the “ toney ” from
Townsville to Tas. Witness the latest important
wedding in Slowbart. The guests hailed from
many centres, and the function was gushed over
in the social columns of five provinces. The
bridegroom had a line in the word-painting —an
innovation which will spread—“he looked very
handsome in his smart get-up.” In this case, the
admiration of the pen-woman had cause in the
subject’s pose as an Adonis, before he was regene-
rated by a tailor-made evangelist and veiled his
manly beauty with a beard.
At another fashionable hitching-up the bride-
groom’s attire was chronicled even to his yellow
buttonhole-flower —a marigold. The floral lan-
guage spoke truly then, as the bride was richly
gilt.
An Australian woman has been booming as a
palmist in London, and adroitly acquired money
at a trade which is tabooed by the police as coming
under “the vag.” Palmistry, as well as other
hanky-panky, has been taken up seriously by many
Sydney and Melb. women since hard times began ;
working cleverly a former society connection, they
have accumulated cash enough to retire comfort-
ably from the scene of their trickery. You can
hardly call it swindling, because they make no
pretence. Other women simply come to them and
clamor to be gulled. Hand-reading up-to-date
ought to have a sure income in it for a nice, smart
woman. The “science” as now worked creaks
with age ; it is all expressed in terms of the middle
ages by the exponent, who invariably tells her sub-
ject to expect a letter, or that a water-journey is
before her, or that she will have a visit from a
dark man with news—all survivals from the times
when a letter or a five-mile trip from one’s home,
or the advent of an unknown in the sleepy little
town were epoch-making events —when there were
no newspapers, nor railways, nor penny-post.
There isn’t any use for an occult science ignoring
telephones, motor-cars, and snap-shot cameras.
The newest idea in connection with titles is the
demand already made for the creation of she-
knights, the order to be composed of women
authors, artists, and philanthropists, to celebrate
the great and glorious record-reign of H.M. There
is no feminine of knight, and they would all have
to be dubbed “ lady,” which unoriginal handle
they must share with every titled woman under
the rank of a duchess, whether she be the wife of
the Marquis of Granby or of M. H. Davies. How
would “Lady ” Corelli and “ Lady ” Edna Lyall
agree among literary knightesses ? These suffixes
wouldn’t carry over to husbands, probably, and
the owner of a “lady” on her own would feel
crushed out of sight as plain “Mr.” American
women, who are rabid about titles, are ’cute
enough to annex them in the good, old-fashioned
way. Amelie Rives, authoress of some frank and
candid erotic literature, went one better than even
Miss Vanderbilt by, a short time ago, shaking off
her first matrimonial shackles and appropriating
a real live Prince, and now advertises her books
as by “Princess Troubetskoi.”
After the diamond - jubilee celebrations there
will probably be a resuscitation of that boom-
excrescence, “ The Colonial Peerage, Baronetage,
and Landed Gentry,” to record the new titles.
The first edition was a crimson morocco gilt-
edged vulgarity, wherein the newly - enriched
Nicest were paged, with all their ancestry and
belongings, under such headings as “Blank of
Weroona,” with a furbished-up title usually de-
rived from Oireland’s tattered aristocracy, Blank
himself being a K.C.M.G. and the name-estate a
suburban villa of ten rooms and a lean-to.
To celebrate the “ record reign ” an enterprising
London medallist is sending out picture-cata-
logues of designs for metal memorial-tokens under
Her Gracious’ patronage. Australia is expected
to invest largely in gilt tin-tags which are drawn
life-size. Loyal citizens will go about labelled
“ Britons, Rejoice ! ” or with a couple of inches of
God-save embossed on brass and hung on their
chests. “An enamelled gilt badge with an appro-
priate motto ” ought to keep loyal feelings stirring
and serve to console disappointed title-hunters.
If everybody can’t be knighted, it’s quite within
the power of each to decorate himself cheaply with
a glittering disc of loyalty, with a metal suspender
to hang it on by thrown in.
A Potts Point resident sent out invitations for
an evening party recently, sensibly remarking on
the left hand bottom comer “No music.” This
seems to hold out promise of a time when an ear-
tortured population can go out for an evening’s
pleasuring with the sweet assurance of resting
from Chopin and all his waltzes and being oblivious
of “ The Holy City ” for evermore. There is too
much music in the world, and far too much
“Jerusalem” in music; the young woman who
stands up to the suburban piano and bids her
mournful victims “ Hark how the Angels Sing ”
in shrill soprano ought to be written down by in-
furiated letter - writers to the papers with the
theatre-hat wearer, and street-hawker, and other
nuisances.
A Nice family, during a recent trip ’Ome,
achieved glory by purchasing for the just-out-
daughter a satin ball-gown, the exact replica, shoes
and all, of the wedding apparel of May of Teck,
with which resemblance—as far as a slender anti-
podean can resemble that exceedingly dull, heavy,
commonplace young woman of Dutch build—the
proud possessor of the frock, who is unfortunately
at present in bad health, intends to paralyse
Sydney sassiety next season.
Flags at Sydney Cricket Ground, last Thursday,
were flying at half-mast to notify the decease ot
Mrs. Stephen, wife of Mr. Justice Stephen, who

Rand wick on Saturday afternoon.
The Ormuz, on Saturday, took away Mr. Carl
Fischer and his daughter, Mrs. Inskip, who were

Fellow-passengers of a recently returned theatri-
cal press-woman tell a smart repartee made by her
as she and some other first-saloonists were watch-
ing a couple of steerage passengers who, on near-
ing Adelaide, their destination, blossomed forth
into swagger suits of latest cut. “Why, they
look quite respectable ,” pronounced some high-
toned maidens, disappointed at only finding out
the youths’ attractions at the end of the voyage.
“ No doubt,” said their mother oracularly,
“ poverty and respectability often go together—
don’t they, Miss Blank? ” “I don’t know,” said
the actress ; “I have never tried either.”
Moonlight nights in Centennial Park are dan-
gerous to pedestrians by reason of the numerous
foolish virgins who take no oil in their lamps, but
rush round on their jiggers in the dark and try
the nerve of beginners on the wheel by whizzing
past them violently in all directions. The number
of bikers—other than the Nicest—who go to the
Park for their Sunday spin is increasing weekly.
“The Federal Banner of United Australia” is
the wordy title of a patriotic outburst by S. Pegum.
The sentiment of the song is all right, but the
metre is a trifle labored.
“ From out the void they led a blooming world”
sounds slangy. “ Australia Fair,” by “ Amicus,”
has advanced into a fifth edition, ’neath a blue
star-spangled cover ; this was played as a march
by the band at the military send-off, week before
last. S. Hoben has published his setting of
“ Melons,” sung at the Palace by Lottie Moore, a
successful attempt to catch the tuneful character
of the melody of the nigger-race, from which, ac-
cording to a Hungarian musical expert with an
unspellable name, the music of the future is to
spring. Paling publishes the three.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent
“Commander Collins, the dapper little Secre-
tary of Vic. Defence Department, hoped that he
was going to England as Premier Turner’s secre-
tary. He is an authority on official etiquette, and
has the mysterious £ Queen’s regulations ’ at his
fingers’ ends —he could tell the Premier when to
walk on his knees, when to lick the Queen’s shoes,
when to stand on his head, and other highly import-
ant things. But Turner said—No ; he would only
take with him Police-sergeant Lloyd, who acts as
Premier’s orderly. Accordingly, Lloyd goes, and
Collins remains.
“When private-secretary Wallington was
shunted from Melb. Guvmentouse, the official
explanation was that Gov. Brassey had imported
his wife’s brother, and wanted the young man to
have a place on his staff. The ‘ explanation ’ was
mere fudge, the real reason, as here explained at
the time, being that Wallington could not get on
with H.E. and the pushful young bucks he had
brought out with him. Mr. Capell has never
acted on said staff, and now he has gone back
’Ome again.

said, never has served nectar to the god.i? Which,

“ At their last concert, the Melb. Liedertafel
chorus, balder and more comfortable-looking than
ever, sang sentimentally of withered roses, and
such like, winding up with ‘ Robin Adair,’ in a
manner surprisingly arch and coy for singers of
substantial build and the wrong sex. Their choice
of a song suggests Scotch influence somewhere. Is
there a solemn-faced, 6ft., 14st. Caledonian on
earth whose happiest moments are not spent at the
piano, dirging with still small voice but vast ex-
pression such doleful ditties as ‘Ye Banks and
Braes ’.
“ It was at the same concert that a new lady
pianist elbowed Chopin fireworks at the audience
from behind the shelter of a huge flowered sash,
while Maggie Stirling, effective as usual in her
golden gown, led on little Bertie Zelman by the
hand to acknowledge their joint encore with quite
a motherly air.
“ The prevalent concert coiffure set one think-
ing of days when the suspicion of a parting on a
feminine scalp was equivalent to a confession of
frumpdom. Nowadays, damsels not naturally
gifted with the orthodox thin white line plant a
comb of paste brilliants among their too-luxuriant
locks and pretend that the desired ‘divide’ is
hidden by the ornament. Another change is the
popularity of natural roses for innocent nestling in
the back hair. Upstanding aigrettes and ospreys
look bold and jaunty by contrast.
“ The widow of a well-known Victorian who
committed suicide after slaughtering his children
and locating some ineffectual bullets in his wife’s
skull, is soon to be married again. Timid people
wonder at her pluck. After such an experience
as hers, they say, the average woman would run
the risks of a sleeping volcano, rather than those
of the most trusted hubby that ever was.
“Apropos of widows: visitors to a certain fashion-
able resort are deeply interested in the intentions
of the handsome relict of a great shipping-magnate
with regard to a love-lorn youth not so very
much older than her own urchins. All the holi-
days Romeo has shadowed the Siren and her
olive-branches, carried cockles, sorted sea-weeds,
and looked so despairing whenever another male
approached the group, that old lady onlookers
weep tears of pity for the poor Innocent and find
nothing hard enough to say of his Charmer.
“ Mixed bathing, of a sort, is generally tolerated
nowadays, though local splash-parties are neither
pretty, Frenchy, nor fashionable. Fat women
from the backblocks, clad in dripping, clinging
night-gowns or short flannel skirts, fight shy of
the water without ‘ pa’s ’ protection. The latter
sheds his upper raiment and dons his oldest nether
garments while the brats paddle round in ‘ buff,’
and ma squats on all-fours in a few inches of
water and lets the ’longshore ripples gently
moisten her shivering bulk. Mrs. Grundy endures
these Yahoo exhibitions without wincing, but
what a shriek she raises when a bevy of smart
girls with taut and trim escorts in up-to-date
swimming-suits take the briny together anywhere
within cooey of civilisation !
“In one of the Sunbeam booklets, the authoress
(the former Lady Brassey) congratulates herself
and readers on the fact that at the Pompeii ex-
cavations the pernicious custom of ‘ tipping ’ has
been disallowed. His Ex. of Victoria apparently
has the knack of inculcating his womankind with
the principles of economy. Govt. House resident
guests, for instance, are all related to their hosts,
so that the cakes and ale are kept in the family,
and at the same time a suitable semblance of vice-
regal hospitality is maintained.
“ A wealthy and childless Melb. art patron has
a nude picture of his own dead daughter hanging
behind a curtain in a cabinet by his bedside. The
picture was painted by a famous Parisian artist
when the young lady was under 20, and is a perfect
piece of flesh painting. The girl is represented
standing tip-toe on the edge of a glove, with a
gleaming star on her head—symbolical of some-
thing or other. There is no muslin about it; just
a girl without reservation.
“ The women doctors of Melbourne are working
hard for the establishment of a Women’s Hospital
at which all the Sawboneses and Boluses are to be
feminine. The Stow group of lady doctors is at
the bottom of the scheme, which is making head-
way, as the promoters are pushing, energetic and
clever.

Two things were remembered against
Wrixon in connection with the Convention Elec-
tion. First, that he was responsible for the pro-
vision of flogging in the Victorian Criminal Code:
Second, that he introduced and got carried a bill
to prevent the publication of Sunday newspapers
in the province.
Melb. Age has been interesting reading
lately. The “ watchman on the tower ” leader
immediately before the elections had perhaps most
to do with the crushing nature of the inevitable
Liberal victory. And how’s this for the “ curiosi-
ties of journalism ” ? It appeared on the day after
the elections : “Through town and country alike,
almost universally, the angel of political death
spread his wings on the blast, and breathed on the
faces of those luckless Tories as he passed.”
Dear Bulletin, —Now that N.S. Wales
has cheered itself hoarse over the success
of the ten “best” men, let us examine
the results for a moment. In the first
place, I would like to ask what is Mr. J.
P. Abbott’s programme, seeing that he obtained
64,000 votes ? During the past few weeks I read
the daily papers very carefully, and do not remem-
ber having seen his manifesto ; neither did he ad-
dress any public meeting. Mr. Millen, on the
other hand, put forward a very sensible plat-
form, and seemed to me one of the few can-
didates who thoroughly grasped the Fede-
ral question. Yet he polled only 10,000 votes.
Why this difference? Possibly the well-known
Democratic views of the former secured
for him his high position. And, by the
way, how the Democracy of N.S.W. asserted
itself ! M'Millan, 84,000 ; Abbott, 64,000; Bruce
Smith, 41,000, and the candidates with Democratic
tendencies nowhere. Then, again, the able and
democratic Ashton, one of the best debaters in
the House, 20,000. Brunker, 76,000 ! Let us pray !
—Sancho Panza.
Some Convention returns up to time of
going to press. New South Wales ten: Barton,
Premier Reid, Carruthers, M'Millan, Lyne,
Brunker, R. E. O’Connor, Speaker Abbott, J. T.
Walker, B. R. Wise. Returns not all in, but the
gap between Wise, the last of the accepted, and
John See, the first of the rejected, is so great that
no alteration is possible. Victorian ten : Premier
Turner, Quick, Deakin, Peacock, Isaacs, Tren-
with, Berry, Fraser, Zeal and Higgins. Sun-
dry returns are yet to arrive, and the
Tory party still hopes against hope that
the eleventh man, Wrixon, may displace
Higgins. Meanwhile, however, Wrixon’s general
tendency is to fall further behind, and the pros-
pect of closing the gap of 900 votes which separates
him from Higgins seems hopeless. S.A.’s ten
(probably) : Premier Kingston, Holder, Cock-
burn, Gordon, Baker, Symon, Downer, Glynn,
Howe, Solomon. Many returns were still to come
in, however, and the position of the last four is
very shaky.
A sidelight on the N.S.W. Convention
election:—
Many of the Convention votes polled by R. W. Reid
were no doubt intended for Premier Reid, for, broadly
speaking, no one had ever heard of R. W. Reid. Simi-
larly Dan. O’Connor deprived R. E. O’Connor, Q.C., of
many votes. The latter, of course, also lost much R.O.
support through his inclusion, without his consent, in the
Protestant bunch, for while no doubt Orange Protestants
refrained from voting for him, a R.C. priestly organ
cold-bloodedly told its readers not to vote for anyone
whose name appeared on the Protestant or Orange ticket.
Which, under the circs., was a most cowardly and dis-
graceful piece of advice, inasmuch as it contained the in-
sinuation that the straight-going “Dick” O’Connor was
playing a foul and double game and was a consenting
party to his inclusion in the “ Protestant bunch.” But
for the intrusion of the sectarian issue, R. E. O’Connor
would probably have been second or third on the poll. It
will be seen from the foregoing that no more adroit
scheme for “ queering ” his chances could possibly
have been devised than that of the parsons who, by pro-
fessing to give him a support which they, of course, knew
would not be seriously extended, heavily damaged him in
the eyes of many of the most influential of his co-reli-
gionists. This time, it is clear, the arts of Machiavelli
were at the service, not of the Cardinal, but of his sec-
tarian opponents. As witness the foregoing facts relating
to the candidature of R. E. O’Connor, and the circum-
stance that Cardinal Moran, instead of contenting himself
with looming large and all-powerful in the distant gloom,
was so stupid and unworldly as to come down and let
himself be weighed and measured and put, as it were,
into a paper bag like a pound or two of onions.

“Amongst the arrivals by the Arcadia,”
says S.M. Herald, “ were Mr. J. C. Hilton, one
of Her Majesty’s Justices of the Peace for the
county of Montgomery (Wales), and High Sheriff
in 1893-4, accompanied by Mr. Stephen Brown,
Dumfriesshire. Mr. Hilton expresses himself as
highly pleased with Australia a/ far as he has seen
it, and thinks it in advance of the mother country
in many respects, notably in the magnificence of
its public buildings, banks, &c., and the excellence
of its tram ami railway services.” Thanks. The
question now is—have Mr. Hilton, .Taypee, and
Mr. Brown yet been accorded the “ well-deserved
distinction ” of honorary membership of Sydney
Union Club?
H. H. * Champion is of noble
lineage, and has even relatives who
live at Toorak. The Social Democrat
sometimes pours himself into a frock-
coat, claps a shiny stove-pipe hat on
his head, and visits them. It is
mainly because he is occasionally seen
in that top-hat and frock-coat, and
because he says “ heah, heah ” at
meetings, and plays the English gen-
tleman, thatH.H. makes no headway
with the Melbourne workman. It is
true that John Hancock also wears a
frock-coat sometimes, but that only
makes him look more Hancooky than
ever, whereas Champion looks like
an emperor at the very least.
“Demos” Palmer is the sole
survivor in Melb. of the genus con-
verted pressman. Once upon a time
there was another specimen, an ex-
member of the staff of a godly daily,
long defunct, who, after being fired
out of his billet for being audibly
blasphemous at a Presbyterian As-
sembly meeting, donned a red jersey
and became a full-blooded Salva-
tionist. No major was there who
yelled “Hallelujah!” more vocifer-
ously or stoushed the tambourine with
greater vigor, and one night, when a
brother scribe happened across him
at a meeting he was “doing,” his
eyes filled with tears at the earnest-
ness of the regenerated one. Sud-
denly the latter caught sight of the
unreformed pressman at his little
table in front of the platform, and
smiled to him one of those sickly
smiles peculiar to the saved. Then
he lifted a slip of paper carefully
from his pocket and, scrawling a few
words, folded it and dropped it
silently below. The reporter, fully
expecting to receive some pious in-
junctions to mend his ways, opened
the missive in trepidation and read :
“ Full up of this blanky rot; is there
any show of getting a billet on the
press again ? ”
“ Salmon ” Wilson paid just £30,000
solid cash for his title on a plain business basis.
His family insisted on the “old man” getting a
title, as they had matrimonial ambitions. “Sal-
mon,” like a level-headed man, went to the proper
quarter, told the circumstances, and asked the
price. “A monumental gift,” was the answer;
“build a big examination hall for Melbourne
University, and the title is yours. “Right.
How much?” “£30,000!” “Very well.” The
cheque was handed over, and that big white
atrocity, Wilson Hall, exists. That hall has so
overshadowed Wilson that his sterling acclimatisa-
tion work is lost in a fog.
"GIDDY, GIDDY GOUTI”
SCHOOL INSPECTOR (questioning physiology class) : “ What are the
early symptoms of gout? ”
YOUNGSTER (promptly): “ Please, sir, I know, sir—your shirt hang-
ing out!”
The deceased Ash, M.P., Premier King-
ston’s law partner, joined the 'Cape Mounted
Rifles nearly 20 years ago, so that he might act as
correspondent at the front in the Kaffir war. He
was a man (writes The Bulletin’s Adelaide
correspondent) of dauntless courage and iron
resolution, and although nearly always in an
insignificant Parliamentary minority, sometimes
standing alone, never yielded an inch to his
opponents. During seven years of political life,
no one can point to a single private promise or
hustings pledge which he broke. He was an
“ Individualist ” of the deepest dye, and he never
flinched from uttering his views, no matter how
unpopular they might be with his audience.
J. B. Mather, who was in partnership with Ash
as a newspap- r proprietor for nine years, and who
shared his libel-verdict misfortunes, says—“ He
was the cleverest, the most honorable, and the
kindest-hearted man I ever knew.”

A cable in Sydney E. News states that
“ Mr. Frank Bennett (one of the News pro-
prietors) arrived by the Austral. He was in Paris
during the Czar’s visit.” What the News means
obviously is that the Czar was in Paris during
Mr. Frank Bennett’s visit.
Two old Vic. legal identities died last week.
One George Barber, solicitor, who had practised
at Warrnambool for 43 years. He arrived in
Melbourne in ’4l, and was 83 when he died. The
other, O. L. Hanbury, was admitted in 1840, and
had practised in Victoria since ’52.
One of the late George Ash’s peculiarities
was that he christened all five of his children with
names beginning with vowels. He said he had
been so teased when a youngster and afterwards
by being called “ Gash ” and Hash that he wished
to save his family from a similar fate.
The son of an Australian Jewish Rabbi
has renounced the faith of his fathers, and turned
Presbyterian, of all things, whereat there is great
rejoicing amongst the “unco gude.” After all,
the conversion is not very surprising. The Jew
who turned at all would naturally turn Scotchman.
James Tyson, who goes to England with
Nelson, is, as a type, the most notable of the Aus-
tralians who will salute the record reign. A native
of N.S.W., 6ft. 4in. high, 74 years of age, straight,
hale, cheerful; never smoked, never swore, never
drank, never fell in love, and several times a
millionaire.
An effort is being made by curator Maiden,
of Sydney Botanical Gardens, to have the
neglected grave of Allan Cunningham in the
“Old Sandhills,” Belmore-road, put into repair.
While about it, some attention might be paid to
the grave of John Oxley, the explorer, near
Cunningham’s.
Less than two hours before his death, Ash,
M.P. (S.A.) insisted upon giving Premier King-
ston, his law partner, particulars and directions
concerning a case he was work.. .g up when he
was taken ill. Ash leaves a widow and five
children very poorly provided for, and a fund for
their benefit has been started.
Q. ex-warden and Mines-Minister Hodg-
kinson is spelling in Sydney after three years spent
in reporting on Westralian mines. His old Q.
friends will be glad to hear that the little man
looks as rosy and as gritty as ever, though he has
recently been known to prefer shandygaff to
whisky. Billy Little will grieve over this sign of
advancing years.
Two Yic. women are getting ready for a
plunge into the legal profession. One is the
daughter of an M.L.C., and the other is a B.A.
There appears to be nothing expressly against
them in English law, but the implied presumption
is pretty steep, and they will need, no doubt, a
statute to give them a leg over it. Maoriland
already has taken down the barriers.

29 Victorian candidates for Convention votes,
although he was one of the very few—if not the
only one—who shouted cabs for the convenience of
his/lamentable disciples. By this time .£..£. has
probably arrived at a dim suspicion that he is
politically deader than the smellful herring.
Socially he is still a mere youth of 67.
A certain lengthy Maoriland politician,
some time ago harassed by a dissatisfied lady
friend, ascribed her unsuccessful attempts to bring
him into disrepute to the feminine love of posing
before the public in any capacity. Lately this
lady was delivered of triplets, and the news was
broken to the politician by a friend. “ Good
Lord ! ” said the long man, excitedly waving his
hands, “ where the Devil will the woman’s
craze for notoriety land her NEXT ? ”
Andrew Lang has the distinction of beina
uncle of a young Australian lady who is regardeo
as a second Trilby. Dr. Wm. Lang, of Corowa,
N.S.W., is bringing up his children to look witk
contempt on shoes and stockings, and his eldest
daughter astonishes those people who are unaware
of his ideas on hygiene by going everywhere bare-
footed. Whether Miss Lang will continue th<
practice when a few years older remains to b<
seen.

Lucien Lesna, the French cyclist, whether
in public on the racing-track, going 30 miles an
hour behind his quintette, or in private lounging
in an arm-chair and caressing a pet opossum, is t
distinct figure in Australian cycling. Essentially
a paced rider, he is the first of his type to visit
Australia ; and although last Thursday (March 4),
in a 20-mile paced race, he beat by three laps
O’Brien, M‘Combe, Meadham and Elliott, re-
lieving each other how they would, neverthelesi
three out of the four vanquished could wallop
Lesna home in an unpaced mile. In France the
paced stayer and the sprinter are distinct specie*
which never conflict. Lesna didn’t start till ’oa
when he was 28 ; now at 32 he gets <£2s a week
salary. He “does not bother about rules of
eating”; he drinks “wine but no spirits”; he
abjures tobacco because “ nicotine is a poisoß
which injures the stomach, and a stayer’s stomach
must be perfectly healthy ”; he goes to bed a*
half-past nine and gets up at seven. The prize*
for Australian handicap races seem to him out of
all proportion—“ a third-class man gets such a
LESNA (a French champion cyclist matched
against Martin.)
Photo., Bond, Adelaide.
start that he can win .£lOO, .£2OO, or .£304
first prize; in France he couldn’t win more
than <£B or £lO until he became first-class. "
The rubbing-down, which is so big jan essen-
tial in the Australian cyclist’s creed, brings
a smile from Lesna—“ Third-class man crawl*
half-a-mile, comes in, and is rubbed do we
one hour ; in France we ride 50 mile, wipe off tht
sweat —it is finished.” The Australian’s love fo»
laying his shirt upon his fancy is a new order os
things to Lesna ; “ in France’they all go, but only
to see the racing—all together do not bet five
bobs.” He thinks the Sydney Cricket Ground
track is beyond comparison the first in Australia |
it is so good, indeed, that he will attempt upon i!
to lower the world’s five-mile record—9min. 16se6,
Lesna’s business is record-breaking and long-dig-
tance racing behind pace, but, all the same, W. J,
C. Elliott, who rode second in the last A.N.A,
and third in the last Austral, but has never raced
behind pace in his life, will take a lap start and
give Lesna a five-mile go. Bilmartin, too, ig
spoiling for Lesna’s scalp, and it is almost agreed
that the pair have a match one mile unpaced, fm
and ten miles paced, at St. Kilda on 10th April,
for .£250 and a side wager of .£lOO. Should
Martin get the pacing he expects, he will giv*
Lesna the battle of his life.
