WATSON’S WHISKY.
“I am a Woman and hence I Suffer.”
How often this expression is heard, and how
much oftener is it felt. Alas ! women do suffer.
Why, we often cannot tell, but we know there is
one great cause, and that i$ weakness. The head-
aches, the depressed feelings, the pains, the dis-
couragements, indeed, almost all the misery has a
common cause—weakness. At such times a
woman always needs a friend that can be relied
upon, and such a friend, for more than twenty
years, has been the greatest of all remedies, War-
ner s Safe Cure. By its purity and its power it
furnishes a prompt relief for women in their hours
of need, and if the grateful expressions which come
up from the homes of the land about what Safe
Cure has done were printed, they would fill
volumes. If you, reader, are a sufferer, can you
not take hope from this suggestion ?
William Fleming,

POLITICAL
POINTS
England’s unsleeping enemy—Germany.
“ Dulce et decorum est pro Pat. Mor.”
was the noble motto on the Kyardinal’s Federa-
tion shield. And still they threw him out.
Westralian forthcoming general elections
will resolve themselves into a ballot-box battle
between the Sandgroper and the T’othersider.
An average sitting of N.S.W. Public
Works Committee costs the country £42. Some
member should call for a return showing the
average duration of a sitting.
Chamberlain’s idea, no doubt, is that the
Australian Premiers are coming to London to dis-
cuss with him the new Federal constitution. And
that is how it will, no doubt, eventuate.
One of the surprises of N.S.W. Federal
Convention election is the return of the Silent-
party —Brunker (who hardly spoke at all) and
*. P. Abbott (who spoke not at all). Silence is
folden !
The Federation election “tickets” pub-
lished by the S.A. National Ass and the young
Patriotic League, which is a foal of the Ass in
8.A., omitted every Minister and every Labor
member.
The 1891 election sent fourteen new mem-
bers into S.A. Parliament. Three of them are
*ow dead—and six are already out of politics. A
politician, on an average, lasts just about as long
as a bicycle.
Six, at least, out of the ten N.S.W. dele-
gates to the Convention oppose the transfer of the
State railways to the Federation. Their Federal
policy is to marry the girl—and leave her at the
church door.
The first Convention in Australia was
the Melbourne one, of 1856. It was estab-
lished at the Commercial Hotel, comer of Lons
Sale and Spring streets, not far from Parliament
House, to watch legislative jugglery with the
Land. The heroes were Wilson Gray, Heales,
Hon, Owens, O’Hea, and so forth.
Vic. Attorney-General Isaacs’ Federation
meetings in the country went to show that his
“unpopularity,” much talked about in Melbourne,
floes not extend beyond the Collin s-street gang,
stuck up by his Companies’ Act and Insolvency
Bill. This gang poisonously hates the gritty
Isaacs, and has good reason for doing so.
The Federal Enabling Act specified that
lists of nominated candidates should be advertised
three times in all N.S.W. newspapers. “In some
cases,” wails a country newspaper-man, “there
srere but two insertions and in a few only one,
owing to dilatoriness at head-quarters. Query:
Cannot some lawyer start a vitiating crusade ? ”
S.A. National Ass originally included
Kingston in its Federation ticket, but at the last
moment several prominent Tories used their in-
fluence to have the name erased. Anyhow, the
aflea of the Stomach Men rolling up to vote unani-
mously for Kingston’s name seemed so incongruous
Shat it was just as well matters turned out as
Shey did.
G. Downes Carter, M.P., the Tory drink-
merchant of Melbourne, complains bitterly thab
the Vic. beer-trade has fallen off 50 per cent, of
3ate years, and seems inclined to hold the Turner
Ministry responsible. Perhaps it is. When the
Ctovt. of which Carter was an ornament was in
power, it took much tanglefoot to drown the
public feelings.
When S.A. Ministerialists and Labor-party
combined and issued a Federation ticket in reply
to Tory tactics, the latter began to realise that
Shey stood a strong chance of utterly standing out
an the cold. Then came the wail that the Liberals
were making the election a party one—perhaps
the cheekiest bit of electioneering, considering the
real facts, yet done in S.A.
Premier Turner has neatly capped the
N.S.W. lamentations over its Federal “sacrifices”
by pointing out that Victoria will lose £230,000 a
year in Customs duties. He proposes that the
Federal Treasury should pay it back for the first
two years. Does Turner mean this, or is it
strategic ? It might be a mistake to let N.S.W.
flo all the self-sacrificial posing.
• One dead Federal candidate, George Ash,
vf S.A., went to the poll—though about 20
Sawyers helped to draft the Enabling Act they all
forgot to make provision for withdrawing the
aame of a deceased candidate; and so far as the
returns had come in at time of writing (Monday)
he had scored 308 votes. He stood at the end of
She list, but still he wasn’t far behind the three
Sast live men, Ebenezer Ward, Williams and John
Jones, and as the returns come in from remote
places where his death is less widely known, the
man that’s dead may creep up and leave a good
many live men behind.
A correspondent
writes to heave the
same old curse at the
clumsy, inartistio
name of New South
Wales, and wants to
suggest “Brightland”
as a promising sub-
stitute. This country
has had woeful bad
luck in names. New
South Wales, South
Australia, West Aus-
tralia (now Westra-
lia), New Zealand
(now Maoriland), and
the Northern Terri-
tory are about the
clumsiest samples of
nomenclature going.
Victoria and Queens-
land are better be-
cause they consist of
one word only ; Tas-
mania is better still.
All the same, The
Bulletin isn’t taking
any “Brightland.” It
seems almost hopeless
to look for a short,
appropriate, euphoni-
ous name with any
special fitness about
it, unless the mother
province is called
plain “ Possum,” yet
the necessity for find-
ing new names must
be faced sometime.
No country in its
senses can propose to
struggle for ever with
a designation like
N.S.W. or S.A. or
W.A. or N.T. It
would be better to
call the country plain
Z. and be done with it.
After ten years of
Prohibition with its
paraphernalia of
“ clubs ” and sly-grog
shops, the Chaffey
colony at Renmark
(S.A.) is about to run
a pub or philanthropic pothouse on Gothenburg
lines. S.A. has tried Prohibition at Renmark,
Victoria at Mildura, Maoriland at Clutha, and
the result at all three places has been disgust and
illicit fusel-oil and failure.
The one wholly-unaccountable absentee
from the list of N.S.W. Federal candidates was
Mandarin Quong Tart.
ALLEGED MURDERER BUTLER BEHIND THE BARS.
(From San Francisco “Call.”)
Miss Spence, of S.A., the only lady
Federal candidate in Australia, stands about 23rd
on the list, and is evidently hopeless.
Watt, an A.N.A. “orator,” is already
organising committees in the fashionable parts of
the North Melbourne electorate with a view to
ousting Labor-member Prendergast next election.
HE WAS SATISFIED.
PAL (very drunk after the races): “ Ten pounds isn’t a bad win—hie —but I
wish it had been ten thousand.”
OTHER PAL: “ Why complain? We couldn’t have got any drunker than
we are ”
The S.A. village settlements, six months
ago, lost their father and best friend by the death
of Lands Minister Gillen. Now their most im-
placable enemy, George Ash, has gone. The
latter represented the district in which they are
located, but he never lost an opportunity of in-
veighing against the crime of the Government in
advancing them money.
A rumour., which the Kingston Govt,
would do well to explain or repudiate, has it that
S. A.’s refusal to pay her agreed-on share towards
Thursday Island fortifications was in consequence
of a bombardier relative of an influential S.A.
politician not obtaining a billet from the Thurs'-
day Island authorities.
At N.S.W. Federation poll about 148,000
electors voted out of about 260,000 qualified to
vote,, or 57 per cent, of the whole. At last Parlia-
mentary election 64 per cent, voted ; and, as
Federation still only exists in theory, and Parlia-
ment has a very practical application to local
roads and bridges, there is no cause to complain
of comparative want of public interest.
Anent Yic. protests, backed municipally,
against the lack of any provision except gaol for
the aged poor, the Yic. Local Government Act in
force for the last 2.3 years gives Councils power to
provide for them out of the rates, and to raise
money by loan to build and maintain asylums and
other places for the aged poor. Yet everyone in
Yic. is howling for an Act of Parliament in the
matter, the parish Pecksniffs especially.
During the N.S.W. Convention election it
was pointed out that because an elector could vote
anywhere double voting and dummying would
occur. As a matter of fact, it is impossible for
anyone to vote twice on one right, as when a vote
is recorded the right is stamped. Also, a polling-
deputy can easily stop fraudulent voting by asking
the elector to sign his name for comparison with
the signature on the right. The electoral right is
one of the best of all possible voting safeguards.
Why doesn’t the National Ass dig up a
poet ? The Fat world is weary waiting for a
singer who can glorify greed in stately measure
and stir the souls of men with strong, sweet words
in defence of the poor, down trodden mortgagee.
The urgent need was proved the other day by a
Fat Tas. Federation candidate, who advertised
Lawson’s “Star of Australasia ” as a recitation at
one of his meetings—obviously because he couldn’t
find anything worth reciting in the literature of
Corpulency.
Energetic efforts were made by Melb.
Freetraders to damn Trenwith’s Convention
chances just previous to the election. Accusations
of refusing to pay up to his union and of infidelity
to the members were scattered wholesale, and a
leaflet accusing Trenwith of going to the boss of a
man who had attacked him on a public platform
and getting him the sack was profusely distributed
where it would do most good for Foreign-trade
and Tory principles. The tract bore the imprint
of the Freetrade Democratic Association. All
lies ; the man in question had not been sacked at
all.
Three Scotchmen were included among
the successful conventwners for Victoria, but
ne’er an Irishman got through. Dr. Quick’s
position of second on the list was a strik-
ing testimony to the efficacy of advertising. Even
in Collingwood the Bendigo champion, who had
been the first man to publish his “platform,”
came next to Turner at the poll, beating Berry,
Trenwith, and Peacock by a few score votes m
each case. Simon Fraser, M.L.C., the one other
advertiser, found 1033 supporters in democratic
Collingwood, and was the only Councillor, bar Pre-
sident Zeal, to get any votes worth mentioning.
The N.S.W. Freetrade Government has
decided to protect local industry some more by
letting to a local firm a tender for 40,000 blankets
at Bs. sd. each. The price for an imported lot of
only 2000 blankets was 7s. 9d., arid this much
larger lot could presumably have been bought still
cheaper. Therefore Premier Reid’s blanket-man
gets 9 per cent. Protection at the least, and pos-
sibly 12 or 15 per cent. The scheme for surrepti-
tiously protecting the steel rail industry, however,
fell through. One tenderer wanted <£150,000
more than the imported price. Another merely
asked that Govt, should guarantee 3$ per cent,
interest on his capital, which might mean £lO,OOO
or .£700,000, or any other sum above the importers’
price, and was altogether too vast an undertaking.
So G. Reid kills one-half of his Freetrade deity,
and sticks by the other half to the last till next
time.
After such things as these, who worships
what is called success? Dan O’Connor, 21,826
votes ; E. D. Millen, 10,034. Where is there a
more empty and exploded skiter than Dan
O’Connor —but Dan O’Connor is picturesque and
has advertised himself; whereas Millen, a hard
student, good speaker and master of his subject,
labors under the disadvantage of not having
been continuously bathed in printer’s ink for
30 years. John Bridge, a non - Federationist,
who can’t put together three coherent
political words, 18,724 votes; Dr. Cullen, a
keen and cultured publicist, solid for Australian
unity, 13,013. Cow-merchant Brunker,grotesquely
dumb on the subject of Federation through sheer
political ignorance and stupidity, 76,691 ; B.
R. Wise, an ardent Federationist and learned
constitutional lawyer, unsurpassed as a debater.
56,873.


Premier Reid probably spent less on his
Federation candidature than any other prominent
N.S.W. candidate, save Abbott. The prudent
patriot who footed the b.ggest bill in the 49 was
eventually 41st joint in the procession.
J ohn Forrest had on 4th March held
the Westralian Premiership for six years, two
months and six days. This breaks the previous
Australian record-that of Herbert, of Queens-
land, by about three weeks. The third-best record
for enduranoe was M'Gulloch’s four years and
eleven months in Victoria ; then Gillies’ four years
and nine months in the same province, and Parkes’s
four years and sixteen days in N.S.W. In Maori-
land, Stafford put up a record of five years one
month and lOdays, and another of nearly four years;
and oeddon, with nearly four years to his credit
an . u new Parliament behind him, looks as if he
might putup the Australasian record par excellence.
Another kind of record was put up by old Henry
Ayers, who was seven times Premier of S.A., and
only ruled for four years and one month alto-
gether.
Adelaide newspaper proprietors and politi-
cians are gleeful over the prospect of the Federal
Convention meeting in the city, but Adelaide
Hansard reporters lift up a loud chorus of
“Damn ! ”
Some irreverent vandal has jabbed a pen
knife several times into the oil-painting of the
late J. B. Patterson in Melb. National Gallery.
It is not known whether the culprit is a political
or an art enthusiast.

STOCK AND STATION AGENTS,
WOOL & PRODUCE SALESMEN.
Sydney, 15th February, 1897.
CIRCULAR.
Dear Sir,
We now beg to notify to you that on the Ist prox. we add to our well-
known Stock and Station Agency business that of Wool and Produce Salesmen.
This is quite a legitimate branch of our business that we now take up with
confidence.
We have secured the services of Mr. Edward N. Row, lately with Messrs.
Campbell, Nimtno & Co., formerly with Messrs. Goldsbrough & Co., and Dalgety
& Co., of Melbourne, who will conduct this branch of our business under the
supervision of our Managing Directors, and whose acknowledged business
capacity and energy, combined with the well-known position and standing of
this Company, are sufficient guarantee that any Wool or Produce entrusted to
our care will be disposed of to the best advantage.
Our Produce Stores are situated on the Circular Quay and Macquarie Street,
are large, well-lighted, and in every way convenient. Wool Stores we will secure
before the opening of next Wool Season, our present accommodation being ample
for any Wool offering during the Winter months.
As in our Stock business, you may rely that we will conduct this branch of
our business strictly as Selling Brokers only ; neither directly nor indirectly will
we entertain any interest in Produce committed to our care beyond that of realis-
ing the highest price. Buyers, too, may know that they will compete only with
legitimate competitors.
Our Produce Sales commence after the Ist March ; but our Stores are now open
for the reception of consignments, which, small or large, shall have equal attention ;
a bag of cow hair, a few horns, sheepskins, or shank bones, will be cared for equally
with 1000 bales of wool, casks of tallow, or hides.
Kindly note also that we remove from our old offices to new and more com-
modious ones in O’Connell Street, on Ist April.
Yours faithfully,
W. WRIGHT, Secretary,
Pitt, Son & Badgery, Limited.

ANOTHER MIRACLE IN SYDNEY.
A CRIMEAN VETERAN AND GOOD
TEMPLAR RECOUNTS HIS RE-
COVERY FROM A LIVING DEATH.
Mr. Charles Terry, a Crimean veteran, of 485
Rent-street, Sydney, having been lecturing lately
in the Domain on the “ Temperance Question,” we
(says the Newspaper) despatched a reporter to his
residence, who gives the following verbatim ac-
count of his illness and marvellous recovery. Our
representative found Mr. Terry a chatty old gen-
tleman of 66, who seemed decidedly pleased to be
interviewed on the subject of his wonderful escape
from death. In response to a few questions, Mr.
Terry stated :—“ I joined the Royal Navy about
1853, and served during the Crimea in H.M.S. Re-
triburion, Captain Thomas Fisher, under Admiral
Dundas, bearing the flag of Rear-Admiral Baines,
and I bear the Crimean decoration. I arrived in
Australia at the latter end of 1877 in H.M.S.
Sapphire, the Hon. Captain Merry. I then joined
the Diamond, under Captain Dale, on this station,
and on leaving the ship quitted the service for good,
having in all served in 17 ships in Her Majesty’s
Royal Navy, during which time I was employed
as Admiral’s, Commodore’s, and Captain’s cook.
In August of this year (1896) I found myself at
Emu Plains, being in the employ of Messrs.
M'Grath, fellmongers, employed as cook. It was
whilst there that I was taken ill, towards the
latter end of August. I attributed my illness to
exposure in periodically leaving a hot kitchen to
attend to some outside work. My first symptoms
were coughing, a spitting of blood, and a bloody dis-
charge from the kidneys of the consistency of jelly.
One man there was particularly kind to me, arid
constantly stayed up with me all night, giving me
eucalyptus to inhale, and continually rubbing it
into my chest, throat, and stomach. This man
kept constantly telling me to clear out from Emu
Plains and go to hospital, otherwise, to use his
own words, he said, ‘ You will peg out.’ Feeling
that he was likely to prove a true prophet, I fol-
lowed his advice, came to Sydney, and took up my
abode at 485 Kent-st., where I still am living. I
should, however, mention that both Mr. and Mrs.
M'Grath gave me the highest of references, and
regretted that my bad health would not allow me
to remain. (These recommendations, which were
of a most satisfactory nature, he showed our re-
presentative.) On my arrival in Sydney I suffered
from great pains in the back and sides, and also
under the ribs. I could take no solid food, and I
entirely subsisted on eggs and new milk, which
my landlady procured for me. I went to see the
Government medical officer at Circular Quay, and
was so horribly weak that it took me an hour and
ten minutes to get there. He told me to strip,
which I did, and he then sounded me and asked
me how long I had been bad. I told him that
from the first it was about eight weeks, upon
which he said, ‘Get away, man; it’s chronic.’
He could not send me to Little Bay, but suggested
that I should go to Liverpool Asylum at once, but
I refused to go there, and returned home. I then
went to see the Grand Chief Templar. I also saw
his wife and the Grand Lodge Secretary, and told
them of my pitiable state. With the assistance of
Brother J. C. .Jones, late District Deputy of the
Good Templars, and the Grand Lodge Secretary,
I had medicine prescribed for me of various kinds,
which proved quite ineffectual, and afforded me
no relief whatever. I took no less than seven
bottles of eucalyptus, and even chlorodyne was
prescribed for me. I fell away lamentably, per-
spired profusely, and could not obtain any sleep.
I was in such pain that I could not shift in bed.
My right side was much the worst, and my breath-
ing was most labored and painful. I cannot be
too grateful to my friend, Brother J. C. Jones.
He would stay with me for three or four hours at
a time, and it was he that saved me from going to
an asylum. One day a friend of mine read to me
the account of Boilermaker Jarvis’s wonderful re-
covery by taking Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, and I
at once procured a box. I had taken all but
two, when the blood-spitting and emission
ceased. After two boxes I was able to get up and
move about, and now, at the end of six boxes, feel
almost well, with the exception of feeling very
weak. The cure was MAGICAL, and I tell all
my friends and the general public that nothing but
Dr. Williams’ Pills brought me once more before
them to agitate the temperance question, and on
each occasion I have met with a great reception.
(Mr. Terry here called our reporter’s attention to
the amount of useless physic he had consumed,
and, indeed, the amount of bottles reminded one
of the interior of a chemist’s shop.) I can speak
in the highest possible terms of the marvellous
curative properties of the Pink Pills. On my
visits to and lectures in the country I shall con-
sider it my duty to suffering humanity to recount
my astounding cure, and I trust I may be the
means of saving the life of many a despairing
mortal.—CHAS. TERRY.”
(Signed on oath and in the presence of Charles
Lovely, J. P.)
Our reporter, on making enquiry, has discovered
that Mr. Terry was, no later than yesterday (Sun-
day, December 27th), speaking in the Domain for
the benefit of humanity on the marvellous cure he
received from the Pills, and it is his intention to
continue to do so during all his temperance lec-
tures, as he feels it is a duty he owes to society.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills strike at the root of
disease, driving it from the system and restoring
the patient to health and strength. They consti-
tute a perfect blood-builder and nerve-restorer,
curing such diseases as rheumatism, lumbago,
sciatica, neuralgia, partial paralysis, St. vitus
dance, indigestion, &c. They are also a specific
for the troubles which make the lives of so many
women a burden, and speedily restore the rich
glow of health to pale and sallow cheeks. Obtain-
able from chemists and storekeepers generally, or
sent (post paid) by Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co.,
Queen’s Place, Sydney, on receipt of 2s. 9d. per
single box, or half-dozen for 14s. 9d. Men broken
down by overwork, worry or excess, will find them
an undoubted specific. *

their technicalities to draw specifications and
claims, &c., that will stand the test of a law-suit.
Hitherto expert Patent agents have made charges
simply prohibitive to most inventors, but Mr.
Arthur Griffith, M.L.A., of 39 Castlereagh-
street (next Hotel Australia), Sydney, and Bolton-
street, Newcastle, having established reciprocal
arrangement with leading Patent agents in all the
principal countries in the world is now prepared
to do the work accurately and effectively at rates
that will place a patent within reach of the poorest
invencor. For the sum of £2O (exclusive of cost of
drawings) he will obtain a certificate of Provisional
Protection (in the first instance) for any invention
in the seven Australasian colonies and Great
Britain, and full Patents all over the world at
similarly reasonable rates. Mr. Griffith also
makes a specialty of designing and registering
Trade Marks and Copyrights.*

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Not one of the Victorian racing “ prophets ”
gave Coil any sort of chance for the Australian Cup, five
days before the race. Several of them picked Fadladeen,
and one (the LEADER-man) chose Resolute, a horse that
hadn't done nearly enough work for such a journey.
Coil’s return to “form” set people thinking
again of last season when he and Newhaven used to play
a game of one down, t’other come up. For the first
time in Australian turf history the champion two-year-
olds of one season have proved rival handicap winners in
the next.
Melb. Herald said of Bloodshot: “He ran in
the Essendon Stakes and failed to get a place, but per-
haps it would be as well not to pay too much attention to
this performance, as his stable companion, Warpaint,
started a warm favorite in this event.’’ How comforting
for owner Harvey (who never bets, but races for stakes
only) to be told that his horse was making the running
for a stable companion, the property of his trainer,
Power! And, by the way, several newspapers said the
same thing quite casually, as though the interests of Mr.
Harvey were bound to be subservient to the interests of
the “ stable.”
Five years in succession an “ odds on” favorite
for the V.R.C. Leger has been beaten. They were Derby
winners, all of them. In ’93 Camoola lost because it was
raining at the time, and he objected to getting wet, they
said. In ’94 Carnage lost because he had “ gone to pieces
since the Spring in ’95 The Harvester “ wasn’t himself
on the day ” ; last year Wallace’s jockey allowed the out-
sider, Cabin Bov, to run away with the race; and this
year Newhaven “ lacked condition.” By turning up the
English racing calendars and noting how seldom a Derby
winner fails to win the Doncaster St. Leger (with odds
laid on him) a punter may easily satisfy himself that the
training system, or the climate, or the jockeyship, or the
equine characteristics of this great country make “rela-
tive form” a ridiculously unreliable thing to bet upon, as
compared with the same r.f. in the original land of the
thoroughbred.
* * •
Herewith a little racing food for the guileless
punter to chew. In the Leger race The Officer romped
away from Newhaven (first favorite) at even weights over
1J miles, and was much the fresher of the pair after their
gallop. In the Australian Cup, a slow-run 21 miles, The
Officer (first favorite) was beaten many lengths by Coil at
even weights. In the slower-run Champion, which was
practically a 1J mile event, Newhaven easily beat The
Officer, who beat Coil (first favorite), all at the same
weight. And j-et the multitude cheered lustily ! The
race was a preposterous upset of “ latest form,” but the
public appeared satisfied, and the V.R.C. stewards said
nothing, as usual.
More food. In the Hopeful Stakes red-hot
favorite Manfred was easily beaten at even weights by
Waraka, whose jockey never moved on the colt, whilst
Manfred was being walloped to no purpose. Waraka
started second favorite at 4tol on this occasion. Five
days later Waraka and Manfred met again, at a difference
of 31bs. only. Manfred was again a “ hot pot,” but this
time Waraka had no friends at 10 to 1. Manfred won in
a canter, and Waraka finished last in a field of nine.
Another tough morsel. In the Brunswick
Stakes (1J miles) Lilith (7st. 91b.), starting at 7 to 1, took
the lead at the mile and just got home in front. The
mare’s performance suggested that she would have pre-
ferred to finish at the mile. Three days later Lilith (Bst.
71b.) started for the Bourke Handicap of one mile. Her
price was 12 to 1, and she finished nowhere. Another
two days interval. Then Lilith started for the River
Handicap (1J miles) and carried Bst. 31b. to a very easy
victory.
The Maiden Stakes race, last week, was a pain-
fully funny procession. Reka, an odds-on favorite, came
East the distance-post six lengths ahead of the second
orse, the other three stragglers following at equally long
intervals. Marusa (a onetime Melb. Cup favorite) brought
up the rear, about 24 lengths behind Reka, and little boy
Redfearn amused himself by making a show of riding
Marusa desperately as the winner “ walked in.”
James Gough distinguished himself in one of
the preliminary canters at Flemington last week. Long-
legged Jim was giving Loyalty a breather past the stand,
when his cap fell off. He seemed to lose his head along
with his cap, for, whilst turning round to look for' the
missing articles, Jim’s boAv fell out of the saddle and re-
ceived a nasty shaking.
• * * *
Considered in the light of his race for the Loch
Plate last Saturday, not to mention his Champion victory,
Newhaven’s performance in the Leger now ranks as a very
remarkable affair, and if the V.R.C. stewards are content
to let it go without an enquiry, the long-suffering public
had better ask themselves whether horse-racing at Flem-
ington is any longer a matter for intelligent sporting
interest. The Bulletin will merely say that, in the light
of subsequent running, Newhaven appears to have been
“ dosed ” —with water, or what not—by some person or
persons unknown previous to the Leger race The sus-
picious surroundings of Newhaven’s Leger fiasco are re-
vealed in the light of “respectable” newspaper comments.
The Argus said of it—“ According to good judges, New-
haven was not half fit. He looked soft, and was sweating
before the battle had begun. . . The betting was
peculiar. ‘ I’ll take 4to I,’ was the cry for some time,
but at the last moment it was possible for the admirers of
Newhaven to get on by laying 9to 4. . . Newhaven
and The Officer will meet again in the Champion Stakes
on Thursday, and then racing men will see what they will
see.”
They saw Newhaven win the Champion Stakes
in the easiest possible style. It wasn’t a severe three-
mile race, but it was at least as genuine a test of condition
in horseflesh as the lf-mile gallop for the Leger. New-
haven looked blooming, not soft. He didn’t sweat before,
during, or after the gallop. In short, the difference
between the two performances was not to be accounted
for by natural causes as far as one could reasonably sup-
pose. Said the Age—“ Only one look was required to see
that Newhaven was in much improved condition—and
not a few good judges declared that he appeared almost
as well as in the spring, although it was hard to believe
that his mentor could have effected such a change in his
condition in such a short time. From sto 1 the colt
rapidly shortened to 3to 1.” And the Argus (which had
advised racing men to look out for what they would see)
remarked “ it is ten thousand pities that the escutcheon
of a horse possessing the phenomenal powers that New-
haven is known to possess should be besmirched by per-
formances which would discredit the meanest selling-
plater. Newhaven’s form has been most inconsistent.”
What would happen in the case of a mean
selling plater displaying such discreditably inconsistent
form ? The owner, trainer and jockey would be called
upon to show good reason for the horse’s in-and-out per-
formances, and, if the explanation were not satisfactory,
certain pains and penalties would ensue to somebody.
How preposterous were the commonly accepted
Springtime excuses for Newhaven’s defeat in the Caul-
field Guineas! It was then said that the horse got beaten
because he wasn’t allowed to gallop away with the race,
making his own running. Which is just what Newhaven
didn’t do in the Champion and Loch Plate. Will it now
be said that he lost the Leger through being ridden on
the lines of his sensational Derby and Cup victories?
Mischief ran very indifferently on Champion
day. In the Autumn Handicap (13 furlongs), the mare
(carrying Bst. 21b.) met Response at an advantage of 71b.,
and was decisively beaten by Dan O’Brien’s candidate.
Later in the afternoon, she ran unplaced and unbacked
for the River Handicap (1J miles), being beaten by Don
John (7st. lib.) among others. On Saturday, in the Place
Handicap (10 furlongs), Mischief met Response again, on
only 51b. better terms, also Don John on 71b. better terms
than before. This time, the daughter of Commotion took
the lead a long way from home, and won comfortably. If
a slight improvement in handicap-terms can effect so con-
siderable an improvement in a horse’s running, it is about
time that punters ceased trying to find winners. The all-
round mediocrity of Australian thoroughbreds makes
them so susceptible to small changes in handicap condi-
tions (seemingly) that a 71h. penalty for winning “after
the declaration of the weights ” is a preposterous burden
to add to any horse’s original impost. Seven ounces
would, on paper, bring A. back to B. next time they meet.
The books harl a queer experience with the All-
Aged Stakes last Saturday. They laid 6 to 4 against a
“ placement ” of the three horses as they would finish,
and punters were about equally divided in their fancies—
Aurum, Coil, The Officer, or Aurum, The Officer, Coil.
Aurum won, and the three-year-olds ran a dead-heat for
second place, consequently they all won at the rate of ss.
on every £ invested.
When it came to the Loch Plate the books took
5 to 4 about placing the three starters one way, and even
money about placing them the other way,
being chosen, of course, as the winner in both ways. Ikey
Mo, therefore, stood to win all his wagers in the event of
Newhaven dropping dead. Otherwise, he would either
win a trifle, or come off quits if he “ fielded ” to the same
amount against both selections.
• * * *
N.S.W. Wheelmen’s League chose Federation
election-day for the opening of its big March carnival;
10,000 people rolled up, and the League shook hands
with itself and wore its hat jauntily all the afternoon.
Lesna, whose big thighs are his only arrestive
feature, made his ddbut in the Electric Handicap, and a
very bad dhmt it was. Starting from scratch he couldn’t
gain an inch on third-class men. In the paced 20-mile
race, however, he caught on tremendously with the
Sydney public. Over 20 miles he beat O’Brien, M'Coombe,
Elliott and Meadham (relieving each other) by three laps.
Lesna, so far, has only shown to advantage as the tail of
a quintet. The pacing-machine is the mind which thinks
and the body which acts ; Lesna attaches himself to it,
and remains there automatically responsive. Lesna and
his machine are one: the automaton pedals without
swaying, without apparent effort. The subjection of his
will and intelligence to the quintet or quad is doubtless
the reason why Lesna is lost in a handicap or unpaced
race. He is then a tail without a body and mind. And a
drearily hopeless tail he is.
What a contrast between the Frenchman and
Yankee Martin ! Martin is active-brained, of invincible
will. He beats faster sprinters than himself home because
he thinks and dares more than they. Martin and a com-
petitor will be racing side by side watching the ruck just
ahead—watching for the opportunity to go through. Of a
sudden Martin dashes while his competitor’s heart beats
wildly because of the smash so imminent. But Martin
has slipped through by the skin of his teeth ; there was
no opportunity, so Martin has made one, and is flying
away with another spurt while the man who was beside
him a moment before is still hopelessly behind the ruck
waiting for a chance to get through. Defeating all the
stewards and getting his two years’ disqualification re-
moved is another instance of his winning with all the
chances tremendously against him.
Look at Martin last Thursday, too. He wanted
to catch the 5.15 train to Melb. in order to compete in
the races there on Saturday. There were 20min. for him
to win or lose the International Scratch Race, to jump
into a cab, and to catch the express for Melb. He came out
and as every minute was precious he took the lead the
last three laps. Just after the bell rang he started his
spurt with Payne and Edwards hanging on his wheel. If
the spurt had been shorter both men could have beaten
him. But he took the steam out of them by his long-
sustained effort. He won 120, rushed off and caught his
train, and arriving in Melb. after his long train journey,
he beat Porta, Walne, Megson, Walker, Middleton, and
the rest in the five miles (£3O). What a hack this man
would make of Lesna in an unpaced race over any
distance 1
There is no doubt of Lesna’s capacity to follow
pace, and when he gets really fit, which should be in a
few weeks time, he will still further reduce his present
Australian records. There will be concerted endeavors
from many quarters shortly to lower existing Aus. records.
One big firm has long contemplated devoting a whole
fortnight to record-breaking.
* * * •
The only cyclist in Australia possessing a Jaw
more stony and more noticeable than Bilmartin’s is
Megson, and Megson Wits the man who took Bill down.
The heat of the Scratch Mile at Melb. cycle-show, where
this calamity occurred, was the scene of more excitement
than the Exhibition Oval has held since Zimmerman was
beaten. The race was practically between the two, and
was a fair and square go without the slightest element of
luck. Only the officials and perhaps a dozen others at the
mark were able to tell who won, so close a thing was it.
When the final came, Megson owned every barracker on the
ground, and when he won from Walne and Porta there
was a repetition of the public enthusiasm. This heat was
a close thing, too, but Megson wasn’t exhausted to the
same extent as when he met the Plugger.
Taken altogether, the racing at the last day of
the cycle-show was the best seen in Melb. this year : all
the other big meetings have been spoilt by suspicious run-
ning. The pace was generally fast, and the finishes excit-
ing, and the fact that Kuhle had a heavy fall in the after-
noon and Finnegan had his ear torn off in the evening
served to cheer up those football barrackers who are
forced to witness cycling in the off season.
Don Walker brought down the quarter and half-
mile records at St. Hilda last week, and next morning his
cut at Lesna’s mile only failed by 1 l-ssec., and that in
the face of wind and rain.
The last two Saturdays inclusive Bilmartin has
cleared *;i ' : n the ei"’U, .lavs.
* * • •
The three Delevines, now at Melb. Opera
House, were in the top rank of Eng. cyclists, and together
formed one of the triplets which paced diminutive
Michael through a heap of records.
« * • *
A. C. Edwards, the Eng. cyclist now racing in
Sydney, was married three weeks before leaving for, Aus-
tralia. Edwards wanted to bring Mrs. E. with him, but
Green, the Bilmartin of the combination, vetoed the sug-
gestion. Take ye warning by Zimmerman, O cyclists.
* * • *
George Giffen is just now regarded in Adelaide
as the world’s greatest cricket captain. When S.A. lost
her matches this season a great howl went up in Adelaide
that it was all owing to Giffen. When Evans, Bailey,
Lyons and F. Jarvis dropped catches that cost hundreds
of runs the S.A. critics unanimously put it down to
George. But it’s all right now. S.A. beat Victoria in
Adelaide by an innings and some odd runs, and Gitten
reigns once more. In fact, a nice storm came up ® n tne
Sunday, and spoilt the Vic.’s second innings, and b.A.
cricketers are very pleased to think that their captain
stands so well with Providence.
* * * *
The S. A.-Victoria cricket-matoh again served to
show up the Vic. bowling—even had Trumble been there
things would have been very little better. The four S.A.
crack batsmen made 379 out of 427, and of these “ Oar-
bine ” Lyons made 113 by beautiful cricket. Clem Hill s
95 again proves him to be the most reliable S.A. batsman
of the day. The Vic. bowling was simply helpless, and
the question arises—what will she do next year ? Albert
Trott has gone to England, and Roche and O’Halloran
follow him at the end of this month. On the other hand,
S.A. has been rewarded for persevering with Carracher,
who took 5 wickets for 24 and bowled 11 maidens out of
21 overs.
The S.A.-Victoria match did not enable any
batsman to displace Noble from top place in Australian
averages. Noble with 68.80 is followed by Lyons with
57.71, and as the latter has made 404 runs to Noble’s 344,
things are about even. Lyons’s performance is an
eloquent reply to the crowd who compared him to the
rocket which came down a stick. Carracher’s bowling
performance puts him at the head of the Australian
averages, but M'Kibbin’s 44 wickets stand out above every-
thing else, and, as the Glebe man has third batting
average —50.66—George Giffen has surely met an all-
round rival.
* * * *
Young Edgar Deering has cause to be proud of
his two creations in the sailing line—Stella 11. and Wonga.
Bad luck sticking to them during the Sydney carnival,
the knowing crowd voted them failures, but George Ellis
has shown what could be done with the Stella by again
winning last Saturday. On Saturday week Wonga forgot
to carry away something, as per usual, and scored a clever
win.
Sydney boat-sailing shows as much “reversal
of form ” as racing or trotting. Effie won three races
in succession, ran nowhere for a few more, and
then came out with Nick Johnson at the tiller and beat
the Vigilant.
* » * *
Some of the Sydney tote-shops had doubles on
the N.S.VV. Federal elections for first and last candidates,
the two favorites being Barton and Foran. No one
thought that “Prudent” Robertson would be the final
tail-joint.
* • • *
Young Tweedie is now the best-known per-
former at Sydney Amateur Gymnastic Club. Always a
tricky and clever boxer, he is developing the confidence
to make him a fighter. His recent battles with Stoneham
and M'Manus, especially' with the latter, were won very
easily. M'Manus hailed from North Shore with a big
reputation, but he couldn’t touch Tweedie, and w«nt out
in the third round.
* • • •
The Maoriland Amateur A. A. won’t fall in vmh
Vic. and N.S.W. re sending an athletic team ’Ome, aver-
ring, and rightly, that there is insufficient time between
now and the Eng. champ, meeting. A. H. Holder, winner
of four championships at Auckland, is undoubtedly the
best man in the world over 440yds. hurdles, his 58 4-sths
being a fresh world’s record. What he would have done
on a dry track can only be conjectured. The old sneer
against amateur times as against those of pro’s doesn’t
hold good here, for he was one of the best pro’s in M.L.,
and, after standing down the requisite time, was “ white-
washed ” by the association.

A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic: 1, New Books ; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery ; 4, Music ; 5, Eancy
Goods ; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures ; 9,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long. *
Nothing which is calculated to please a
customer is a trouble to Messrs. Callaghan and
Son. Hence the firm will lengthen or shorten
boots—a most difficult undertaking. They will
replace the fronts of patent boots with new leather,
in cases where people having bought their boots
somewhere else come with the unreliable material
much worn to Callaghan’s. They do repairing of
every description ; from the smallest size in
children’s shoes to men’s ll's. They introduced a
special fastening for buttons, which secures the
buttons against falling off. The coloring of white
satin shoes to match any costume is a great boon
to ladies ; and the attaining of these delicate
shades is an accomplishment which no other house
attempts. The making of special lasts has been
made a study for the comfort of anyone suffering
from deformed feet. Studding the soles of boys’
boots with steel points for school wear is another
appreciated feature in the firm. Last, though not
least, is the staining and cleaning of tan-boots—
turning out as good as new those tan-boots which
have suffered from the wet.*

I Bishop Julius, of Christchurch, M.L.
(formerly Arohdeacon of Ballarat) : —
My own belief ia that Maoriland is a sober country, that
Prohibition is dangerous as a policy, and has very little
chance of success, and that a State system of licensing
control is probably the best for the country.
I Mat Reid, who sat for Toowong in last Q.
i Parliament, once boasted that he represented two
Governors in his constituency, who neither felt
inclined to oppose nor vote for him. The joke
was a grim one, and ’tis said helped to lose Mat
his seat: they were dead Governors, and lay in
. the local cemetery.
The late porter at the Bank of England,
an imposing flunkey named Hoard, seems to have
forfeited his billet under interesting circum-
stances. Hoard drew legitimate salaries and
“ perqs. ” from two sources. During the day he
did duty at the great Bank in flaming scarlet
livery. The night, as a rule, found him in snow-
white garments officiating as carver in one or
other of the halls where the City Companies
devote their bloated revenues to gorge and guzzle.
All the bald-headed magnates, financial and com-
mercial, knowing Hoard in either, or both, of his
solemn city characters, patronised him as a
humble but irreproachable emblem of Hingland’s
greatness. Meanwhile, he ran a snug house at
fashionable Kensington in his extra capacity of
thief. He purchased largely at the Civil Service
Stores, and a detective employed there happened
to spot Hoard, Esq., in one of the few weak
moments when he was “ shaking ” goods clumsily.
Once on his track the “ d.’s ” soon discovered that
their man derived the thick-end of his income from
plunder. He operated upon the solid silver-ware
at the company banquets, and made a hobby of
collecting portable property wherever he might be
left alone with it. Also, he picked the pockets of
Fat Man previous to casting suspicion upon the
waiters. A more versatile comedian never carved
a turkey. The exposure of Hoard has shaken
public confidence. The new porter at the Bank of
England complains that the small boys who hang
around that awful building make his days un-
happy by referring to him indirectly as a burglar
in disguise. The boys have never shown a proper
respect for “ Robin Redbreast (as bank porters
are styled), but it is only of late that they have
started to ask him where he keeps his skeleton-
keys.
According to the Sydney police, quite a
number of small boys are in the habit of sleeping
in the sewers, and are known as sewer rats.
was sent to the Sobraon training-ship the other
day.
The irony of it. A Sydney dead-beat
attempted to sell a pint “pewter ’ without notic-
ing or being able to read upon it the legend,
‘ Stolen from the Colonnade Hotel. ” Four months.
Mudgee Haynes, M.P,, says he approves
of having an elective Governor-General because
he intends standing for the position himself. 1 his
paper reckons that the struggle between John
Haynes and John Norton for that Governorship
will be something terrible.
Pathetic advt. from S.M. Hekald :—•
IJABY’S first OUTFIT, new; owner, disappointed,
-U will sacrifice, cheap. Baby, Post-office Burwood.
The pathos relates to the “ owner,” who was dis-
appointed. As for the baby, it seems to have
struck a patch. This isn’t much of a country for
any baby to come to.
German Veterinary-professor Nocard finds
from examination of public poultry markets that
at least 23 per cent, of all birds sold are tubercu-
lous, and, therefore, a public menace. Also, that,
besides the danger from tuberculous meat and
milk, there is a big likelihood of a consumptive
farmer himself spreading tuberculosis among
healthy stock, which were certificated clean.


H:rr Bassmussen,
Till (ELKUKATED
DANISH HERBALIST.
DISCOVERER AND PROPRIETOR
OF THE MOST
SUCCESSFUL
Herbal Remedies
—FOR THE —
BLOOD & NERVES.
A.N INTERESTING NOTICE OF A MOST
SUCCESSFUL CAREER.
MARVELLOUS TESTIMONIALS.
FLATTERING PRESS OPINIONS.
- - - MIRACULOUS CURES.
A few words as to the progress and growth in
public favour of the Registered Alfaline Herbal
Remedies would not be out of place here, and
would be additional evidence as to their real
genuineness and great virtues as claimed for them
by Herr Rassmussen, their discoverer and sole
proprietor.
The great virtues of the Alfaline Herbal Reme-
dies are so well known that it is quite unnecessary
to say much in regard to them in this notice; the
very fact of their having been before the public
for so many years, and continually growing in
popularity, is quite sufficient evidence that they
are all they are claimed to be. Taking into con-
sideration the fact that they have been awarded
the Parisian Gold Medal, and that so many thou-
sands of original and unsolicited testimonials have
been received, and may be seen at the Head Office,
some of which appear in his pamphlets, there
should be no difficulty in predicting a great future
for these health-giving Remedies unprecedented
in the History of Herbal Remedies.
About twelve years ago Herr Rassmussen intro-
duced these Remedies to the Australian public by
opening a Depot for their sale at 547 George
Street, Sydney, N.S.W., his present Head Office,
and although at that time he did not advertise in
any journal, the wonderful virtues of these Reme-
dies were known far and wide within a few
months. Orders for them came from every
quarter, not only from all the Australian colonies,
but from all over the world. In 1891 the clamour
for the Alfaline Herbal Remedies had become so
great that it became impossible to supply everyone
from the Sydney Depot. Letters arrived by thou-
sands, many orders came by telegraph, and some
people even travelled over a thousand miles to
make sure of obtaining some of these God-inspired
Remedies. What was to be done ? It was Herr
Rassmussen’s duty to see that every sufferer from
the Blood, Skin, Nerves, Liver, and other affec-
tions, whose very life and happiness depended on
these Remedies, should be able to obtain same
without undue delay. Herr Rassmussen had thus
no other alternative than to open Branch Depots
for the sale of the Alfaline Remedies in every
colony, and, if possible, in every country in the
world, as "he desired to keep the sale of his Herbal
Remedies in his own hands entirely, and thus
avoid the agency of chemists, who generally have
a substitute which they prefer to sell instead of
the real article. A start was made at once in that
direction, and a very large and palatial Depot
was opened at 150 Bourke Street, Melbourne, in
1891. Immediately after, a fine Branch was
established at 154 Rundle Street, Adelaide, fol-
lowed by the opening of an extensive Branch at
91 Lambton Quay, Wellington, New Zealand. A
large Branch was then established at the corner of
Hay and William Streets, Perth, W.A., and
shortly after another Branch, next to Coolgardie
Hotel, Bailey Street, Coolgardie, and N 0.2 Lin-
dell’s Chambers, Hannan street, Kalgoorlie, W.A.,
not to forget the very extensive Branch opened
some seven years ago at 160 Queen Street, Bris-
bane, which is now the Head Depot for Queens-
land, and finally a Branch just opened at Charters
Towers, North Queensland.
It is not unreasonable to ask, “Is it possible
such success should follow unless these well-known
Herbal Remedies are all they are claimed to be,
and is it likely that their popularity should keep
growing to such an extent, unless they contained
exceptional virtues out of the common ? ” The
evidence that Herr Rassmussen’s Herbal Remedies
contain wonderful virtues is simply overwhelming,
as he possesses thousands of Testimonials to that
effect.
The whole of these Branches are each doing a
large and flourishing business. There is not a
country on the face of the globe where these Re-
medies have not been heard of, and orders arrive
daily from the uttermost parts of the earth.

Herald Office.
Roberts’ Hotel, at the corner of George
and Market streets, Sydney, is one of the very
best hotels in the city. The tariff is moderate,
the accommodation most comfortable, and every
guest receives the greatest possible attention.
The average man, when he takes his wife to tour
for a day or a month—or when he merely takes
himself—wants a nice, quiet place to stay at,
handy to the centres of business and pleasure,
luxuriously conducted, yet without too much irri-
tating fuss and frill, and without an enormous bill
at the end of his visit. And in Roberts’ Hotel
the average man gets just that.*
Salisbury’s “triumph of diplomacy” in
connection with the Cretan jumble is merely a
repetition of several other alleged successes. Crete
was given self-government in ’6B, and nine years
later its chaotic condition resulted in full parlia-
mentary powers being granted. The plucky
islanders have been fighting Turkey for upwards
of two centuries, and nothing short of absolute
separation will satisfy them. Salisbury’s victory
is a triumph of timidity, which merely postpones
the inevitable European burst-up for another year
or two, until Bull or one of the other “Powers”
feels sure-footed enough to fly into an incompre-
hensible temper about nothing in particular.
SIR JOHN Power’s whiskey is now dis-
tributed all over Australia and New Zealand, and
can therefore be pronounced the spirit of Federa-
tion. It has great advantages over the other
spirits of Federation, because first acquaintance
with it is convincing of its permanent good, and
because it belongs to the practical—not the theo-
retical. The London Wine and Spirit Gazette
says of this whiskey : “It stands highest in the
London whiskey market, and can challenge the
world as the finest spirit distilled.” This whiskey
is made from the best quality barley, and exclu-
sively in the old pot-still. There is no Indian
corn in its manufacture—no rubbish, no patent
still. Its history begins with 1791, and the chief
warehouse has a storage capacity for 36,000 hogs-
heads. You had better try SIR JOHN Power’s
whiskey.*
Several dozen sound old Tories met and
liquored old Duncan Gillies on his return to Mel-
bourne, and slobbered painfully over that ancient
failure. The climax was reached, however, when
G. D. Carter, the evil genius of Patterson’s Minis-
try, hoped that Duncan had returned to give Vic-
toria the benefit of his “ wise head, cool judgment,
andcommonsense.” Duncan wasthernaddestpoliti-
cal boomer, the worst spendthrift, the most reckless
borrower and chucker-away of money on wild-cat
railways, and the most affably thick-headed man
who ever let things slide in Victoria ; and yet he
dropped a tear quite seriously in the brimming
cup over Carter’s eulogy, and almost wept at the
contemplation of his own vast sagacity. And
the meeting agreed, with deep emotion all round,
that Victoria was saved now that slow, fat,
dunderheaded Duncan had come back on his two
short legs to buzz round the scene of his own and
his country’s downfall.
A Westralian number of the Clarion,
edited by Randolph Bedford, is the most imposing
work of boom fiction ever produced on this
side of the Equator. Gold glitters on the front
cover, tempting one to explore the Clarion’s
interior. The contents comprise about 70 pages
of descriptive matter f intersected by some 500
photo’s., all tending to the glory of mines and
mine-promoters in the Land of Bedfordian Pro-
mise. To read is to believe, or nearly so. Any-
way, Truth bows her diminished head before the
editor’s version of the Golden West as it is, and
scepticism gets blown away by the hot wind of his
prophetic preface. “Westralia will be dotted
with cities living on reefing when generations
hence have become impalpable ashes.” Amen !
Meanwhile, here’s to the health of our versatile
friend, Randolph the Reefer ! May he lie long
ere he lies low, promoting the happiness of him-
self and co-directors until his share of Nature’s
debt be fully paid up, and may his last successful
“ float ” be upon the gracious bosom of the Jasper
Sea!

Dead Baron von Mueller, some years back,
having read of the bare alkali deserts of south-
western U.S. A.,suggested in his generous altruistic
fashion that Australian salt-bush should be planted
in selected spots and given a fair trial, sending
along a few pounds of different salt-bush seeds for
the purpose. Now the “ bad lands ” sown widely
with salt-bush are producing 20 tons of green and
5 tons of dry food per acre, though formerly as bare
as a billiard-ball. Also, as much as 5 dols.
per lb. has been given for salt-bush seed in Cali-
fornia. Sheep and hogs eat it with avidity, while
cattle and horses gradually acquire a huge liking
for it, after mixing in judiciously incre ising doses
with ordinary food. In its latest report, the Uni-
versity of California especially think < the dead
Baron for his disinterestedness. Jnd still Aus-
tralian pastoralists won’t learn the lesson, perhaps
because distant hills are greenest.

The decision of Q. Supreme Court in re-
spect of the succession duty to be paid in Queensland
bv the estate of the late Silas Harding is mainly respon-
sible for the question of succession duty brought before
the Federal Council by Att.-Gen. Byrnes. Silas Harding
left a Q. estate valued at £I3O,(XX), with Q. debts of
£14,000. His executors contended that there was no
succession duty to pay in Q., as the testator had been
“domiciled” in Victoria. Not very long- ago N.S.W.
Supreme Court decided a probate case where a partner,
domiciled in N.S.W., had willed certain Q. property. The
N.S.W. Court ruled that the Q. estate must pay N.S.W.
probate duty to the amount of t 2440. The executors
paid it (in addition to probate duties on N.S.W. pro-
perty), but appealed. The Full Court dismissed the
appeal. Now, according to the Silas Harding ruling, that
Q. estate, which has paid N.S.W. probate duties of £2440,
will have to pay Q. “succession duties.” If the “suc-
cessor ” is a “ stranger in blood ” to the predecessor,
those “ succession duties ” will amount to about £ll,OOO.
Several similar cases are under the attention of Q. Stamp
Department.
* * * *
A tale comes from the Old World of a scheme—
said to have been formulated by the Russian Government
—for maintaining wheat at a steady price. The idea is
that the Governments of Russia, Austria, the U.S. and
Argentina—the four great wheat-producing countries of
the world—should take the matter in hand, and induce
other grain-growing nations to co-operate. In the first
place, a minimum price is to be fixed for all the markets
of the world. State granaries are to be constructed on a
gigantic scale for the storage of grain—sufficient to hold
the best part of the year’s crop. When there is a surplus
the State will take all the grain offering, and pay the
farmer the fixed minimum price in paper currency—paper
with a wheat basis. Whenever the market will absorb
the store at a price high enough to repay the cost and
leave the needful margin to cover waste and cost of
storage it will be sold again, and the proceeds applied to
the redemption of the paper currency. The advantages
are : (1) The State can afford to keep its stores on hand,
as the money invested in grain will cost it nothing for
interest; (2) "therefore it can take all the grain that offers
at the minimum price, and wait for the lean years which
always come before long; (3) by buying in years of great
plenty, it will prevent prices going ruinously low and the
farmer being financially wrecked for want of a market;
(4) by letting the accumulated surplus loose in years of
scarcity it will prevent prices going ruinously high, to the
disaster of the consumer. It is a huge idea that only a
league of States could attempt, but there seems no
reason why it should not work—when the world gets
sufficiently civilised. Bi-metallists allege that 16oz. silver
can be made worth loz. of gold always and everywhere.
If so, with equally good management ifi bushels of wheat
could be made equally reliable in price. Wheat is no
more unfixable than silver.
* * * *
The early -diggings storekeeper and the peddling
land-monger are mixing with the Australian rush to
British New Guinea, with predaceous eyes fixed on
chances for town allotments in Port Moresby and Sumaria.
It will be the old story of every new rush repeated. Hun-
dreds will die like rotten sheep, but still delirious thou-
sands more will press on.
• * * »
Surest sign of the New Guinea boom. The
Queensland Nevermind Govt, has “ under consideration ”
—never mind, there’s no hurry—the question of the desir-
ability of establishing telegraphic communication between
Queensland and New Guinea.
That “ 900 oz. of gold obtained on the Mambare,
New Guinea, by two prospectors” has dwindled down
woefully in significance. It appears now that nine men,
working over a month, made the 9000 z., and since that
find nothing at all has been got, although 25 men have
been working there for three months in terrible distress.
Officially the sum total of gold obtained recently on the
mainland is 1400 oz.
* * * *
Four sturdy N.Q. miners recently abandoned
Westralia and went over to N. Guinea. Within the last
few weeks the whole four returned to N.Q. to die, the
last one expiring of malarial fever in Cairns Hospi-
tal. The Merrie England returnedl the other day
from Port Moresby with three miners, two of whom were
prostrate with fever.
* * • *
Dear Bulletin, —I am an auditor. Let me give
you a recent experience. For 1896 I was auditor to a
limited liability co. doing business up North, and working
under table A of the Q. Act. It became my duty to
require certain alterations in the form of balance-sheet
submiti.ed, so that when certified by me it might give the
information demanded by the Companies Act. I also
required a provision to be made for possible bad and
doubtful debts due to the co. The directors did, in con-
sequence, make a moderate provision, but in their report
issued to the shareholders mentioned that “ this item was
insisted upon by the auditor for reasons best known to
himself. The directors held nearly half the shares of the
co. I was proposed for re-appointment as auditor—but
was not elected.—A.

4O P er cent, of the “ tied ” pubs, in
Melbourne are looked after by “ whippers-in.” For the
benefit of the uninitiated, it is explained that “ whippers-
in are the seedy gentlemen sent round by the breweries
to sit underneath the cash-slits in the pub.’s counters,
and catch the coins dropped through by the bejewelled
lady or gent, who poses as landlady or “lord” in exchange
for the “ run of their teeth.”

“Have you struck anything fresh?” wired an
exasperated Westralian consulting engineer to the boss of
the “ cat.” He replied, “ No —nothing fresh, but plenty
of salt water.”
* * • •
The process of cyanidation patented by Deeble,
of pyrites works, is receiving bold advt. Said to laugh at
any base matter as long as the crushing or rather grinding
is fine enough. Years ago Deeble confided to the writer
that he was on the track of something in which cyanide
would fill an important part, and that he was quite clear
of the C3'anide patents.
* * • *
To test whether telegraph-lines “leak” an
Adelaide broker, the other day, arranged with some West
Australian friends to send him half-a-dozen telegrams in-
structing the sale of certain unquoted stock. Next day
15,000 shares changed hands.
* * • •
“ Peesness is not too goot ” at present with the
60 percenters (and more when they can get it). In
a recent Court list of cases for a single day the
name of one money-lender appears in no less than
13 ca. sa. applications and one application to show cause.
Still legal money-lending pans out well. First, there are
the costs of the necessary security—seldom less than £2
2s ; then the 60 (and over) per cent., and then, in a good
many cases, the cost of proceedings to recover. Of course,
there are a few bad marks, but there is plenty of margin,
even in these times.

more mining directorates than any other person in that
city, and in the Register on Tuesday, Feb. 23, a corre-
spondent sums up the list thus :
Sir,—Mr. Tucker will no doubt correct me if I am wrong
when I state for information . . . that the following
Mining Boards are, or were until quite recently, graced
by his presence and enriched by his advice : West Calling-
ton, Eden Park, Queen Margaret No. 1 South, General
Gordon Extended, Hannan’s Victoria Extended, Ilannan’s
Consols, Boulder Central Extended, Hannan’s Junction,
Hands Across Sea South, Royal Mint South, Ready Money,

• * * *
There is one Australian time-payment jewellery
firm which has no less than six of its canvassers in gaol.
The same people can afford to employ an official at a good
salary as a sort of private detective and enquiry man,
who is on good terms with the various members of the
“ foorce,” pawnbrokers, and second-hand dealers gener-
ally. This person’s duties are to find out if any time-pay-
ment jewellery has been “ popped” or otherwise disposed
of, to rouse up and threaten bad pays, and conduct
police-court prosecutions. And as the great bulk of the
parties who get time-payment trinkets have some remote
idea of not paying the full amount, or of swindling and
otherwise dodging the collector, the private detective’s
hands are always full.
♦ * • •
The boss of a small co-operative party working
a reef and a small mill in an out-of-the-way locality near
Bright, Gippsland, was induced to get up at about 1 a.m.
recently by a fear that he had not locked the battery
doors. The mill was working one shift only, there was
very little amalgam on the plates, and nobody lived
within five miles, but the visit of inspection was made,
and the miner found the Chow gardener from down the
creek cheerfully scraping the top plate with a hoe-head.
“ John ” was “ laid out,” the rest of the party were called
up, and, after making him “ smell pertickler agony,”
Cow Bung was led home by the pig-tail. Arrived there,
the party searched the pagan’s hut, and found at least
20oz. amalgam, and as much retorted gold. This they
pocketed, swearing it must have been stolen from their
mill. “ Anyhow,” explains the pious Wesleyan boss, “ I
had to punish him for his sin.” /
The Bursting of the Boom.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
The shipping-office clerks are “ short,” the
manager is gruff—
“ They cannot make reductions,” and “ the fares
are low enough.”
They ship us West with cattle, and we go like
cattle too ;
And fight like dogs three times a day for what we
get to chew. . . .
We’ll have the pick of empty bunks and lots of
stretching room, .
And go for next to nothing at the Bursting of the
Boom.
So wait till the Boom bursts ! —we’ll all get a
show.:
Then when the Boom bursts is our time to go.
We’ll meet ’em coming back in shoals, with looks
of deepest gloom,
But we’re the sort that battle through at the
Bursting of the Boom.
The captain’s easy-going when Fremantle comes
in sight;
He can’t say when you’ll get ashore — perhaps
to-morrow night; ”
Your coins are few ; the charges high ; you must
not linger here—
You’ll get your boxes from the hold “when she’s
’longside the pier.” . . .
The launch will foul the gangway, and the
trembling bulwarks loom
Above a fleet of harbor craft—at the Bursting of
the Boom.
So wait till the Boom bursts ! —we’ll all get a
show;
He’ll “ take you for a bob, sir,” and where
you want to go.
He’ll “take the big portmanteau, sir, if he might
so presume ”
You needn’t hump your luggage at the Bursting
of the Boom.
It’s loafers—Customs loafers —and you pay and
pay again;
They hinder you and cheat you from the gangway
to the train ;
The pubs, and restaurants are full—they haven’t
room for more;
They charge us each three shillings for a shake-
down on the floor;
But, “Show this gentleman upstairs—the first
front parlor room.
“ We'll see about your luggage, sir ”—at the
Bursting of the Boom.
So wait till the Boom bursts ! —we’ll all get a
show;
And wait* till the Boom bursts, and swear
mighty low.
“We mostly charge a pound a week. How do
you like the room ? ”
And, “Show this gentleman the bath” —at the
Bursting of the Boom.
I go down to the timber-yard (I cannot face the
rent)
To get some strips of Oregon to frame my hessian
tent ;
To buy some scraps of lumber for a table or a
shelf :
The boss comes up and says I might just look
round for myself ;
The foreman grunts and turns away as silent as
the tomb—
The boss himself will wait on me at the Bursting
of the Boom.
So wait till the Boom bursts —we’ll all get a
load. •
“ You had better take those scraps, sir, they’re
only in the road.”
“ Now, where the hell’s the carter? ” you’ll hear
the foreman fume ;
And, “ Take that timber round at once ! ” at the
Bursting of the Boom.
Each one-a-penny grocer, in his box of board and
tin,
Will think it condescending to consent to take
you in ;
And not content with twice as much as what is
just and right,
They charge and cheat you doubly, for the Boom
is at its height.
It’s “ Take it now or leave it now “ your money
or your room ; ”
But “Who’s attending Mr. Brown?” at the
Bursting of the Boom.
So wait till the Boom bursts !—and take what
you can get,
“ There’s not the slightest hurry, and your bill
ain’t ready yet.”
They’ll call and get your orders until the crack o’
doom,
And send them round directly, at the Bursting of
the Boom.
No Country and no Brotherhood—such things
are dead and cold ;
A camp from all the lands or none, all mad for
love of gold ;
Where othersider Number One makes slave of
Number Two,
And the vilest women ,of the world the vilest
ways pursue;
And men go out and slave and bake and die in
agony
In western hells that God forgot, where never
man should be.
I feel a prophet in my heart that speaks the one
word “ Doom ! ”
And aye you’ll hear the Devil laugh at the
Bursting of the Boom.
HENRT LAWSON.
The Buxletin lately pointed out how
grovellers of our day attribute the progress made
from 1837 onwards to the lady who has occupied
apartments at Windsor Castle during that period.
Turn to Hawksworth’s introduction to Captain
Cook’s first voyage, and you will find this patch of
grease about fat-headed King George 111. :
“ Under your Majesty’s auspices, in little more
than seven years, discoveries have been made far
greater than those of all the navigators in the
world collectively, from the expedition of Colum-
bus to the present day.” Isn’t that just how the
modern grovellers are talking about little
Queen Victoria? “ Auspices ”is a most compre-
hensive and useful word.

After Doctors Fail and Hospitals are Unsuccessful,
Dr. Williams’ PINK PILLS for PALE PEOPLE
CURE I
ANOTHER MIRACLE IN SYDNEY.
A CRIMEAN VETERAN RECOUNTS HIS RECOVERY FROM
A LIVING DEATH.
Mr. Charles Terry, a Crimean veteran, of 485 Kent-
street, Sydney, having been lecturing lately in the Domain
on the “ Temperance Question,” we (says the German Post)
despatched a reporter to his residence, who gives the follow-
ing verbatim account of his illness and marvellous recovery.
Our representative found Mr. Terry a chatty old gentleman
of 66, who seemed decidedly pleased to be interviewed on the
subject of his wonderful escape from death. In a response
to a few questions, Mr. Terry stated “ I joined the Royal
Isavy about 1853, and served during the Crimea in H.M.S.
Retribution, Captain Thomas Fisher, under Admiral Dun-
das, bearing the flag of Rear-Admiral Baines, and I bear the
Crimean decoration. I arrived in Australia at the latter
end of 1877 in H.M.S. Sapphire, the Hon. Captain Merry.
I then joined the Diamond, under Captain Dale, on this
station, and on leaving the ship quitted the service for good,
having in all served in 17 ships of her Majesty’s Royal Navy,
during which time I was employed as Admiral’s, Com-
modore’s, and Captain’s cook. In August of this
year (1896) I found myself at Emu Plains, being in the
employ of Messrs. M'Grath, fellmongers, employed as cook.
It was whilst there that I was taken ill, towards the latter
end of August.. I attributed my illness to exposure in
periodically leaving a hot kitchen to attend to some outside
work. My first symptoms were coughing, a spitting of blood,
and a bloody discharge from the kidneys of the consistency
of jelly. One man there was particularly kind to me, and
constantly stayed up with me all night, giving me eucalyptus
to inhale, and continuously rubbing it into my chest, throat,
and stomach. This man kept constantly telling me to clear
out from Emu Plains and go to hospital, otherwise, to use
his own words, he said, ‘ You will peg out.’ Feeling that he
was likely to prove''a true prophet, I followed his advice,
came to Sydney, and took up my abode at 485 Kent-street,
where I still am living. I should, however, mention that
both Mr. and Mrs. McGrath gave me the highest of
references, and regretted that my bad health would not
allow me to remain. (These recommendations, which
were of a most satisfactory nature, he showed our re-
presentative.) On my arrival in Sydney I suffered
from great pains in the back and sides, and
also under the ribs. I could take no solid food, and entirely
subsisted on eggs and new milk, which my landlady procured
for me. I went to see the Government medical officer at
Circular Quay, and was so horribly weak that it took me an
hour and ten minutes to get there. He told me to strip,
which I did, and he then sounded me and asked me how long
I had been bad. I told him that from the first it was about
eight weeks, upon which he said, ‘ Get away, man ; it’s
chronic. He could not send me to Little Bay, but suggested
that I should go to Liverpool Asylum at once, but I refused
to go there, and returned home. I then went to see the
Grand Chief Templar. I also saw his wife and the Grand
Lodge Secretary, and told them of my pitiable state. With
the assistance of Brother J. C. Jones, late District Deputy
of the Good Templars., and the Grand Lodge Secretary, I
had medicine prescribed for me of various kinds, which
proved quite ineffectual, and afforded me no relief whatever.
I took no less than seven bottles of eucalyptus, and
even chlorodyne was prescribed for me. I fell away
lamentably, perspired profusely, and could not obtain
any sleep. I was in such pain that I could not
shift in bed. My right side was much the worst, and my
breathing was most labored and painful. T cannot be. too
