Culpepper’s Brother-In-Law.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The fight had been hot amongst the tobacco on
the hill-side, but the Dutchmen had been heavib
out-numbered, and, for every long-haired Sat
chanese that lay bulleted or bayoneted, half-a
dozen white jackets were stretched motionless anr
bloody.
So near to the man in hiding had one flaxen-
moustached young Hollander fallen that Cul-
pepper, as he crouched, could have touched the
horribly unseeing eyes that seemed to stare at him
between the tobacco-stems. Once he did stretch
out his hand, but the instant buzz of the blood-
flies made him snatch it hastily back—striking
wildly at such of the sickening things as came his
way.
“ Looks almost as if the poor devil was askin
me what business I have here. And, as for the
livin’ Dutchmen, if they should happen along to
bury dead or such like, it’s very likely they’d just
put a bayonet through me without askin’ me that
or any other question. So dear’s the word—now
it’s getting a bit duskish.”
Culpepper called himself a medical botanist,
but the Dutch authorities —suspecting that the
mineral kingdom was more the object of his atten-
tion than the vegetable—had refused him permis-
sion to leave Samarang, except by sea. By sea
he had left —but it was alone and by night—and
now, on the frontier of the unsubdued Satchan
country, he was disgusted to find himself, one
evening, in the very centre of a fight between the
fierce aboriginals thereof and the Dutchmen he
had come so far to avoid. From the sheltering
tobacco he had looked on at as much of a desperate
half-hour’s engagement as the broad leaves would
allow him to see—coolly ready all the time to pot
the first man—white or yellow—who should happen
across him in his concealment.
“ A crack, more or less, wouldn’t have been
noticed, and I may have to blow a hole through
somebody yet before I get to the boat. And,
Jericho ! —here he comes ! —or is it a she ?”
« * » *
The sun was down, and the goblin-like halungs
were flapping past towards the mango-groves
around the village, but there was light enough to
make, out that it was a Satchanese girl who came
creeping into the tobacco within a couple of rows
of him, and Culpepper remained still as stone
until, with scarcely a rustle, she had glided ghost-
like away in the direction he had come.
“ Now, what in thunder’s she after?” he mut-
tered, and then speedily answered his own ques-
tion according to his lights. “ Plunder ! The hilts
of some of them krisses has emeralds in ’em, I’ll
bet—and diamonds ! And that ought to’ve been
■your contract, Silas Culpepper.”
He turned, and crept back to where the fire-flies
were already casting here and there a swift gleam
upon the white jackets of the fallen Hollanders.
His ear told him where the girl was—talking
gently, as it seemed, to someone. But the
someone as Culpepper saw when, silently as a
snake, he had stolen near enough—was a
corpse; and he almost sighed as he told him-
self that the “ wiinming, poor critters!”
were the same under every skin. Which con-
cession to sentiment, however, was followed
immediately by a very practical disgust with such
a check upon his business operations.
“ I’ll see what this chap’s got, in the meantime,
anyhow ! ” and, stretching a cautious hand to the
body nearest him, Culpepper began to work th
kris out of what ought to have been the stiffenec
grasp. But it wasn’t —and hardly had th
warmth of the hand telegraphed to Culpepper’
brain that the man was still alive, before the
Javanese, with a long-drawn groan, turned ove
from his back to his side. The girl sprang
instantly up, came over, and peered so intent!;
down at the wounded man’s face that she saw no
more of the crouching figure within a yard of her
than she did of the other form that now made
suddenly a dim appearance behind her. Then—
but let Culpepper here take up his own story.
* • • *
“ When she turned and caught sight of the chap
that had just made one of the company, she didn’
screech—like a white woman would—but she gave
a jump like a kangaroo, an’ would have beer
mighty scarce in them parts if the fellow hadn’
been as quick as herself. But he has her by the
wrists like winkin’, and sticks to her like wax til
she stops squirmin’—which she does all of
sudden, and says somethin’ to him that don’i
appear to stroke him overmuch the right way, for
he lets go one of her wrists and whips out his kris
And then—l couldn’t see very well—but, after
she’d rubbed in something more that made him
twist her to the ground, I thought I saw him
drawin’ back his sticker to settle her, and I makes
a jump and lands him on the noddle with my
revolver-butt.
“Well, the girj, of course, got an almighty
scare, but she just dropped to things like the rea]
brainy critter she has since panned out, and she
flies round and gathers me half-a-dozen bandaras
from the stiff-’uns, and helps me to fix my man up
as tight as cement—not forgettin’ to get one of
the sashes over his ugly mouth. At least”—
Culpepper began reflectively to fill his pipe—“ I
suppose it was ugly, for I never saw the gentle-
man in daylight till—this morning ! ”
I looked round for my fishing-line. “This
morning ? You’ve seen nobody this morning but
myself and old Gojur, there ! ”
Culpepper sniggered as he struck a light.
Didn’t you notice—cuss the matches !—didn’t
you see me squintin’ mighty hard down into the
water when we were abreast of that rock with the
two holes through it ? No ? Well, as we come
back, I’ll show you what I was lookin’ at. Be-
cause it was behind that patch of mangroves at
the bend of the creek that my boat was hidden,
the night I’m yarnin’ about. And I may as well
tell you, before I go any furder, that the fellow
the girl was pipin’ her eye over was her sweet-
heart, with his head caved-in by a musket-butt.”
“ And the other? ”
“ Her brother ! Dead agen the match, he was ;
and when he came sneakin’ out in the dark after
her, she taunted him with keepin’ out of the way
while the fightin’ was on hand. And, of course,
though I couldn’t understand their lingo, I wasn’t
goin’ to let him stick the poor girl like a pig ! ”
{( “Come, now, my ingenious friend!’’ I said,
it hasn t been half a bad yarn, but you’ve
knocked a big hole in it now ! If you didn t
understand a word of what they said—how do you
know all this?”
“How? Why, Mrs. Culpepper told me after-
wards ! Kinder breaks you up, that, I reckon !
so don’t you ask any more fool’s questions till I’m
through, which won’t be long ; for the fact is the
girl made me understand—talk or no talk—that
’twould be a heap safer for her to skedaddle with
me, and safer for both of us if we toted the pris’ner
along, likewise. Which we did. Between us we
lugged him down that hill in front of you, anc
dumped him into the boat not a hundred yard
from where we’re sittin’ in this one. This Satchan
country’s quiet enough now, but I never though
to set eyes on the darned place again. Now, put
her about, and we’ll run back to the rock with the
holes in it.”
* « * *
A light began to come to me. Hadn’t I hearc
from somebody that Mrs. “ Dr. ” Culpepper was a
Java-woman? “ May I ask, Culpepper,” I said,
as the boat’s head came round, “if the lady ol
the skull-cracked knight found a consolatory sub-
stitute in ”
“Inme? That’s so, mister —she’s the present
Mrs. Culpepper. Sort of wife that don’t grow on
every bush neither —I tell you, sirree ! ”
“No doubt. But I shouldn’t have thought that
brother of hers would have made a first-class
brand of relation ! ”
“Him?” The old villain sucked in his lean
cheeks, as his way was when immensely tickled.
“ Well, now, mister, you see, he didn’t give the
thing a square trial. How in natur’ he worked
himself loose, I dunno; but some of these
Javanese chaps could give pints to the Daven-
ports ; and, at all events, just when we were pretty
well where we are now, up he jumps and lays me
out with a lump of the stone-ballast. It was ”
Culpepper reminiscently fingered his shaggy occi-
put—-“it was a knock that stiffened me for ten
minutes, but when I came-to my yaller friend
was a heap stiffer. She had brought half-a-dozen
of the krisses with her, you see, and she let him
have one of ’em for all she was worth ! Twenty
holes there was in that chap’s carcase, if there was
one ! ”
“ Made by his sister ? ” I exclaimed—feeling
that, upon the whole, the commonplace white article
had its advantages.
“ Bet your boots !” Culpepper steered close to
the rock with the holes in it. “ And, as ’twouldn’t
have done to let him go floatin’ around, tollin’
tales, I just wound a couple of fathoms of chain
round him and dropped him overboard—here ! ”
He lowered away the sail and, as the boat lost
her way, peered over the side for half-a-minute
before he beckoned to me. “ There you are !” he
said —“ nearly plumb beneath us.”
* » » *
I looked steadily down through what —being
apparently a three-fathom depth—was probably,
therefore, about five. A couple of yards to one
side of us, the shadow of the boat lay like a silver-
margined cloud upon the bottom, but, immediately
below —in knobs, and branches, and bowl-like
whorls—the coral gleamed white, and red, and
green ; with sponges, here and there, of blue, and
brown, and yellow ; while in and out amongst the
gorgeous, flower-like stuff played brilliant, tiny
fishes—stumpy, emerald-backed fellows with wide
rainbow-banded fins, and slender, shining, cylin-
drical creatures that looked like gold pencil-cases
endowed with lightning swiftness. And presently
—distinct against a dark red patch of moss-coral—
I made out the remnant of a human skeleton. The
skull was there, with the spine and ribs and pel-
vis, plainly recognisable through that perfectly
pellucid medium, and endowed by the shimmer of
the sun-pierced water with a fantastic semblance
of motion.
“You see him, do you? ”
“ I see it! ”
“ Never mind the word ! ” Culpepper cackled
sardonically as he ran the sail up again. “That’s
my brother-in-law, anyhow ! ”
ALEX. MONTGOMERY
The Ghoul o’ Guinea Gold.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There’s a voice that calls the bearded men and
brown,
Calls the laughing lads whose lips are tipped with
down,
As the siren-song of old lured the Greek to death! v
fold
So it lures to-day the brawny and the bold.
Where the gorge is dark that cleaves the Guinea
hills,
Where the swelt’ring swamp her fever-dew distils,
Scooping grave-pits in the mould
Soft he sings “ 0 Hearts a-cold / ”
The merry Ohoul that guards the Guinea Gold.
For he loves to see the merry pick a-swing,
Loves to hear the cradle reck, the axes ring,
But he better loves to spread nightly board and
nightly bed , . •
Where the delvers droop—the dying o’er the dead.
And he draws them with the magic of his song,
Our bravest and our best in eager throng,
Maid and wife may mourn and weep, but their
tryst our lads will keep
With the voice that calls them northward o’er
the deep !
Where the gorge is dark that cleaves the Guinea
hills,
Where the swelt’ring swamp her fever-dew distils,
Scooping grave-pits in the mould •
Soft he sings “ O Hearts a-coldl ”
The merry Ghoul that guards the Guinea Gold.
N.Q.
P. LUFTIG
A native dog has been killed down Bom-
bala, N.S.W., way, the skin of which, when
pegged out, measured 6ft. 6in. from nose-tip to
tail-tip.
“ Politician Gillies said nothing, excepting
a fair number of words, at the St. George’s Club
dinner,” writes The Bulletin’s London corre-
spondent, “although much was expected of him
by those who knew not the individual. You will
see sometimes at the Zoo a stumpy iguana in a
state of loosely-motionless torpidity. Gin-trap
jaws suggest viciousness if interfered with, and,
doubtless, many an audacious fly is ‘ taken in ’ by
the beast’s apparent stupor. The slop-made, six-
sizes-too-large skin hangs in dry, dull crinkles and
folds around the barely-breathing body. The bald
eyelids droop o’er the expressionless eyes, and a
cold, gritty atmosphere envelopes the abbreviated
length of negative passivity. You turn away to
moisten your orbs with something fresh and green
and lifelike. The reptile disappears, leaving an
impression of waviness on the sands of its glass-
house and one of its lazy worthlessness in the
mind of the observer. Cold-blooded, heartless,
useless, it departs, having done nothing and not
even done that well. Ugh ! Let Victoria send a
human politician with human feelings, life, and
energy.”
